10 min
Modalities as Relational Matters
Author argues that modalities are relational, not real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ontological status of wujub, imtina', and imkan as i'tibari matters and distinguish them from real existents.

Core Idea
Necessity, impossibility, and possibility are relational, not real.

In Islamic logic, the three primary modalities are often debated: are they real entities, privative matters, or something else? The author settles the matter by asserting that they are relational (i'tibari), having no independent existence in external reality (a'yan). This lesson unpacks the key claim and its implications for modal reasoning in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modalities
Locate the terms wujub (necessity), imtina' (impossibility), and imkan (possibility) in the Arabic. What does each signify in the context of propositions?
They are the three modal qualifiers of a proposition's truth.
2
Understand the i'tibari concept
The author states that these modalities are 'i'tibari' — relational matters. This means they exist only in the mind as mental constructs, not as separate entities in the external world.
Contrast with 'haqiqi' (real) entities.
3
Analyze the author's argument
The author argues that a necessary concomitant (lazim) need not be a real existent. Similarly, the modalities are not real; they are relational judgments about existence or non-existence.
The argument is more assertive than deductive; the author presents it as the truth (al-haqq).
4
Distinguish i'tibari from adami
The passage mentions a controversy: are these modalities entirely privative (adami) or not? The author rejects both extremes, affirming they are i'tibari — neither fully privative nor fully positive.
An adami matter is a pure negation or absence; i'tibari is more nuanced.
5
Apply to a real example
Consider the proposition 'A living being is possibly alive tomorrow.' The possibility is not a thing in the world; it is a mental relation between the essence and future existence.
This applies directly in kalam discussions of divine attributes and human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غيره فإنه لا يلزم من كون الشَّيء لازما للشَّيء أنْ يكون وجوديًا فضلا عن أن يكون داخلا في ماهيّته. وقد يقال ممكن ويفهم منه معنى ثالث وهو سلب الضرورة من كل وجه، وهذا هو الإمكان الأخص. وإنَّه أحقُّ باسم الإمكان لخلوه عما ينافيه وهو الضَّرورة فتكون مقابلتها بحسب الذات والوصف والوقت وغير ذلك. (هـ) الخامس أن هذه الثلاثة وهو الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان ففيها من الكلام بأنَّها من الأمور العدمية كلا وجملة، أو لا من الأمور العدمية، والحق أنّها من الأمور الاعتبارية فلا وجود لها بنفسها في الأعيان. وقد قيل في الإمكان إنَّ العام منه عبارة عن سلب الامتناع والامتناع سلب محض فيكون الإمكان العام أمرًا ثبوتيا لكنَّه لا يمكن أن يكون جنسًا للوجوب بالذات. وللإمكان الخاص كذلك وإن كان مشتركًا بينهما ؛ إذ لو كان جنسا لكان امتياز الوجوب بالذات عن الإمكان بالفعل. وما يكون كذلك فهو مركب، والمركب ممكن لافتقاره إلى الغير وهو جزؤه فيكون الواجب ممكنا؛ هذا خلف. ثم لقائل أن يقول إنَّه سلب محض وإلا لا يكون محمولا على الإمكان الخاص، والإمكان الخاص سلب محض وإلا لكان ممكناً لذاته فيكون إمكانه زائدا عليه؛ ولزم التسلسل . وأما الخاص فإنه لا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا البتة؛ وذلك لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون الممكن موجودا؛ وحينئذ يكون واجب الوجود فلا يكون ممكن العدم، أو معدوما؛ وحينئذ يكون واجب العدم فلا يكون ممكن الوجود. ومَنْ أصرّ على هذا فإنه لا يعترف بالإمكان الخاص إلَّا بالنسبة إلى الزمان المستقبل، كما أنَّ هذا التخصيص متّصف بصفة كونه حيا في الحال؛ ففي الاستقبال يمكن أن يبقى حيا ويمكن أن لا يبقى لكنه لا يكون واردا على الحكم بكون الشَّيء ممكنا في الحال والاستقبال؛ إذ الحكم عليه بالنظر إلى ذاته لا بالنظر إلى كونه موجودًا أو معدوما. ولا يمتنع في العقل أن يكون الشَّيء واجبًا من وجه وممكنا من وجه.. وما يكون]
Otherwise, it does not follow from something being a necessary consequent of something that it is existent, let alone that it enters into its essence. And 'possible' may be said, and a third meaning is understood from it, which is the negation of necessity in every respect, and this is the specific possibility. And it is more deserving of the name 'possibility' because it is free from what contradicts it, namely necessity, so its opposition is according to essence, description, time, and other things. (H) Fifth: These three—namely necessity, impossibility, and possibility—involve discussion as to whether they are entirely privative matters, or not privative matters. The truth is that they are relational matters, so they have no existence by themselves in external entities. It has been said regarding possibility that the general one is an expression of the negation of impossibility, and impossibility is a pure negation, so general possibility becomes a positive matter, but it cannot be a genus for necessity per se. And likewise for specific possibility, even though it is common between them; for if it were a genus, then the distinction of necessity per se from specific possibility would be by actuality. And whatever is like that is composite, and the composite is possible due to its dependence on another, which is its part, so the necessary would be possible; this is a contradiction. Then one may say that it is a pure negation, otherwise it would not be predicated on specific possibility, and specific possibility is a pure negation, otherwise it would be possible in itself, so its possibility would be additional to it, and infinite regress would follow. As for the specific, it cannot exist at all; that is because the possible is either existent—and then it would be necessarily existent, so it would not be possibly non-existent—or non-existent—and then it would be necessarily non-existent, so it would not be possibly existent. And whoever insists on this does not acknowledge specific possibility except with respect to future time, just as this particular thing is described with the attribute of being alive in the present; so in the future it can remain alive or not remain, but this does not apply to the judgment that something is possible in the present and future, because the judgment concerning it is with respect to its essence, not with respect to its existence or non-existence. And it is not intellectually impossible for something to be necessary in one respect and possible in another respect.. and what is].
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the three primary modalities: wujub (necessity), imtina' (impossibility), and imkan (possibility). The author first clarifies that a necessary concomitant (lazim) need not be a real existent or part of the essence. He then distinguishes a third meaning of 'possible'—specific possibility (al-imkan al-khas), which is the negation of necessity in every respect, and argues it more fittingly bears the name 'possibility' than general possibility because it is free from the contrary, necessity. Opposition is considered in terms of essence, description, time, etc. The fifth discussion concerns whether these three are non-existential (adami) matters entirely or not; the truth is they are relational (i'tibari), having no independent existence in external reality. The author addresses a claim: general possibility is expressed as a negation of impossibility, and since impossibility is pure negation, general possibility would be a positive attribute. However, it cannot be a genus for necessity per se, nor for specific possibility, even though it is common. If it were a genus, necessity would be distinguished from specific possibility by actuality, making necessity composite and thus possible—a contradiction. Alternatively, if general possibility is pure negation, it cannot be predicated of specific possibility. Specific possibility itself must be pure negation, otherwise it would be possible in itself, requiring an extra possibility and leading to infinite regress. The author then argues that specific possibility cannot exist at all in the present: if a possible thing exists, it is necessary (so not possibly non-existent); if it does not exist, it is impossible (so not possibly existent). Thus, one who insists on this view only acknowledges specific possibility relative to future time, like the possibility of a living being continuing to live. The judgment of possibility is based on the essence itself, not on its existential state. The passage concludes that it is not rationally impossible for something to be necessary in one aspect and possible in another. This discussion is crucial for understanding modal logic in the Islamic tradition, particularly the distinction between general and specific possibility and the relational nature of modalities. All examples must be drawn from Islamic sciences: for instance, the attribute of 'life' in a living being is a real attribute, but the possibility of its continuation is a relational concept.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الامتناع
الإمكان
الأمور الاعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ontological status of wujub, imtina', and imkan?
A) They are real existent entities (a'yan).
B) They are purely privative (adami matters).
C) They are relational matters (i'tibari) with no independent existence.
D) They are both real and privative simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Necessity, impossibility, and possibility are relational concepts, not real entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'The resurrection is possible.' Identify whether this possibility is general or specific, and explain why it is an i'tibari matter according to the author.
?
In usul al-fiqh, jurists say 'Purity of water is a condition for wudu.' Is this condition a real attribute or an i'tibari relation? Argue using the author's framework.

Propositions

10 min
Specific Possibility and Future Time
Author argues that specific possibility only applies to future time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that specific possibility applies only to future time and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Specific possibility exists only for the future, not the present.

The author makes a striking claim: specific possibility (al-imkan al-khas) cannot exist in the present. If something exists, it is necessary; if it does not exist, it is impossible. Therefore specific possibility, defined as the negation of necessity in every respect, can only be predicated of future states. This lesson walks through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define specific possibility
Find the definition: specific possibility is the negation of necessity in every respect (salb al-darura min kull wajh). It is contrasted with general possibility.
It is more deserving of the name 'possibility' because it is free from necessity.
2
Set up the two cases
The author considers two exhaustive cases: the possible thing either exists or does not exist. If it exists, it is necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud) in that moment; if it does not exist, it is necessarily non-existent (wajib al-'adam).
In both cases, the thing does not exhibit specific possibility in the present.
3
Derive the temporal limitation
Since specific possibility negates necessity entirely, it cannot hold for an existing thing (which is necessary) or a non-existing thing (impossible). Hence it can only apply to future time, where the state is undetermined.
Example: a living being can continue to live or die in the future.
4
Understand the essence vs existence distinction
The author clarifies that the judgment of possibility is based on essence (dhat), not on current existence. This allows something to be necessary in one respect and possible in another.
This avoids contradiction.
5
Apply to a kalam example
In kalam, a created being's existence in the future is possible before it exists; after creation, it is actual and thus necessary in that moment.
This aligns with the Ash'ari view of contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that specific possibility cannot exist in the present?
A) Because the possible is always actual.
B) Because if it exists, it is necessary; if it does not exist, it is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Specific possibility is a concept that applies only to future time, not to present existence or non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional syllogism on the topic of a person's death: 'If Zaid is alive now, his death is possible in the future. If he is dead, his death is impossible.' Identify the form and conclusion.
?
Evaluate the statement: 'A possible thing can be both possibly existent and possibly non-existent in the present.' Does the author accept this? Why or why not?

Propositions

10 min
General Possibility Cannot Be a Genus
Author argues that general possibility cannot be a genus for necessity or specific possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio argument that general possibility cannot be a genus for necessity or specific possibility.

Core Idea
General possibility as a genus would make necessity possible, a contradiction.

Some thinkers treat general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) as a positive attribute and even as a genus (jins) for necessity and specific possibility. The author refutes this by showing that if it were a genus, necessity would become composite and thus possible—an absurd conclusion. This lesson unpacks the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify general possibility
General possibility is defined as the negation of impossibility (salb al-imtina'). It is a concept common to necessity and impossibility? Actually, it is common to both necessity and specific possibility? The text says it is common between them (necessity and specific possibility). So it is a broader concept.
General possibility is not the same as specific possibility; it is more inclusive.
2
Suppose general possibility is a genus
Assume for the sake of argument that general possibility (imkan 'amm) is a genus for necessity (wujub) and specific possibility (imkan khas). The author then examines the consequences.
This is a hypothetical scenario.
3
Derive composition
If it is a genus, then necessity must be distinguished from specific possibility by a differentia—in this case, actuality (fi'l). So necessity is composed of genus (general possibility) and differentia (actuality).
Actuality is the distinguishing factor.
4
Show contradiction
If necessity is composite, it depends on its parts, so it is possible (mumkin). But necessity (wajib) is by definition not possible—it is necessary. Thus the assumption leads to a contradiction: the necessary would be possible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Conclude the refutation
Therefore, general possibility cannot be a genus for necessity. The same argument applies to specific possibility as well.
The author further argues that general possibility must be a pure negation, otherwise it would lead to infinite regress when predicated of specific possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غيره فإنه لا يلزم من كون الشَّيء لازما للشَّيء أنْ يكون وجوديًا فضلا عن أن يكون داخلا في ماهيّته. وقد يقال ممكن ويفهم منه معنى ثالث وهو سلب الضرورة من كل وجه، وهذا هو الإمكان الأخص. وإنَّه أحقُّ باسم الإمكان لخلوه عما ينافيه وهو الضَّرورة فتكون مقابلتها بحسب الذات والوصف والوقت وغير ذلك. (هـ) الخامس أن هذه الثلاثة وهو الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان ففيها من الكلام بأنَّها من الأمور العدمية كلا وجملة، أو لا من الأمور العدمية، والحق أنّها من الأمور الاعتبارية فلا وجود لها بنفسها في الأعيان. وقد قيل في الإمكان إنَّ العام منه عبارة عن سلب الامتناع والامتناع سلب محض فيكون الإمكان العام أمرًا ثبوتيا لكنَّه لا يمكن أن يكون جنسًا للوجوب بالذات. وللإمكان الخاص كذلك وإن كان مشتركًا بينهما ؛ إذ لو كان جنسا لكان امتياز الوجوب بالذات عن الإمكان بالفعل. وما يكون كذلك فهو مركب، والمركب ممكن لافتقاره إلى الغير وهو جزؤه فيكون الواجب ممكنا؛ هذا خلف. ثم لقائل أن يقول إنَّه سلب محض وإلا لا يكون محمولا على الإمكان الخاص، والإمكان الخاص سلب محض وإلا لكان ممكناً لذاته فيكون إمكانه زائدا عليه؛ ولزم التسلسل . وأما الخاص فإنه لا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا البتة؛ وذلك لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون الممكن موجودا؛ وحينئذ يكون واجب الوجود فلا يكون ممكن العدم، أو معدوما؛ وحينئذ يكون واجب العدم فلا يكون ممكن الوجود. ومَنْ أصرّ على هذا فإنه لا يعترف بالإمكان الخاص إلَّا بالنسبة إلى الزمان المستقبل، كما أنَّ هذا التخصيص متّصف بصفة كونه حيا في الحال؛ ففي الاستقبال يمكن أن يبقى حيا ويمكن أن لا يبقى لكنه لا يكون واردا على الحكم بكون الشَّيء ممكنا في الحال والاستقبال؛ إذ الحكم عليه بالنظر إلى ذاته لا بالنظر إلى كونه موجودًا أو معدوما. ولا يمتنع في العقل أن يكون الشَّيء واجبًا من وجه وممكنا من وجه.. وما يكون]
Otherwise, it does not follow from something being a necessary consequent of something that it is existent, let alone that it enters into its essence. And 'possible' may be said, and a third meaning is understood from it, which is the negation of necessity in every respect, and this is the specific possibility. And it is more deserving of the name 'possibility' because it is free from what contradicts it, namely necessity, so its opposition is according to essence, description, time, and other things. (H) Fifth: These three—namely necessity, impossibility, and possibility—involve discussion as to whether they are entirely privative matters, or not privative matters. The truth is that they are relational matters, so they have no existence by themselves in external entities. It has been said regarding possibility that the general one is an expression of the negation of impossibility, and impossibility is a pure negation, so general possibility becomes a positive matter, but it cannot be a genus for necessity per se. And likewise for specific possibility, even though it is common between them; for if it were a genus, then the distinction of necessity per se from specific possibility would be by actuality. And whatever is like that is composite, and the composite is possible due to its dependence on another, which is its part, so the necessary would be possible; this is a contradiction. Then one may say that it is a pure negation, otherwise it would not be predicated on specific possibility, and specific possibility is a pure negation, otherwise it would be possible in itself, so its possibility would be additional to it, and infinite regress would follow. As for the specific, it cannot exist at all; that is because the possible is either existent—and then it would be necessarily existent, so it would not be possibly non-existent—or non-existent—and then it would be necessarily non-existent, so it would not be possibly existent. And whoever insists on this does not acknowledge specific possibility except with respect to future time, just as this particular thing is described with the attribute of being alive in the present; so in the future it can remain alive or not remain, but this does not apply to the judgment that something is possible in the present and future, because the judgment concerning it is with respect to its essence, not with respect to its existence or non-existence. And it is not intellectually impossible for something to be necessary in one respect and possible in another respect.. and what is].
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the three primary modalities: wujub (necessity), imtina' (impossibility), and imkan (possibility). The author first clarifies that a necessary concomitant (lazim) need not be a real existent or part of the essence. He then distinguishes a third meaning of 'possible'—specific possibility (al-imkan al-khas), which is the negation of necessity in every respect, and argues it more fittingly bears the name 'possibility' than general possibility because it is free from the contrary, necessity. Opposition is considered in terms of essence, description, time, etc. The fifth discussion concerns whether these three are non-existential (adami) matters entirely or not; the truth is they are relational (i'tibari), having no independent existence in external reality. The author addresses a claim: general possibility is expressed as a negation of impossibility, and since impossibility is pure negation, general possibility would be a positive attribute. However, it cannot be a genus for necessity per se, nor for specific possibility, even though it is common. If it were a genus, necessity would be distinguished from specific possibility by actuality, making necessity composite and thus possible—a contradiction. Alternatively, if general possibility is pure negation, it cannot be predicated of specific possibility. Specific possibility itself must be pure negation, otherwise it would be possible in itself, requiring an extra possibility and leading to infinite regress. The author then argues that specific possibility cannot exist at all in the present: if a possible thing exists, it is necessary (so not possibly non-existent); if it does not exist, it is impossible (so not possibly existent). Thus, one who insists on this view only acknowledges specific possibility relative to future time, like the possibility of a living being continuing to live. The judgment of possibility is based on the essence itself, not on its existential state. The passage concludes that it is not rationally impossible for something to be necessary in one aspect and possible in another. This discussion is crucial for understanding modal logic in the Islamic tradition, particularly the distinction between general and specific possibility and the relational nature of modalities. All examples must be drawn from Islamic sciences: for instance, the attribute of 'life' in a living being is a real attribute, but the possibility of its continuation is a relational concept.

الإمكان العام
الجنس
الوجوب بالذات
التركيب
التسلسل

Distinguishing Genuine and Derivative Predication
Author distinguishes two ways something can be attributed to a subject: essential predication and derivative attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given predication statement as genuine (al-mahmul 'ala al-mawdu) or derivative (al-mushtaq) based on the criteria in the passage.

Core Idea
Genuine predication identifies the subject with its essence; derivative predication only attributes a derived property.

This passage lays the foundation for understanding propositions in Islamic logic. By distinguishing genuine predication (e.g., 'Man is animal') from derivative attribution (e.g., 'The cloth is white'), you gain a tool essential for analyzing definitions and arguments in usul al-fiqh and kalam. The author also clarifies when a universal subject requires a universal predicate for genuine predication, and when particular subjects yield only conventional identification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
In any predication statement, find the subject (al-mawdu) and the predicate (al-mahmul). For example, in 'Man is animal', subject = man, predicate = animal.
2
Test if the subject can be said to be the predicate
Ask: Can we say 'the subject is the predicate'? If yes, it is genuine predication. For 'Man is animal', we can say 'Man is animal' – so genuine. For 'The cloth is white', we cannot say 'the cloth is whiteness' – so derivative.
3
Check if the predicate is a derived term
A derived term (mushtaq) like 'white' from 'whiteness' or 'writer' from 'writing' indicates derivative predication. The predicate is not identical to the subject but expresses possession of an attribute.
4
Handle particular subjects
If the subject is particular (e.g., 'Zayd'), and the predicate is also a particular distinct from the subject, genuine predication is impossible. Such statements are only conventional identifications, as when we say 'Zayd is Abu al-Qasim' – this identifies rather than describes essence.
5
Require universal predicate for universal subject
For genuine predication when the subject is universal, the predicate must also be universal. This ensures the predication is real (bil-haqiqa) and not merely verbal. For example, 'Man is animal' works because both are universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينسب إلى الموضوع تكون نسبته إليه على وجهين: أحدهما أنه بحيث يمكن أن يقال إن الموضوع هو كالحيوان الذي يمكن أن يقال إن الإنسان هو جنس يقال إن الإنسان حيوان، ومثل هذا فهو المحمول على الموضوع. وثانيهما أنه بحيث لا يمكن كالبياض الذي لا يمكن أن يقال لموضوعه الذي هو الثوب مثلا أنه هو فلا يقال إنه بياض؛ بل يقال إنه ذو بياض أو أبيض، وهذا لفظ مشتق من البياض أو مؤلف منه ومن غيره. والموضوع المجرد عن السور لا يخلو من أن يكون كليا أو جزئيًا، فإن كان جزئيًا فالمحمول عليه إن كان جزئيا لم يكن ذلك غيره فإن الجزئيين المتابينين لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر فلا يمكن أن يقال إن هذه الإنسان ذلك الإنسان. وإذا لم يكن ذلك غيره لم يكن في الحقيقة موضوعًا ومحمولًا بحسب الطبع بل بحسب القول كما يقال زيد هو أبو القاسم أو ابن عمرو، وكذلك إذا قلت هذا الأبيض هو هذا الكاتب. وإن كان كليًّا فالمحمول عليه بالحقيقة لا يكون إلا كليا فإن طبيعة
Then that which is attributed to the subject: its attribution to it is in two ways: one of them is that it is such that it can be said that the subject is it, like animal, which can be said that man is a genus, it is said that man is animal, and such is the predicate on the subject. The second is that it cannot, like whiteness, which cannot be said of its subject, which is cloth for example, that it is it, so it is not said that it is whiteness; rather it is said that it is possessor of whiteness or white, and this is a word derived from whiteness or composed from it and from something else. And the subject stripped of quantifiers is either universal or particular. If it is particular, then the predicate if it is particular is not other than it, for two distinct particulars are not predicated one of the other, so it cannot be said that this man is that man. And if it is not other than it, then it is not in reality subject and predicate according to nature, but according to speech, as when one says Zayd is Abu al-Qasim or Ibn Amr, and likewise if you say this white is this writer. And if it is universal, then the predicate in reality is only universal, for the nature...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage classifies the two types of attribution (nisba) of a predicate to a subject. The first type is genuine predication (al-mahmul 'ala al-mawdu), where the subject can be said to be the predicate itself, e.g., 'Man is animal' – here animal is truly predicated of man, and man is animal. The second type is derivative, where the predicate is not identical to the subject but the subject possesses an attribute, e.g., 'The cloth is white' (not 'the cloth is whiteness'). The predicate 'white' is derived from the abstract noun 'whiteness'. The author then considers the subject stripped of quantifiers (al-mawdu mujarradan 'an al-sawar). If the subject is particular and the predicate is also particular and distinct, they cannot be genuinely predicated (e.g., 'This man is that man' is false unless they are the same). Such predication is only conventional (bi-hasb al-qawl), like calling Zayd 'Abu al-Qasim' – that is an identifcatory predicate, not a description of essence. If the subject is universal, then the predicate must also be universal for genuine predication. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing the nature of universal predication.

Key Terms
المحمول على الموضوع
المحمول بالحقيقة
المشتق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Genuine predication requires the subject to be identical to the predicate; otherwise the attribution is derivative or conventional.

Linguistic vs Logical Composition
Author distinguishes linguistic from logical composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between linguistic composition (ta'lif lughawi) and logical composition (ta'lif mantiqi) using the author's examples of 'Ta'abbata Sharran', 'amshi', and 'ya'ishu'.

Core Idea
Not every compound word is a logical unit; logical composition requires semantic determination.

The author begins by reminding us that the ten categories provide the raw material for composition. But not everything that sounds compound is logically compound. He gives striking examples to show that language and logic do not always align: a phrase like 'Ta'abbata Sharran' is linguistically compound but logically simple, while verbs like 'amshi' are logically compound because their prefixes specify the subject. Understanding this distinction is crucial for recognizing what counts as a proper logical component.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of composition
Notice that the author distinguishes composition 'according to hearing' (linguistic) from composition 'according to the usage of the logicians' (logical). Read the examples carefully.
2
Analyze 'Ta'abbata Sharran'
This is a proper name meaning 'He carried evil under his armpit'. It is linguistically a sentence, but the author says it is not logically composed. Why? Because it does not break down into independent logical parts that each contribute a clear meaning to a proposition.
The term 'logically composed' here means the expression can serve as a unit in a logical argument.
3
Examine inflected verbs
Look at 'amshi' (I walk), 'namshi' (we walk), 'tamshi' (you walk). The prefixes hamza, nun, ta each specify the subject. They add an additional meaning (the subject) to the root meaning 'walking'. This addition makes them logically composed, because they combine two atomic meanings: walking + a specific agent.
Contrast with 'ya'ishu' (he lives): the ya only indicates an unspecified subject, so it does not add a new atomic meaning.
4
Apply the rule to a new example
Consider 'qatala' (he killed) vs 'qatalahu' (he killed him). Which is logically composed? (Answer: 'qatalahu' has an object pronoun, adding an atomic meaning, so it is logically composed.)
Test your understanding by finding other examples from Arabic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما يكون من اللوازم ذكره في هذا الموضع فذلك أن يقال إن الألفاظ العشرة، ومعانيها هي التي تكون أحرى لما يؤلف. وليس كل لفظ مؤلف بحسب المسموع يكون لفظًا مؤلفًا بحسب استعمال أهل المنطق. ألا ترى تأبط شرًا، وأمثال هذا مؤلف بحسب اللغة؟ ولا يعد من مؤلفات بحسب المنطق، وربما كان اللفظ بحسب اللغة غير مؤلف، وبحسب المنطق مؤلف كما مرَّ ذكره من قبل أن قولنا أمشي ونمشي وتمشي من المؤلفات؛ فإن همزة أمشي ونون نمشي وتاء تمشي كلها تدل على تعيين للموضوع دلالة لفظ مفرد على معنى مفرد : وذلك زيادة دلالة بالكلمة. وأما يعيش بالياء فإنه ليس في عداد المؤلفات؛ لأن الياء فيه تدل على نسبة إلى موضوع غير معين فقط. فليس فيه إلا مجرد الدلالة التي للكلمة وهي الدلالة على موضوع غير معين؛ فهذه العشرة هي التي تؤخذ منها أجزاء الألفاظ المؤلفة تسمى أقوالًا، وبعض ما يؤلف من معاني هذه يكون قصة وخبراً، وبعض ذلك ليس قصة وخبراً نحو الحد مثلا. فإنه يؤلف من ألفاظ يأتي بعضها أثر بعض على سبيل زيادة تعريف أو تخصيص للمعنى المتقدم على أنه هو نحو قولك الحيوان الناطق، فإن ذلك كقولك الحيوان الذي هو الناطق. وهذه الألفاظ المفردة فإنها لا تدل على معنى صادق ولا كاذب، ولا معانيها في النفس تكون صدقا ولا كذبًا؛ الصدق والكذب الذي في المعاني، بل إذا ألفت هذه الألفاظ على وجه مخصوص دلت على معنى صادق أو كاذب ومعانيها إذا ألفت في الذهن كانت صادقة إن كانت مطابقة للوجود، أو كاذبة إن لم تكن مطابقة، وبالجملة فهذه وإن لم يكن صادقة أو كاذبة فهي أجزاء الصادقة والكاذبة.
As for what must be mentioned in this place, that is to say that the ten words and their meanings are those that are more worthy for composition. Not every word that is composed according to hearing is a composed word according to the usage of the logicians. Do you not see 'Ta'abbata Sharran' and the like? It is composed according to language, but it is not counted among composed expressions according to logic. And sometimes a word is not composed according to language but is composed according to logic, as was mentioned earlier, that our saying 'amshi' (I walk), 'namshi' (we walk), 'tamshi' (you walk) are among the composed expressions. For the hamza of 'amshi', the nun of 'namshi', and the ta of 'tamshi' all indicate a specification of the subject, indicating a single word for a single meaning, and that is an additional indication in the word. As for 'ya'ishu' (he lives) with the ya, it is not among the composed expressions, because the ya in it only indicates a relation to an unspecified subject. So there is in it only the mere indication that belongs to the word, which is indication of an unspecified subject. These ten are the ones from which the parts of composed utterances are taken; they are called utterances (aqwal). Some of what is composed from their meanings is a story (qissa) and a report (khabar), and some of that is not a story and report, like the definition (hadd) for example. For it is composed from words that come one after another by way of increasing definition or specification of the preceding meaning as being it, like your saying 'al-hayawan al-natiq' (the rational animal). For that is like your saying 'al-hayawan alladhi huwa al-natiq' (the animal that is the rational). And these individual words do not indicate a meaning that is true or false, nor do their meanings in the soul become true or false; truth and falsity are in meanings. Rather, when these words are composed in a specific manner, they indicate a meaning that is true or false, and their meanings when composed in the mind are true if they correspond to existence, or false if they do not. In summary, these, even if they are not themselves true or false, are parts of the true and false.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of logical composition (ta'lif) and its relation to truth. The author begins by noting that the ten categories provide the material for composition. He then distinguishes between linguistic composition and logical composition: a phrase like 'Ta'abbata Sharran' is linguistically compound but not logically, because it does not function as a propositional component. Conversely, inflected verbs like 'amshi' (I walk) are logically compound because the prefix indicates a specific subject. The prefix adds a determination, whereas the ya in 'ya'ishu' (he lives) only indicates an unspecified subject, so it is not logically compound. The ten categories serve as the atomic elements from which logical utterances (aqwal) are built. Some compositions yield a 'story' (qissa) or 'report' (khabar) — i.e., a proposition that can be true or false — while others, like definitions (hadd), do not. The author uses 'al-hayawan al-natiq' (rational animal) as an example of a definition that specifies a meaning but does not assert something true or false. Finally, he explicitly states that simple words and their mental meanings are not themselves truth-bearing; truth arises only when words are combined in a specific way and the resulting meaning corresponds to reality (mutabaqa li-l-wujud).

Key Takeaway
Logical composition requires that the parts contribute distinct atomic meanings, as seen in inflected verbs that specify the subject.

When Composition Becomes Truth-Bearing
Author explains truth conditions for compositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of composed expressions can be true or false and explain why definitions like 'al-hayawan al-natiq' are not truth-bearing.

Core Idea
Truth and falsity arise only when meanings are composed in a way that asserts correspondence with existence.

In the second part of the passage, the author turns from the building blocks of composition to their products. Some compositions yield 'story' (qissa) or 'report' (khabar) – these are truth-bearing propositions. Others, like definitions (hadd), specify meaning without asserting anything. Understanding this distinction is essential for logic because truth is the ultimate concern of sound reasoning. The author also gives the classic definition of truth as correspondence (mutabaqa).

Guided Path
1
Separate truth-bearing from non-truth-bearing compositions
The author divides composed meanings into two kinds: those that are 'qissa/khabar' (story/report) and those that are not. The first kind can be true or false; the second kind, like definitions, cannot.
A definition like 'al-hayawan al-natiq' (rational animal) tells you what something is but does not claim that such a thing exists.
2
Understand the definition example
The author explains that 'al-hayawan al-natiq' is equivalent to saying 'the animal that is the rational'. This composition adds specification but does not assert any fact. It is a complex expression that points to an essence, not a judgment.
Contrast with a proposition like 'al-insanu hayawan natiq' (Man is a rational animal), which asserts that man indeed is rational animal – that can be true or false.
3
Grasp the correspondence theory of truth
Truth (sidq) is defined as correspondence (mutabaqa) between a mental meaning and external existence (wujud). Falsity (kidhb) is lack of correspondence. This is a foundational definition in Islamic logic.
This definition aligns with the standard realist epistemology of the Islamic tradition.
4
Apply: classify compositions
Given a composed expression, determine whether it asserts a relation to existence (i.e., it can be true or false) or merely defines a concept. For example, 'al-‘alamu muhdath' (The world is temporally originated) is a proposition; 'al-‘alamu al-muhdath' (the temporally originated world) is a definition-like phrase.
This skill is crucial for evaluating arguments: only propositions serve as premises or conclusions.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of logical composition (ta'lif) and its relation to truth. The author begins by noting that the ten categories provide the material for composition. He then distinguishes between linguistic composition and logical composition: a phrase like 'Ta'abbata Sharran' is linguistically compound but not logically, because it does not function as a propositional component. Conversely, inflected verbs like 'amshi' (I walk) are logically compound because the prefix indicates a specific subject. The prefix adds a determination, whereas the ya in 'ya'ishu' (he lives) only indicates an unspecified subject, so it is not logically compound. The ten categories serve as the atomic elements from which logical utterances (aqwal) are built. Some compositions yield a 'story' (qissa) or 'report' (khabar) — i.e., a proposition that can be true or false — while others, like definitions (hadd), do not. The author uses 'al-hayawan al-natiq' (rational animal) as an example of a definition that specifies a meaning but does not assert something true or false. Finally, he explicitly states that simple words and their mental meanings are not themselves truth-bearing; truth arises only when words are combined in a specific way and the resulting meaning corresponds to reality (mutabaqa li-l-wujud).

Key Terms
قصة
خبر
حد
صدق
كذب
مطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is grammatically correct.

C) It is composed of words from the ten categories.
D) It is a definition.

Key Takeaway
Only statements that assert a relation to reality (khabar) can be true or false; definitions and other non-assertive compositions are not truth-bearers.

The Unique Property of Affirmation and Negation Among Opposites
Author explains why affirmation/negation are distinct from other opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish affirmation/negation from other opposites by determining whether both can be false given the subject's existence.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation are the only opposites where one is always true, regardless of subject existence.

In logic, opposites are not all the same. The author of this passage explains a crucial difference: affirmation and negation, which form contradictory opposites, have a unique property—they always yield one true and one false statement, no matter if the subject exists or not. Other opposites, like relatives and contraries, can both be false. Understanding this distinction is essential for accurate logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed property of affirmation/negation
Locate the statement that one of them is always true and the other false, whether the subject exists or not. This is the unique characteristic.
2
Examine the relatives example
Consider the pair 'Zayd is the father of Khalid' and 'Zayd is the son of Khalid'. Both are false because a person cannot be both father and son to the same individual.
Relatives can both be false.
3
Examine the contraries with an intermediate
For hot and cold, if an intermediate (e.g., lukewarm) exists, then a lukewarm object is neither hot nor cold, so both propositions are false.
Contraries with an intermediate can both be false.
4
Understand contraries without intermediate
If two contraries have no intermediate and the subject exists, one must be true; if the subject is non-existent, both are false.
5
Summarize the distinction
Only affirmation/negation guarantee a truth value regardless of subject existence; other opposites may both be false under certain conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في الفرق بين هذه الأقسام. وأما الفرق بين هذه الأقسام على حسب الرأيين فذلك أن الإيجاب والسلب يفارق سائر المتقابلات لما أنه في القول لا في الوجود وأحدهما صادق لا محالة والآخر كاذب سواء كان الموضوع موجودًا أو لم يكن موجودًا؛ وهذا في الإيجاب والسلب اللذين هما إثبات شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه كما في المتناقضين. وأما سائر المتقابلات فيمكن أن يكذبا جميعًا إذا نقلا إلى الحكم كما في المضاف مثلا : فإنك إذا قلت زيد أبو خالد، زید بن خالد فقد كذبت في الحكمين جميعا. وكما أنه يمكن الكذب في المتضايفين جميعًا فكذلك في المتضادين فإنه إذا كان بينهما واسطة كانت الواسطة سلب الطرفين مطلقًا من غير إثبات واسطة خلطية كالفاتر بين الحار الصرف والبارد الصرف، وكهيئة بين الأسود الصرف والأبيض الصرف كذلك. وربما لم يكن لها اسم محصل بل إنما يدل عليها سلب الطرفين مطلقًا كقولنا لا عادل ولا جائر، وهذا السلب مما يفارق السلب في قولنا السماء لا ثقيلة ولا خفيفة؛ إذ ذاك لا يدل على إثبات واسطة خلطية. وإن كان يدل على إثبات واسطة فالموضوع عند تحقق الواسطة يكذب عليه كل واحد من الطرفين أيضًا، وإن كان أحد الطرفين لازما له فعند عدم الموضوع تقديرًا أو تحقيقا يكذب عليه كل واحد منهما كذلك إذا كان بين الطرفين واسطة. فإذا لم يكن فأحدهما واجب لا محالة ما دام الموضوع موجودًا، وأما إذا كان معدوما فـلا فإنه يكذب كل واحد منهما حينئذ.
Chapter: On the difference between these divisions. As for the difference between these divisions according to the two opinions, it is that affirmation and negation differ from the rest of the opposites because they are in speech not in existence, and one of them is necessarily true and the other false, whether the subject exists or does not exist. This is in affirmation and negation, which are affirming something of something or negating it from it, as in contradictories. As for the rest of the opposites, it is possible for both to be false when transferred to judgment, as in relatives for example: if you say 'Zayd is the father of Khalid' and 'Zayd is the son of Khalid', you have lied in both judgments. And just as lying is possible in relatives together, so too in contraries: if there is an intermediate between them, the intermediate is the negation of both extremes absolutely, without affirming a composite intermediate, like the lukewarm between pure hot and pure cold, and like the grey between pure black and pure white. Sometimes it has no exact name but is only indicated by the absolute negation of both extremes, as we say 'he is neither just nor unjust'. This negation differs from the negation in our saying 'the sky is neither heavy nor light', because that does not indicate the affirmation of a composite intermediate. And if it does indicate the affirmation of an intermediate, then when the intermediate is realized, both extremes are false of the subject; and if one extreme is necessary for it, then when the subject is absent in estimation or actuality, each of them is false as well, if there is an intermediate between the extremes. So if there is no intermediate, then one of them is necessarily true as long as the subject exists; but if it is non-existent, then both of them are false in that case.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage defines the unique property of affirmation/negation (al-ijāb wa al-salb) compared to other types of opposites. The author begins by stating that the distinction is based on two opinions. Affirmation and negation are the only opposites where one is always true and the other false, whether the subject exists or not. This is because they are in speech, not in existence. For other opposites, both can be false when transferred to judgment. For example, with relatives, if you say 'Zayd is the father of Khalid' and 'Zayd is the son of Khalid', both are false (since Zayd cannot be both father and son of the same person). Similarly, with contraries like hot and cold, if there is an intermediate (e.g., lukewarm), neither extreme is true of the subject (e.g., water is neither hot nor cold if it is lukewarm). The author clarifies that the negation indicating the intermediate is different from a simple negation like 'the sky is neither heavy nor light', which does not imply an intermediate but a lack of both qualities. If an intermediate is implied, then when it applies, both extremes are false. When the subject does not exist, both extremes are also false. If there is no intermediate between contraries, then one must be true as long as the subject exists; otherwise, both are false. This explanation uses Islamic examples: heat/cold, black/white, justice/injustice are all valid categories within the Islamic intellectual tradition. The passage guards against assuming all opposites behave like contradictories (where one is always true).

Key Terms
الإيجاب والسلب (al-ījāb wa al-salb): Affirmation and negation; a pair of propositions where one affirms a predicate of a subject and the other denies it, considered the only opposites that are always truth-valued regardless of subject existence.
المتقابلات (al-mutaqābilāt): Opposites; the four categories: contradictories, relatives, contraries, and privation/possession.
المتناقضين (al-mutanāqiḍayn): Contradictories; identified with affirmation/negation in this passage.
المتضايفين (al-mutaḍā'ifayn): Relatives; opposites that are defined by relation to another, e.g., father and son.
المتضادين (al-mutaḍāddayn): Contraries; opposites that may have an intermediate between them, e.g., hot and cold.
الواسطة (al-wāsiṭa): Intermediate; a term that is neither extreme, e.g., lukewarm between hot and cold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Relatives (e.g., 'Zayd is the father of Khalid' and 'Zayd is the son of Khalid')
C) Contraries with an intermediate (e.g., 'This water is hot' and 'This water is cold' if it is lukewarm)
D) Affirmation and negation (e.g., 'The world is created' and 'The world is not created')

Key Takeaway
Only affirmation and negation (contradictories) guarantee one true and one false proposition regardless of subject existence; other opposites can both be false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Unique Conditions of Privation and Possession
Author explains the unique conditions of the 'adam wa malaka opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish privation/possession from other opposites by identifying the required conditions of the subject and time.

Core Idea
Privation/possession requires a capable subject; it is not a simple contradictory.

In this passage, the author clarifies what makes privation/possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka) unique among the six types of opposites. You will learn the precise conditions that apply to this type of opposition and why it is not a simple contradictory. The classic example of blindness and sight in animals illustrates the key points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subtypes
Notice that the author distinguishes between the commonly known (mashhūrī) and the real (ḥaqīqī) privation/possession. The example of blindness and sight applies to both.
2
Understand the time condition
In the mashhūrī type, both extremes are negated before the time when the possession normally occurs. For instance, a child is neither blind nor sighted before the age of sight.
3
Understand the subject condition
Even when the subject exists, if it is not the kind that can possess the quality (e.g., a stone for sight), both terms are false. The subject must be capable of possessing the attribute.
4
Distinguish from other opposites
Unlike contradictories (where one always applies) or contraries (where both can be false but do not require a capable subject), privation/possession demands a specific subject and sometimes a specific time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العدم والملكة فسلب كل واحد من الطرفين قبل حلول الوقت في المشهوري منه ظاهر، وإن كان الموضوع موجودًا فإن الجزء الغير المفقح لا أعمى ولا بصير بحسب المشهور ويعم المشهوري والحقيقي جميعا عند عدم الموضوع فإن المعدوم لا أعمى ولا بصير. ولما كان العمى والبصر من خواص الحيوان فالجماد لا أعمى ولا بصير بالضرورة. ثم العدم الحقيقي وإن كان أعم من المشهوري فليس عدما مطلقا حتى يصدق إطلاقه عند عدم الموضوع بل هو عدم عن موضوع يمكن أن يكون مع الشيء المعدوم؛ وهذا الموضوع لا بد من أن يكون موجودًا.
As for privation and possession, it is clear that the negation of each of the two extremes before the time occurs in the commonly known one, and if the subject exists, then the non-fecund part [؟] is neither blind nor sighted according to the commonly known, and it applies to both the commonly known and the real together when the subject is absent, for the non-existent is neither blind nor sighted. And since blindness and sight are properties of animals, then inanimate objects are necessarily neither blind nor sighted. Then the real privation, although it is more general than the commonly known, is not an absolute privation such that its application is true when the subject is absent; rather, it is a privation of a subject that can exist with the privated thing; and this subject must exist.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage defines the distinct feature of privation/possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka) among the opposites. The author states that in the commonly known (mashhuri) type, both extremes are negated before the time when the possession normally occurs. For example, prior to a certain age, a child is neither blind nor sighted (since sight is expected later). But even when the subject exists, if the subject is of a kind that does not normally possess the quality, both terms are false: e.g., a stone (in the case of blindness/sight) is neither blind nor sighted. The author then distinguishes between the commonly known privation and the real privation. The real privation is more general but still requires a subject that can possess the quality; it is not absolutely true when the subject is absent. Thus, for privation/possession, the subject must exist and be of a type that can potentially possess the attribute. This explanation uses the Islamic example of blindness and sight in animals, a standard illustration in the tradition. The passage guards against assuming that privation/possession works like ordinary contradictories where one term always applies.

Key Terms
العدم والملكة
المشهوري
الحقيقي
الموضوع

Key Takeaway
Privation/possession is a unique type of opposition that requires a subject capable of possessing the quality, and in the commonly known subtype, time also matters.

What Makes Relatives Unique Among Opposites
Author defines relatives and contrasts them with other opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify relatives by the condition of mutual entailment in existence and non-existence and distinguish them from contraries, contradictories, and privation/possession.

Core Idea
Relatives imply each other; other opposites do not.

In this passage, the author clarifies that relatives have a unique property: each relative is defined by reference to another that necessarily accompanies it in both existence and non-existence. For example, 'father' implies 'son' – if one exists, the other exists; if one is negated, the other is negated. This is not true for other opposites like contraries (hot/cold) or contradictories (affirmative/negative). Understanding this distinction helps you correctly classify propositions in logic and Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposite type
Look for the opposite pairs discussed: relatives, contraries, contradictories, privation/possession. Note which one is being defined.
The passage focuses on relatives first.
2
Extract the defining condition for relatives
Each relative is 'said with reference to another which accompanies it in existence and non-existence.' This is the key property: mutual entailment.
For example, 'father' and 'son' always go together.
3
Apply the condition to other opposites
Check if contraries, contradictories, and privation/possession satisfy this condition. The author says they do not. For instance, 'hot' does not imply 'cold' because lukewarm exists.
Contraries can have an intermediate.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Use examples like father/son (relatives) vs. valid/invalid (contradictories) to see if mutual entailment holds.
Think of pairs from fiqh or usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفرق بين المتضايفين وسائر المتقابلات فذلك أن كل واحد من المتضايفين مقول بالقياس إلى الغير الذي يلزمه وجودًا وعدما، والغير من المتقابلات لا يكون كذلك. وأما الفرق بين المتضادين وسائرها فإن المتضادين قد تكون بينهما واسطة ينتقل إليها الطرفان؛ وهذه الواسطة لا يمكن أن تكون للغير. وأما بين العدم والملكة وسائرها فالجزء الموضوع في العدم والملكة مشهوريًا كان أو حقيقيًا مشروط بشرط لا يعتبر ذلك الشرط في موضوع الغير أصلا؛ وذلك من جملة ما مرَّ ذكره عند تحقيق العدم والملكة في الفصل السابق فلا يعاد مرة أخرى. وكما أن الفرق متحقق بين كل واحد منها وبين الغير على سبيل العموم فكذلك بين كل واحد منها وبين الغير على سبيل الخصوص. أما بين المتضادين والمتناقضين فظاهر لما امتنع في المتناقضين أن يكون كل واحد منهما بالإيجاب، وقد كان من اللوازم أن يكون أحدهما بالسلب على ما عرف. وكذلك بينهما وبين المتضايفين. فإن المضاف لا يكون مضافًا إلا بالقياس إلى الغير على خلاف الضد. ولأن
As for the difference between relatives and the rest of the opposites, it is that each of the relatives is said with reference to another which accompanies it in existence and non-existence, but the other opposites are not like that. As for the difference between contraries and the rest, it is that contraries may have an intermediate to which both extremes move, and this intermediate cannot exist for the others. As for between privation/possession and the rest, the subject part in privation/possession, whether commonly known or real, is conditioned by a condition that is not considered in the subject of the other at all; this is among what was mentioned when investigating privation/possession in the previous chapter and will not be repeated. And just as the difference is realized between each one and the others in a general way, so too between each one and the others in a specific way. As for between contraries and contradictories, it is clear because it is impossible in contradictories for each of them to be affirmative, and one of them must be negative, as known. And similarly between them and relatives: for a relative is only relative by reference to another, unlike the contrary. And because...
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage distinguishes relatives (al-mutaḍā'ifayn) and contraries (al-mutaḍāddayn) from the other opposites, including each other. The author begins with relatives: each relative is defined by its relation to another, and that other necessarily accompanies it in existence and non-existence. For example, 'father' implies 'son'; if one exists, the other must exist, and if one is negated, the other is also negated. This is not the case for other opposites. Next, contraries can have an intermediate (like lukewarm between hot and cold) that both extremes can move towards; this intermediate does not exist for other opposites (e.g., contradictories have no intermediate). Then the author summarizes that privation/possession has already been distinguished in the previous chapter, noting its condition on the subject. Finally, specific differences are given: between contraries and contradictories, it is impossible for both contradictories to be affirmative; one must be negative. Between contraries and relatives, a relative is defined by relation to another, whereas a contrary is not (e.g., 'hot' is hot in itself, not relative to 'cold'). The passage ends with 'and because' (wala'anna), suggesting a continuation. The author uses these logical distinctions to help the student correctly classify propositions. Islamic science examples: 'blindness/sight' for privation, 'father/son' for relatives, 'heat/cold' for contraries.

Contraries: The Only Opposites with an Intermediate
Author contrasts contraries with other opposites, highlighting the intermediate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contraries by the presence of an intermediate and distinguish them from contradictories, relatives, and privation/possession.

Core Idea
Contraries allow an intermediate; other opposites do not.

The author contrasts contraries with other opposites by noting that contraries may have an intermediate to which both extremes can move, such as lukewarm between hot and cold. This intermediate is absent in other opposites: contradictories have no middle (e.g., either valid or invalid), relatives have no intermediate (no third between father and son), and privation/possession has a conditioned subject. Recognizing this feature helps you correctly classify propositions in logic and Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify contraries in the passage
Look for pairs like hot/cold. The author uses this as an example of a contrary pair.
Contraries are a type of opposite.
2
Recognize the intermediate property
Contraries may have an intermediate that both extremes can move towards. For example, between hot and cold there is lukewarm.
This is the key distinguishing feature.
3
Compare with contradictories
Contradictories cannot both be affirmative; one must be negative, and they have no middle. For example, 'the prayer is valid' and 'the prayer is not valid' – there is no third option.
Contradictories are exhaustive and exclusive.
4
Compare with relatives and privation
Relatives have no intermediate (no third between father and son). Privation/possession has a subject condition and no intermediate either.
Only contraries have this feature.
5
Apply with Islamic examples
In fiqh, 'wajib' (obligatory) and 'haram' (forbidden) are contraries because there is an intermediate of 'mubah' (permissible) or 'mandub' (recommended).
Check if an intermediate exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفرق بين المتضايفين وسائر المتقابلات فذلك أن كل واحد من المتضايفين مقول بالقياس إلى الغير الذي يلزمه وجودًا وعدما، والغير من المتقابلات لا يكون كذلك. وأما الفرق بين المتضادين وسائرها فإن المتضادين قد تكون بينهما واسطة ينتقل إليها الطرفان؛ وهذه الواسطة لا يمكن أن تكون للغير. وأما بين العدم والملكة وسائرها فالجزء الموضوع في العدم والملكة مشهوريًا كان أو حقيقيًا مشروط بشرط لا يعتبر ذلك الشرط في موضوع الغير أصلا؛ وذلك من جملة ما مرَّ ذكره عند تحقيق العدم والملكة في الفصل السابق فلا يعاد مرة أخرى. وكما أن الفرق متحقق بين كل واحد منها وبين الغير على سبيل العموم فكذلك بين كل واحد منها وبين الغير على سبيل الخصوص. أما بين المتضادين والمتناقضين فظاهر لما امتنع في المتناقضين أن يكون كل واحد منهما بالإيجاب، وقد كان من اللوازم أن يكون أحدهما بالسلب على ما عرف. وكذلك بينهما وبين المتضايفين. فإن المضاف لا يكون مضافًا إلا بالقياس إلى الغير على خلاف الضد. ولأن
As for the difference between relatives and the rest of the opposites, it is that each of the relatives is said with reference to another which accompanies it in existence and non-existence, but the other opposites are not like that. As for the difference between contraries and the rest, it is that contraries may have an intermediate to which both extremes move, and this intermediate cannot exist for the others. As for between privation/possession and the rest, the subject part in privation/possession, whether commonly known or real, is conditioned by a condition that is not considered in the subject of the other at all; this is among what was mentioned when investigating privation/possession in the previous chapter and will not be repeated. And just as the difference is realized between each one and the others in a general way, so too between each one and the others in a specific way. As for between contraries and contradictories, it is clear because it is impossible in contradictories for each of them to be affirmative, and one of them must be negative, as known. And similarly between them and relatives: for a relative is only relative by reference to another, unlike the contrary. And because...
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage distinguishes relatives (al-mutaḍā'ifayn) and contraries (al-mutaḍāddayn) from the other opposites, including each other. The author begins with relatives: each relative is defined by its relation to another, and that other necessarily accompanies it in existence and non-existence. For example, 'father' implies 'son'; if one exists, the other must exist, and if one is negated, the other is also negated. This is not the case for other opposites. Next, contraries can have an intermediate (like lukewarm between hot and cold) that both extremes can move towards; this intermediate does not exist for other opposites (e.g., contradictories have no intermediate). Then the author summarizes that privation/possession has already been distinguished in the previous chapter, noting its condition on the subject. Finally, specific differences are given: between contraries and contradictories, it is impossible for both contradictories to be affirmative; one must be negative. Between contraries and relatives, a relative is defined by relation to another, whereas a contrary is not (e.g., 'hot' is hot in itself, not relative to 'cold'). The passage ends with 'and because' (wala'anna), suggesting a continuation. The author uses these logical distinctions to help the student correctly classify propositions. Islamic science examples: 'blindness/sight' for privation, 'father/son' for relatives, 'heat/cold' for contraries.

Key Terms

المتضايفين
المتناقضين
العدمة والملكة
الواسطة

The Five Types of Utterance (Qawl) by Purpose
Author classifies utterances by purpose into five categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given Arabic statement into one of the five types of utterance (qawl) and justify your classification based on the speaker's purpose as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Every utterance reveals what is in the soul; its purpose determines whether it is for conception, affirmation (explicit or shifted), or to elicit a response.

This passage from the logic text presents a systematic classification of utterances based on their intended function. The author moves beyond the simplistic division into declarative, interrogative, and imperative, offering a five-category scheme rooted in the purpose of indication (dalala). Understanding this classification is essential for correctly interpreting the logical and legal force of statements in theological and juristic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching purpose of speech
The passage states that the purpose of speech (qawl) is to indicate what is in the soul. The first major division is whether this indication is intended for its own sake or for something else.
2
Distinguish indication for itself vs. for something else
If the indication is intended for its own sake (lizatiha), it falls into the first three categories. If it is intended for something else (lishay' akhar), it falls into the fourth and fifth categories.
3
Break down the three categories for itself (lizatiha)
First: utterance for conception (tasawwur), i.e., to grasp the essence (e.g., definitions). Second: utterance for explicit affirmation (tasdiq sarih), which can be true/false (e.g., 'The world is temporally originated'). Third: utterance for shifted affirmation (tasdiq muharrif), which implies a judgment indirectly (e.g., wishes, oaths).
The 'shifted' category is also called 'tanbih' and has the force of a declarative.
4
Break down the two categories for something else (lishay' akhar)
Fourth: utterance seeking another indication from the hearer (e.g., 'What is a pair?' — asks for essence; 'Make me understand' — asks for action of explaining). Fifth: utterance seeking an action from the hearer (e.g., commands, prohibitions).
The fourth category seeks either essence (istifham) or action (istifham ma' al-fi'l), while the fifth directly seeks an action.
5
Analyse the three modes of the fifth category (seeking action)
The passage subdivides the fifth category based on the speaker's attitude: with superiority (isti'la') gives command/prohibition; with supplication (tadarru') gives du'a; with equality (tasawi) gives iltimas. Note that prohibition is considered an action because abstention is an action the hearer can perform.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ب] ومنها ما يكون خبريا وهو الذي يكون قابلا للصدق والكذب بالفعل أي بالتصريح كقولنا العالم حادث مثلا. ت] ومنها ما لا يكون خبريا؛ بل في قوة الخبري، وذلك هو المحرف عنه في الحقيقة ويسمى تنبيها نحو التمني والترجي والقسم والنداء، فإنك إذا قلت ليتك تأتيني، أستفيد من هذا أنك مُريد لإتيانه. [ث] ومنها ما لا يكون خبريا ولا في قوة الخبري كذلك، وذلك على قسمين : مثل الاستفهام والاستعلام ومثل الأمر والنهي ونحو ذلك.
وأما بيان الحصر في هذه الأقسام فذلك أن الغرض من القول هو الدلالة على ما في النفس، والدلالة إما أن تراد لذاتها، وإما أن لا تراد لذاتها بل لشيء آخر يتوقع من الغير : والتي لذاتها فهي إما للتصور وهو الأول من الأقسام، وإما للتصديق وذلك إما على وجه وهو الثاني منها، وإما بالتحريف وهو الثالث. والتي تراد لشيء آخر فإما أن يكون ذلك الشيء دلالة أيضًا وهو الرابع، أو فعلا وهو الخامس. ويمكن أن يعبر هذا بعبارة أخرى كما يقال تركيب الألفاظ واستعمالا لتعريف المعاني المدلول عليها بحسب لغة لغة: وذلك إما لتصور ماهية الشيء كما هي أو لتمييزه عن الغير وهو الأول، أو لا للتصور فقط بل للحكم على ذلك الشيء إما صريحًا وهو الثاني، أو لا صريحًا بل محرفًا عن التصريح وهو الثالث، أو لا للتصور ولا للحكم كذلك لا صريحًا ومحرفًا بل لطلب الشيء عن الغير وهو الرابع والمطلوب فيه إما نفس الماهية وهو الاستفهام أو الفعل الذي يصدر عنه. وذلك إن كان بطريق الاستعلاء فهو . الأمر والنهي بهذا فظهر الفرق بين قولنا ما الزوج وبين قولنا أفهمني ماهية الزوج؛ إذ الأول طلب الماهية والثاني طلب الفعل وهو إفهام تلك الماهية. [و] وأما الفرق بين العلو والاستعلاء، فظاهر لما أن الاستعلاء قد يوجد / من السفلة. وإن كان بطريق التضرع والخضوع فهو الدعاء سواء كان ذلك بصيغة الأمر كقولنا تقبل منا صلاتنا، أو بصيغة النهي كقولنا لا تؤاخذنا إن نسينا : وإن بطريق التساوي فهو الالتماس بصيغة الأمر هو كقولك للغير من أمثالك أفعل كذا أو بصيغة النهي كقولك لا تفعل كذا، ولا يظن أن النهي لا يكون من هذا القبيل لما أنه بطريق الامتناع عن الفعل لا بطريق الفعل؛ إذ المعنى من الفعل هو صرف الممكن من الإمكان إلى الوجوب. وكما أن الإقدام على الفعل يشتمل عليه فكذلك الامتناع عنه. ولأن المطلوب من الغير ما يقدر عليه الغير. وما يقدر عليه الغير فهو فعله؛ والامتناع من جملة ما يقدر عليه وإلا لما يصح الأمر به والنهي عنه فهو فعله إذن.
[B] Among them is the declarative (khabariy), which is capable of truth and falsity actually, i.e., explicitly, like our saying 'The world is temporally originated' for example. [T] Among them is what is not declarative but has the potential of declarative, and this is actually what is shifted from it and is called 'tanbih' (indication) like wishing, hoping, oath, and call, for if you say 'Would that you came to me', it is understood from this that you desire his coming. [Th] Among them is what is not declarative nor has the potential of declarative, and that is of two types: like interrogation and inquiry, and like command and prohibition and the like.
As for the enumeration of these divisions, that is because the purpose of speech is to indicate what is in the soul, and indication is either intended for itself or not intended for itself but for something else expected from the other. That which is for itself is either for conception (tasawwur) which is the first of the divisions, or for affirmation (tasdiq) which is either in one way which is the second, or by shifting which is the third. That which is intended for something else is either that thing being also an indication which is the fourth, or an action which is the fifth. It can be expressed in another way: the composition of words and their usage to define the meanings indicated according to language by language: that is either for conceiving the essence of the thing as it is or to distinguish it from others, and that is the first; or not for conception alone but for ruling on that thing either explicitly which is the second, or not explicitly but shifted from explicitness which is the third; or not for conception nor for ruling, neither explicit nor shifted, but for seeking the thing from the other, which is the fourth, and what is sought therein is either the essence itself which is interrogation (istifham) or the action that emanates from him. If it is in the manner of superiority (isti'la'), then it is command and prohibition. Thus the difference between our saying 'What is the pair?' and our saying 'Make me understand the essence of the pair' becomes clear; the first is a request for essence, and the second is a request for action, which is making understand that essence. [W] As for the difference between 'uluw (highness) and isti'la' (seeking superiority), it is apparent because isti'la' can be found from the lowly. If it is in the manner of supplication and humility, then it is supplication (du'a), whether it is in the form of command like 'Accept our prayer' or in the form of prohibition like 'Do not punish us if we forget'. If it is in the manner of equality, then it is entreaty (iltimas) in the form of command like you say to your peer 'Do this' or in the form of prohibition like 'Do not do this'. And do not think that prohibition is not of this kind because it is in the manner of abstention from action not in the manner of action; for the meaning of action is to turn the possible from possibility to obligation. Just as undertaking an action includes it, so does abstaining from it. And because what is demanded from the other is what the other is capable of. And what the other is capable of is his action; and abstention is among what he is capable of, otherwise it would not be correct to command and forbid it, so it is his action then.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage categorizes utterances (qawl) according to their purpose and function. The author first presents a preliminary division: some utterances are declarative (khabariy), capable of truth and falsity; some are non-declarative but have the force of declarative, like wishes, oaths, etc.; and some are neither declarative nor having its force, like questions and commands. Then he provides the comprehensive enumeration (hasr). The purpose of speech is to indicate what is in the soul. This indication is either intended for its own sake (lizatiha) or for something else (lishay' akhar). Intended for its own sake can be for conception (tasawwur) or for affirmation (tasdiq). Affirmation can be explicit or shifted (muharrif). The shifted includes things like wishes. Intended for something else can be for another indication (like seeking a definition) or for an action (like command). The author then gives an alternative expression. Finally, he elaborates on the fifth category: seeking something from another. This seeking is divided into three modes: with superiority (isti'la') leading to command/prohibition; with supplication (tadarru') leading to du'a; and with equality (tasawi) leading to iltimas. He clarifies that prohibition is also an action because it is an abstention, and action includes both undertaking and abstaining. This classification is foundational for understanding the different functions of speech in logic.

The Self-Evident Nature of Categorical Propositions
Author introduces the second type (categorical propositions), stating the declarative sentence is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a proposition (qadiyya) and explain why it is considered self-evident in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The proposition (qadiyya) is the building block of logic, and its truth-bearing nature is intuitively known.

In this passage, the author turns to categorical propositions (hamliyyat), which are the most basic units of logical reasoning. He asserts that the declarative compound (al-khabari) is called a proposition (qadiyya), and that its fundamental characteristic—being either true or false—is self-evident to every rational person. This means you do not need a formal definition to recognise a proposition; you intuitively know it when you hear one. This lesson will help you grasp this foundational concept and see why it matters for all logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term
Find the term 'qadiyya' in the Arabic text. The author defines it as a declarative compound (al-khabari min al-murakkabat). What does 'declarative' mean in contrast to other types of speech?
2
Understand the claim of self-evidence
The author says the proposition is 'mustaghni 'an al-ta'rif' (needless of definition). This means the concept is so basic that it does not require a formal definition; it is known by default.
3
Recognise the intuitive judgment
The author states that everyone judges a proposition as either 'mawjud' (existent/true) or 'ghayr mawjud' (non-existent/false) by an intuitive (badihi) judgment. This intuition is the foundation of logical truth.
4
Contrast with the impossibility of a particular proposition being self-evident
The author notes it is impossible for a specific declarative compound to be self-evident in itself. Why? Because while the general concept of a proposition is self-evident, the truth or falsity of a particular proposition may require evidence.
5
Relate to later logic
Understanding that propositions are self-evident prevents circular definitions. In later logical operations, such as syllogisms, we rely on the intuitive recognition of propositions to build arguments without redefining the basic unit each time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني : في الحمليات واعلم أولا بأن الخبري من المركبات يسمى قضية وأنه من حيث هو هو غني عن التعريف ، فإن كل أحد يحكم بأنه موجود وليس بمعدوم حكما بديهيا، ومن المحال أن يكون واحد من المركبات الخبرية بديهيا بعينه والمركب الخبري
The second type: on categorical propositions. Know first that the declarative type of compound expressions is called a proposition (qadiyya), and that it, in itself, is needless of definition, for everyone judges that it is existent and not non-existent by an intuitive judgment. And it is impossible that one of the declarative compounds be intuitive in its very self, and the declarative compound...
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new section on categorical propositions (hamliyyat). The author states that the declarative compound (murakkab khabari) is called a proposition (qadiyya). He asserts that this kind of expression is intrinsically needless of definition, because every person intuitively judges that it is either existent or non-existent—that is, true or false. This is a self-evident (badihi) judgment. The author then notes the impossibility that a specific declarative compound could be self-evident in itself, while the general category of declarative compound is being discussed. The text cuts off, but the point is to establish the foundational nature of propositions as the building blocks of logical reasoning, requiring no prior definition.

Key Terms
قضية (qadiyya): A declarative sentence (compound) that can be true or false, also called a proposition.
خبري (khabari): A type of compound expression that is declarative (as opposed to imperative or interrogative), forming the basis of a proposition.
بديهي (badihi): Self-evident or intuitive; known without need for definition or proof.

Key Takeaway
A proposition (qadiyya) is a declarative compound that is inherently known as true or false, requiring no definition because of its intuitive self-evidence.
