The Nature of the Assertive Statement (al-Qawl al-Jāzim)
Defining the assertive statement and its truth-condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the assertive statement and identify its essential characteristics from a logical passage.

Core Idea
The assertive statement is the basic unit of truth-valuation in logic.

Every argument is built from propositions. But what exactly counts as a proposition in Islamic logic? The author begins by defining the declarative compound (al-murakkab al-khabarī) as an 'assertive statement' (qawl jāzim) that is either true or false. This definition is the gateway to understanding all subsequent logical operations. Let's dissect it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic. The author starts with 'ثم المركب الخبري يسمى قولاً جازما' – the declarative compound is called an assertive statement. So the definiendum is the declarative compound (al-murakkab al-khabarī).
2
Extract the defining property
What makes a statement assertive? The author says: 'يقال لجميع ما هو صادق أو كاذب' – it is said of everything that is true or false. So the defining characteristic is truth-aptness.
3
Note the exclusive scope
The author stresses that other types of statements (like commands or questions) are not called assertive, just as they are not called true or false. This exclusivity is key.
4
Confirm the internal structure
An assertive statement 'يحكم فيه بنسبة معنى إلى معنى إما بإيجاب أو بسلب' – it judges a relation of a meaning to a meaning via affirmation or negation. So every qawl jāzim contains two meanings and a judgment linking them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المركب الخبري يسمى قولاً جازما. والقول الجازم يقال لجميع ما هو صادق أو كاذب ولا يقال على غيره من الأقوال إنه جازم كما لا يقال إنه صادق أو كاذب. والقول الجازم يحكم فيه بنسبة معنى إلى معنى إما بإيجاب أو بسلب فيكون على ثلاثة أقسام؛ وذلك لأن كل واحد من المعنيين لا يخلو من أن يكون خاليًا عن هذه النسبة وهي التي بها يكون القول قولًا جازمًا أو لا يكون. فإن كان خاليا فهو الحملي كقولنا الإنسان حيوان مثلا، وإن لم يكن خاليًا بل يكون مشتملا عليها فهو الشرطي؛ وإنه إما يحكم فيه باللزوم وهو الشرطي المتصل كقولنا إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود: يحكم فيه بإيجاب نسبة الاتصال بين قولنا الشمس طالعة وبين قولنا النهار موجود؛ وهذا هو إيجاب اللزوم بينهما على معنى أنه لا يمكن أن يوجد الأول منهما بدون الثاني البتة أو لا يحكم فيه باللزوم بل يحكم فيه بالعناد، وهو الشرطي المنفصل كقولنا إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجودًا؛ يحكم فيه بإيجاب نسبة الانفصال بين قولنا الشمس طالعة وبين قولنا الليل موجود؛ وهذا هو إيجاب العناد بينهما على معنى أنه يمكن أن يوجد الأول منهما مع الثاني البتة. ثم التركيب في أجزاء كل واحد من القولين وهما المقدم والتالي في الشرطي المتصل والشرطي المنفصل ظاهر، وكذلك النسبة وهي النسبة التي بها يصير القول قولاً جازما كما مر. وإن كان كل واحد من القولين في الشرطيين لا يسمى قولاً جازماً لما أن المقترن به من حروف الشرط يخرجه عن أن يكون كما كان من قبل. ولو سمي بهذا الاسم لا يكون خطأ وقد كان كل واحد منهما قولاً جازما في نفس الأمر. وإن قال سلمنا أن كل واحد من المقدم والتالي في الشرطيات مشتمل على التركيب والنسبة التي مرَّ ذكرها لكن لم قلتم بأن كل واحد من الموضوع والمحمول في الحمليات غير مشتمل عليهما، فنقول الموضوع من حيث هو الموضوع لا يكون مشتملا عليهما أصلًا؛ وكذلك المحمول من حيث هو المحمول على خلاف المقدم والتالي؛ ولأن كل واحد من الموضوع والمحمول قد يجيء مفردًا والمفرد لا يمكن أن يكون مشتملا على التركيب والنسبة. ولو كان كذلك لكان كل واحد من الموضوع والمحمول خاليًا عن التركيب والنسبة. ثم التركيب في الحملي قد يكون من المفردين كقولنا الإنسان حيوان، وقد يكون من المركبين كقولنا الحيوان الناطق جسم حساس متحرك بالإرادة، وقد يكون من المختلفين؛ وذلك على قسمين: كما في قولنا الحيوان جسم حساس متحرك بالإرادة، وعلى العكس كقولنا الجسم المتحرك بالإرادة حيوان. ولو كان كذلك لكان من الممكن أن يكون فيه مفرد يقوم مقامه لفظ مركب ويكون فيه مركب يقوم مقامه لفظ مفرد والمركب قد يكون مركبا يجري فيه الصدق والكذب، وقد يكون مركبًا لا يجري فيه الصدق والكذب كما مر لكنه إذا أخذ فقد أخذ من حيث إنه جملة يمكن أن يدل عليه لفظ مفرد.
Then the declarative compound is called an assertive statement. The assertive statement is said of everything that is true or false, and it is not said of other statements that they are assertive, just as it is not said that they are true or false. The assertive statement judges a relation of a meaning to a meaning, either by affirmation or negation, and thus it is of three divisions. That is because each of the two meanings is either free from this relation (which is that by which the statement becomes assertive) or not. If it is free, then it is the categorical, like our saying 'Man is an animal' for example. If it is not free but includes it, then it is the conditional. And it either judges by implication, which is the conjunctive conditional, like our saying 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists': it judges by affirming the relation of connection between our saying 'the sun is rising' and our saying 'daytime exists'; this is affirming implication between them, meaning that it is impossible for the first to exist without the second at all. Or it does not judge by implication but judges by opposition, which is the disjunctive conditional, like our saying 'Either the sun is rising or night exists': it judges by affirming the relation of separation between our saying 'the sun is rising' and our saying 'night exists'; this is affirming opposition between them, meaning that it is possible for the first to exist with the second at all. Then composition in the parts of each of the two statements—namely the antecedent and consequent in the conjunctive conditional and the disjunctive conditional—is apparent, as is the relation which makes the statement assertive as mentioned. However, each of the two statements in conditionals is not called an assertive statement because the conditional particles attached to it remove it from being as it was before. But if it were called by this name, it would not be an error, and each of them was an assertive statement in itself. If one says: 'We grant that each of the antecedent and consequent in conditionals includes composition and the relation mentioned, but why do you say that each of the subject and predicate in categoricals does not include them?' We say: The subject as such does not include them at all; likewise the predicate as such, unlike the antecedent and consequent. And because each of the subject and predicate may come as a single term, and a single term cannot include composition and relation. If that were so, then each of the subject and predicate would be free from composition and relation. Then composition in the categorical may be from two single terms, like our saying 'Man is an animal'; or from two compound terms, like our saying 'The rational animal is a body that is sensitive and moving by will'; or from two different ones, and that is of two kinds: as in our saying 'The animal is a body that is sensitive and moving by will', and conversely like our saying 'The body moving by will is an animal'. If that were so, then it would be possible that in it a single term could be replaced by a compound expression, and a compound could be replaced by a single expression. And a compound may be a compound in which truth and falsity apply, or a compound in which truth and falsity do not apply, as mentioned, but when it is taken, it is taken as a sentence that can be indicated by a single expression.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the types of assertive statements (qawl jāzim). The author begins by defining the declarative compound (al-murakkab al-khabarī) as an assertive statement, which is characterized by being either true or false. He then specifies that the assertive statement predicates a relation of one meaning to another via affirmation (ījāb) or negation (salab). The division into three types arises from the nature of the two meanings involved: if the meanings are free from the relation, the statement is categorical (ḥamlī), like 'Man is an animal'—here 'man' and 'animal' are related directly without any conditional connective. If the meanings are not free but include the relation, the statement is conditional (sharṭī). Conditional statements are further of two types: conjunctive (muttasil), where implication (luzūm) is affirmed, e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists'—meaning the first cannot exist without the second; and disjunctive (munfaṣil), where opposition (ʿinād) is affirmed, e.g., 'Either the sun is rising or night exists'—meaning the first can exist together with the second (i.e., they are incompatible in a particular sense). The author then discusses the composition of parts: in conditionals, the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī) are clearly composite and each has the relation that makes a statement assertive. Yet they are not called assertive statements because the conditional particles alter their status; however, this is not a crucial error since they originally were assertive. A potential objection is raised: the antecedent and consequent in conditionals have composition and relation, but why do the subject and predicate in categoricals not have them? The author responds that the subject as such is not composed, and similarly the predicate; they may be single terms, which cannot include composition and relation. Finally, he notes that composition in categoricals can be from simple terms (both single), from compound terms (both compound), or from mixed (one simple, one compound). This shows the flexibility of logical expression, where a single term can represent a compound idea and vice versa. The entire discussion provides a precise classification of propositions foundational to Islamic logic, using examples from everyday knowledge. The Islamic example 'Man is an animal' is used, which is a standard logical example in the tradition.

المركب الخبري

An assertive statement is any declarative compound that can be truthfully assessed as true or false, consisting of a judgment between two meanings.

Division into Categorical and Conditional Propositions
Dividing assertive statements into three types based on the relation of the two meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish categorical (hamli) from conditional (sharti) propositions and identify the two subtypes of conditionals: conjunctive (muttasil) and disjunctive (munfasil).

Core Idea
A proposition is either categorical (direct relation) or conditional (implication or opposition).

After defining the assertive statement, the author divides it based on whether the two meanings are free from the relation of judgment or not. This yields three types: categorical, conjunctive conditional, and disjunctive conditional. Understanding this division is crucial for analyzing arguments, as each type requires different logical treatment. Let's walk through the criteria and examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division criterion
The author says: 'كل واحد من المعنيين لا يخلو من أن يكون خاليًا عن هذه النسبة... أو لا يكون' – each of the two meanings is either free from the relation or not. If free, it's categorical; if not free (i.e., includes the relation), it's conditional.
The 'relation' here is the judgment of affirmation or negation that makes the statement assertive.
2
Identify the categorical type (hamli)
If the meanings are free from the relation, the statement is hamli. Example: 'الإنسان حيوان' (Man is an animal). Here 'man' and 'animal' are directly related without a conditional particle.
3
Identify the conditional type (sharti) and its first subtype
If the meanings are not free, it is sharti. Then, if the judgment is one of implication (luzūm), it is muttasil (conjunctive). Example: 'إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود' (If the sun is rising, then daytime exists). The relation is that the first cannot exist without the second.
4
Identify the second subtype of conditional
If the judgment is one of opposition (ʿinād), it is munfasil (disjunctive). Example: 'إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجودا' (Either the sun is rising or night exists). Here the two parts are opposed; the author notes that it is possible for the first to exist with the second ('يمكن أن يوجد الأول منهما مع الثاني البتة'), which may mean they are incompatible in a specific sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المركب الخبري يسمى قولاً جازما. والقول الجازم يقال لجميع ما هو صادق أو كاذب ولا يقال على غيره من الأقوال إنه جازم كما لا يقال إنه صادق أو كاذب. والقول الجازم يحكم فيه بنسبة معنى إلى معنى إما بإيجاب أو بسلب فيكون على ثلاثة أقسام؛ وذلك لأن كل واحد من المعنيين لا يخلو من أن يكون خاليًا عن هذه النسبة وهي التي بها يكون القول قولًا جازمًا أو لا يكون. فإن كان خاليا فهو الحملي كقولنا الإنسان حيوان مثلا، وإن لم يكن خاليًا بل يكون مشتملا عليها فهو الشرطي؛ وإنه إما يحكم فيه باللزوم وهو الشرطي المتصل كقولنا إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود: يحكم فيه بإيجاب نسبة الاتصال بين قولنا الشمس طالعة وبين قولنا النهار موجود؛ وهذا هو إيجاب اللزوم بينهما على معنى أنه لا يمكن أن يوجد الأول منهما بدون الثاني البتة أو لا يحكم فيه باللزوم بل يحكم فيه بالعناد، وهو الشرطي المنفصل كقولنا إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجودًا؛ يحكم فيه بإيجاب نسبة الانفصال بين قولنا الشمس طالعة وبين قولنا الليل موجود؛ وهذا هو إيجاب العناد بينهما على معنى أنه يمكن أن يوجد الأول منهما مع الثاني البتة. ثم التركيب في أجزاء كل واحد من القولين وهما المقدم والتالي في الشرطي المتصل والشرطي المنفصل ظاهر، وكذلك النسبة وهي النسبة التي بها يصير القول قولاً جازما كما مر. وإن كان كل واحد من القولين في الشرطيين لا يسمى قولاً جازماً لما أن المقترن به من حروف الشرط يخرجه عن أن يكون كما كان من قبل. ولو سمي بهذا الاسم لا يكون خطأ وقد كان كل واحد منهما قولاً جازما في نفس الأمر. وإن قال سلمنا أن كل واحد من المقدم والتالي في الشرطيات مشتمل على التركيب والنسبة التي مرَّ ذكرها لكن لم قلتم بأن كل واحد من الموضوع والمحمول في الحمليات غير مشتمل عليهما، فنقول الموضوع من حيث هو الموضوع لا يكون مشتملا عليهما أصلًا؛ وكذلك المحمول من حيث هو المحمول على خلاف المقدم والتالي؛ ولأن كل واحد من الموضوع والمحمول قد يجيء مفردًا والمفرد لا يمكن أن يكون مشتملا على التركيب والنسبة. ولو كان كذلك لكان كل واحد من الموضوع والمحمول خاليًا عن التركيب والنسبة. ثم التركيب في الحملي قد يكون من المفردين كقولنا الإنسان حيوان، وقد يكون من المركبين كقولنا الحيوان الناطق جسم حساس متحرك بالإرادة، وقد يكون من المختلفين؛ وذلك على قسمين: كما في قولنا الحيوان جسم حساس متحرك بالإرادة، وعلى العكس كقولنا الجسم المتحرك بالإرادة حيوان. ولو كان كذلك لكان من الممكن أن يكون فيه مفرد يقوم مقامه لفظ مركب ويكون فيه مركب يقوم مقامه لفظ مفرد والمركب قد يكون مركبا يجري فيه الصدق والكذب، وقد يكون مركبًا لا يجري فيه الصدق والكذب كما مر لكنه إذا أخذ فقد أخذ من حيث إنه جملة يمكن أن يدل عليه لفظ مفرد.
Then the declarative compound is called an assertive statement. Distinguishing Conditional from Categorical Propositions
Author explains compositional differences between conditional and categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditional and categorical propositions by analyzing their compositional structure and the logical status of their parts.

Core Idea
Conditionals assert a connection between parts; categoricals assert a predication between subject and predicate.

In this passage, the author explains the fundamental difference between conditional propositions (shartiyya) and categorical propositions (hamliyya). While both types have parts, the nature of the composition and the judgment differ. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing arguments in Islamic logic, especially in syllogisms where conditionals play a role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of each proposition type
Recognize that conditionals consist of an antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali), while categoricals consist of a subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul). The author notes that the composition of parts in conditionals is 'apparent' (zahir).
The antecedent and consequent in conditionals are themselves assertoric statements when isolated, but conditional particles modify their status.
2
Analyze the author's response to the objection
Consider the objection: why don't subject and predicate have composition and relation like antecedent and consequent? The author replies that subject and predicate, as such, are simple terms; even if compound, they are treated as a single unit. This differs from the parts of conditionals, which retain their internal structure.
In categoricals, a compound like 'the rational animal' is considered as a single subject.
3
Compare the type of judgment
In categoricals, the judgment is 'this is that' (predication). In conditionals, the judgment is 'this is necessary for that', 'this is consequent to that', or 'this is contrary/opposite to that'.
The conditional particle (ḥarf al-sharṭ) transforms the relationship from predication to implication or opposition.
4
Test the logical independence of parts
The author emphasizes that the truth of a conditional does not entail the truth of its parts, nor does its falsity entail the falsity of its parts. This is not true for categoricals, where the truth of the proposition depends on the truth of the predication.
This feature is essential for conditional syllogisms, such as modus ponens and modus tollens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم التركيب في أجزاء كل واحد من القولين وهما المقدم والتالي في الشرطي المتصل والشرطي المنفصل ظاهر، وكذلك النسبة وهي النسبة التي بها يصير القول قولاً جازما كما مر . وإن كان كل واحد من القولين في الشرطيين لا يسمى قولاً جازماً لما أن المقترن به من حروف الشرط يخرجه عن أن يكون كما كان من قبل. ولو سمي بهذا الاسم لا يكون خطأ وقد كان كل واحد منهما قولاً جازما في نفس الأمر. وإن قال سلمنا أن كل واحد من المقدم والتالي في الشرطيات مشتمل على التركيب والنسبة التي مرَّ ذكرها لكن لم قلتم بأن كل واحد من الموضوع والمحمول في الحمليات غير مشتمل عليهما، فنقول الموضوع من حيث هو الموضوع لا يكون مشتملا عليهما أصلًا؛ وكذلك المحمول من حيث هو المحمول على خلاف المقدم والتالي؛ ولأن كل واحد من الموضوع والمحمول قد يجيء مفردًا والمفرد لا يمكن أن يكون مشتملا على التركيب والنسبة . ولو كان كذلك لكان كل واحد من الموضوع والمحمول خاليًا عن التركيب والنسبة . ثم التركيب في الحملي قد يكون من المفردين كقولنا الإنسان حيوان، وقد يكون من المركبين كقولنا الحيوان الناطق جسم حساس متحرك بالإرادة، وقد يكون من المختلفين؛ وذلك على قسمين : كما في قولنا الحيوان جسم حساس متحرك بالإرادة، وعلى العكس كقولنا الجسم المتحرك بالإرادة حيوان. ولو كان كذلك لكان من الممكن أن يكون فيه مفرد يقوم مقامه لفظ مركب ويكون فيه مركب يقوم مقامه لفظ مفرد والمركب قد يكون مركبا يجري فيه الصدق والكذب، وقد يكون مركبًا لا يجري فيه الصدق والكذب كما مر لكنه إذا أخذ فقد أخذ من حيث إنه جملة يمكن أن يدل عليه لفظ مفرد. والمعتبر هو وحدته لا تفصيله حتى إذا قلت الإنسان يمشي قضية لا يلتفت إلى حال الإنسان وحال حمل المشي عليه الجملة التي تجوز أن تسمى قضية ولا يراد أن يحكم في أجزائها بالنسبة الإيجابية والسلبية. وإن كان يتفق في البعض أن يكون في الجزء منه إيجاب أو سلب بل يجعل مثل هذا التأليف كشيء واحد؛ ويحكم بأن هذا ذلك لا بشرط أن يكون معناه معنى ذلك بل بشرط أن يكون معنى هذا ومعنى ذلك يجتمعان في محل واحد كما في قولنا الإنسان حيوان مثلا؛ وهذا من اللوازم في الحملي على خلاف الشرطي فإنه لا يحكم فيه بأن هذا ذلك بل يحكم فيه بأن هذا لازم لذلك وتاليه أو معاند له ومنافيه؛ وإنه أي الشرطي بالحقيقة من القضايا التي يجري فيها الصدق والكذب وإنما هو قضية واحدة بارتباط البعض منها بالبعض من حروف الشرط وإلحاقه بالبعض منها لما لحقه به فقد حرفه عن أن يكون قضية يجري فيها الصدق والكذب؛ ولذلك لا يلزم من صدق الشرطية صدق أجزائها ولا من الكذب كذبها كذلك.
Then the composition in the parts of each of the two statements—namely the antecedent and the consequent in the conditional conjunctive and the conditional disjunctive—is apparent, and likewise the relation, which is the relation by which the statement becomes an assertoric statement as previously mentioned. Even though each of the two statements in the conditionals is not called an assertoric statement because the conditional particle connected to it removes it from being as it was before. And if it were called by this name it would not be an error, and each of them was an assertoric statement in itself. And if one says: we concede that each of the antecedent and consequent in conditionals contains the composition and relation mentioned, but why do you say that each of the subject and predicate in categoricals does not contain them? We say: the subject insofar as it is subject does not contain them at all; likewise the predicate insofar as it is predicate, unlike the antecedent and consequent. And because each of the subject and predicate may come as a simple term, and a simple term cannot contain composition and relation. If it were so, each of the subject and predicate would be devoid of composition and relation. Then composition in the categorical may be from two simples, as in our statement 'Man is an animal'; or from two compounds, as in our statement 'The rational animal is a sentient body moving by will'; or from different types; and that in two ways: as in our statement 'Animal is a sentient body moving by will', and conversely as in our statement 'The body moving by will is an animal'. If it were so, it would be possible that in it a simple term be replaced by a compound expression, and a compound be replaced by a simple expression. And the compound may be a compound in which truth and falsity occur, or a compound in which truth and falsity do not occur as previously mentioned, but when it is taken it is taken insofar as it is a sentence that can be indicated by a simple expression. What is considered is its unity, not its details, such that when you say 'Man walks' as a proposition, you do not pay attention to the state of man and the state of the attribution of walking to him, the sentence that may be called a proposition, and it is not intended to judge regarding its parts with respect to affirmative or negative relation. Even if it sometimes happens that in some parts there is affirmation or negation, rather such a composition is treated as a single thing; and it is judged that this is that, not on condition that its meaning is the meaning of that, but on condition that the meaning of this and the meaning of that come together in one location, as in our statement 'Man is an animal' for example. And this is among the necessary features in the categorical, unlike the conditional, for in it it is not judged that this is that, but rather it is judged that this is necessary for that, or its consequent, or contrary to it or its opposite. And it (i.e. the conditional) in reality is among the propositions in which truth and falsity occur, but it is a single proposition through the connection of some parts to others by conditional particles, and its attachment to some parts due to what attaches to it has altered it from being a proposition in which truth and falsity occur; therefore, from the truth of the conditional, the truth of its parts does not follow, nor from its falsity does the falsity of its parts follow likewise.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytical discussion distinguishing conditional propositions (shartiyya) from categorical ones (hamliyya) in terms of their composition and the logical status of their parts. The author begins by noting that the composition of parts—antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in conditionals—is clear, as is the relational aspect that makes a statement assertoric (qawl jazim). Although each part of a conditional, taken alone, would be an assertoric statement in itself, the presence of conditional particles removes that status, so they are not called assertoric statements. However, calling them such would not be an error. The author then addresses an objection: if the parts of conditionals have composition and relation, why do subject and predicate in categoricals not have them? The response is that subject and predicate, as such, do not contain composition and relation because they can be simple terms; a simple term cannot have composition. Categoricals can have compounds as subject or predicate, but the composition is considered as a unity. The author illustrates with examples from the Islamic sciences: 'Man is an animal' is a simple categorical; 'The rational animal is a sentient body moving by will' has a compound subject and predicate. The key difference is that in categoricals, the judgment is that 'this is that' (i.e., identity or predication), while in conditionals, the judgment is that 'this is necessary for that', or 'this is consequent to that', or 'this is contrary or opposite to that'. The author emphasizes that a conditional is a single proposition formed by connecting its parts with conditional particles, but it does not follow that if the conditional is true, its parts are true individually, nor if false, its parts are false. This underscores the logical independence of parts in conditionals, unlike categoricals where subject and predicate are integrally related.

شرطي منفصل
قول جازم

A) Categoricals judge 'this is necessary for that'; conditionals judge 'this is that'.
B) Categoricals judge 'this is that'; conditionals judge 'this is necessary for that', 'this is consequent to that', or 'this is contrary/opposite to that'.
C) Both judge 'this is that'.
D) Neither makes a judgment.

Key Takeaway
The essential difference is that categoricals predicate identity (this is that), while conditionals assert a necessary connection, consequence, or opposition between parts.

Priority of Categoricals and Affirmative over Negative
Author argues for priority of categoricals and affirmative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why categorical propositions are considered primary over conditionals and why affirmative propositions precede negative ones in the logical order.

Core Idea
The simple is prior; the affirmative is prior because the negative is merely its removal.

In this passage, the author establishes the foundational ordering of proposition types in Islamic logic. He argues that before tackling conditional propositions, we must first understand the simple categorical, and within categoricals, we start with the affirmative. This hierarchy is not arbitrary but stems from the logical structure: the affirmative has a positive relation of existence, while the negative is the absence of that relation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two levels of priority
Notice that the author first gives categorical propositions priority over conditionals, then within categoricals gives affirmative priority over negative. These are two distinct claims.
The reasoning for each is based on simplicity and dependency.
2
Understand the reasoning for categorical priority
The author says categoricals are composed from simples, and composition from simples takes precedence over composition from non-simples (i.e., conditionals). This is a principle that simpler structures are prior.
3
Analyze the reasoning for affirmative priority
The affirmative consists of subject, predicate, and a relation of existence. The negative consists of the same but with the removal of that relation. Thus the negative is dependent on the affirmative as its absence; therefore the affirmative is prior.
The negative is described as an 'accident' upon the affirmative.

And the exhaustive discussion of conditional propositions will come later; for it is necessary that the discussion begins with the simple assertoric statement, which is the categorical, for it may be composed from simples, and it may be composed from what is in the ruling of simples as has been known, and composition from simples takes precedence over composition from non-simples. So we give precedence to it, then we say: The categorical, its simplest is the affirmative, then the negative. Thus the affirmative is given precedence; that is because it is composed of that to which something is attributed, which is the subject, and that which is attributed, which is the predicate, upon a relation of existence. As for the negative, it is composed of that to which something is attributed and that which is attributed and the removal of the existence of the relation, so it is an accident upon the affirmative and a remover of it because it is its absence. Then it necessarily follows that it is dependent on
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage provides a definitional argument for the priority of categorical propositions over conditionals, and within categoricals, the priority of affirmative over negative. The author states that a full treatment of conditionals will come later; it is necessary to begin with the simple assertoric statement, which is the categorical (hamliyya). The categorical can be composed from simples (e.g., simple terms) or from what is considered as simples. Since composition from simples is prior, we start with the categorical. Within categoricals, the simplest form is the affirmative (mujab), then the negative (salib). The affirmative is given priority because it consists of the subject (mansub ilayhi), the predicate (mansub), and a relation of existence (nisbat wujud). The negative consists of the same components but with the removal (raf') of the existence of the relation. Thus the negative is an accident ('arid) upon the affirmative, removing it, because it is the absence ('adam) of the affirmative. Therefore, the negative depends on the affirmative. An example from fiqh: 'Zakat is obligatory' (affirmative) is prior to 'Zakat is not obligatory' (negative); the negative is understood as a denial of the affirmative relation.

موجب
سالب

D) Because the conditional is more complex.

Key Takeaway
The priority of the affirmative over the negative is based on the latter being a removal of the affirmative relation, making it logically dependent.

Priority of Affirmation over Negation in Categorical Propositions
Author explains affirmation's priority and negation's dependence on affirmation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why affirmation is prior to negation and demonstrate how negation depends on affirmation using definitions from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Negation is the absence of affirmation; it cannot be conceived without first understanding affirmation.

This passage from the logic text makes a foundational claim: affirmation is simpler than negation. The author argues that affirmation is a positive composition of subject and predicate with the existence of the relation, while negation is simply the removal of that relation. Therefore, negation is secondary and dependent. To illustrate, he compares it to the relationship between sight and blindness. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows its significance for Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the types of propositions
The author distinguishes categorical (حملي) from conditional (شرطي). He postpones conditionals and focuses on categoricals because they are simpler. Identify this move and note why categoricals are given priority.
The categorical asserts 'this is that' directly.
2
Define affirmation and negation as used here
Affirmation (إيجاب) is the judgment that the predicate belongs to the subject by the existence of the relation. Negation (سلب) is the judgment that removes that existence. Write these definitions in your own words.
3
Analyze the dependence claim
The author states that negation is incidental to affirmation and removes it because it is its lack. Therefore, negation cannot be known except through affirmation. Find the sentence where he says this.
Look for: 'يَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُفْتَقِرًا فِي التَّصَوُّرِ إِلَى الْإِيجَابِ'.
4
Examine the analogy of sight and blindness
Blindness is defined as the absence of sight in something capable of seeing. Sight is part of the definition of blindness, not part of blindness itself. Similarly, affirmation is part of the definition of negation. Apply this to the example: 'Zaid is not knowledgeable' – the affirmation 'he is knowledgeable' is not actually in the negation, but it is used to understand it.
The author warns against thinking affirmation coexists with negation in reality.
5
Reflect on the nobleness question
The author concludes that neither affirmation nor negation is inherently nobler; it depends on the subject matter. Understand why this is consistent with the logical priority of affirmation.
Priority in conceptual dependence does not imply value superiority.

It is its unity, not its detail, so when you say 'Man walks,' it is a proposition without considering the state of man and the state of carrying walking upon him - the sentence that may be called a proposition is not intended to judge its parts regarding affirmative and negative relation. Although it may happen in some that there is affirmation or negation in a part, rather such composition is made as one thing; and it is judged that this is that, not with the condition that its meaning is the meaning of that, but with the condition that the meaning of this and the meaning of that come together in one place, as in our saying 'Man is an animal' for example. And this is from the necessities in the categorical, unlike the conditional, for in it it is not judged that this is that, but rather it is judged that this is necessary for that and its consequent, or opposing and contrary to it; and that it - i.e. the conditional in truth - is among propositions in which truth and falsehood occur, but it is one proposition by the connection of some of it with others by conditional particles, and attaching some of it to others because of what attaches to it has distorted it from being a proposition in which truth and falsehood occur; and therefore, the truth of the conditional does not necessitate the truth of its parts, nor does the falsehood of it necessitate the falsehood of them likewise. The exhaustive discussion of conditional propositions will come to you later; since it is necessary that the discussion commence with the simple decisive statement, which is the categorical, for it may be composed from simples or from what is in the status of simples as known, and composition from simples is given priority over composition from non-simples. So we give it priority, then we say: The categorical, its simplest is the affirmative, then the negative. Thus affirmation is given priority; that is because it is composed of that which is attributed to - the subject, and that which is attributed - the predicate, upon the existence of the relation. As for negation, it is from that which is attributed to and that which is attributed and the removal of the existence of the relation, so it is incidental to affirmation and removing it because it is its lack. Then it necessarily follows that it is dependent in conception upon affirmation, in the sense that it is not known except through it, unlike affirmation. And it should not be thought that if it is incidental to affirmation, that affirmation is in it or with it, for it is impossible for affirmation to exist with negation. Rather, the thing which if isolated would be affirmation, exists in the definition of negation, just as it is said that sight exists in the definition of blindness, not meaning that sight exists with blindness, but meaning that blindness is not known except by mentioning the absence of sight. So it is said that it is an expression for the absence of sight in what is capable of seeing, thus sight is a part of what defines it, not a part of it. Similarly, affirmation is mentioned in the definition of negation, so it becomes a part of what defines it, not a part of it. And the meaning which is negated exists in the wording of negation, even if it does not exist in the essences of things. If you said 'Zaid is not knowledgeable,' then your saying 'he is knowledgeable' is an affirmation about Zaid, were it not for the particle of negation. Thus negation is something that removes the meaning which is affirmation, so affirmation is with it in conception even if it is not included in it either in potentiality or actuality. And it does not necessitate that affirmation and negation come together: do you not see that the even exists in the odd and the odd in the even likewise? And it does not necessitate that the even be odd and the odd be even, such that one thing is even and odd; for the even is a part of the odd and the odd is a part of the even, not identically, nor that a single thing is described by both. And they are among that whose existence is impossible otherwise, that is, that a thing be a part of a thing or a part of what defines it as has passed. And it is not that if a thing is a part of what defines it, that this is identically that; but rather it necessitates that it be with it in the mind. And regarding what has been said about whether affirmation is nobler or negation: the truth is that this is not nobler than that in terms of itself, nor that of this likewise, but rather it differs according to the subject matter if by being nobler is meant being more inclusive of benefit, unless something else is intended, and that is among that upon which judgment is suspended until it is learned.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is an extended exposition on the nature of categorical and conditional propositions, and specifically on the priority of affirmation over negation. The author begins by clarifying that a conditional proposition, though composed of two parts connected by a conditional particle, is considered a single proposition for the purpose of truth and falsity. However, unlike the categorical proposition where the judgment is that 'this is that' (e.g., 'Man is an animal'), the conditional judges a relation of consequence or opposition. The author then announces that the detailed discussion of conditionals will come later, and that we must first deal with the simpler categorical proposition. The categorical is simpler, and its simplest form is the affirmative, then the negative. Affirmation is given priority because it is a composition of subject and predicate with the existence of the relation. Negation, on the other hand, is the removal of that relation, so it is incidental to affirmation and removes it, being its lack. Therefore, negation depends on affirmation for its conception: we can only understand negation by first understanding affirmation. The author uses the analogy of sight and blindness: blindness is defined as the absence of sight in something that normally sees. Sight is a part of the definition of blindness, not part of blindness itself. Similarly, affirmation is a part of the definition of negation. To illustrate, he gives the example: 'Zaid is not knowledgeable' — the phrase 'he is knowledgeable' is an affirmation that is negated. The negation does not contain affirmation actually, but the affirmation is present in the mind as part of the conception of negation. This does not entail that affirmation and negation coexist in reality, just as the even is part of the definition of the odd, but they do not coexist in one subject. Finally, the author addresses the question of whether affirmation or negation is nobler. He concludes that it is not absolute; it depends on the context and what is intended. The judgment on this matter requires further learning.

Matter and Form of Categorical Propositions
Author defines the matter and form of categorical propositions and the language used to express them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul) as the matter of a categorical proposition and distinguish them from the relation (nisba) as the form.

Core Idea
A proposition is not just words; it is matter (subject/predicate) structured by form (relation).

In this passage, the author defines the essential components of any categorical proposition. He explains that every categorical proposition consists of three elements: subject, predicate, and the relation between them. Understanding these components is foundational for analysing syllogisms and evaluating definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
Find where the author lists the three elements that make up a categorical proposition. Note the Arabic terms: mawdu', mahmul, and nisba.
2
Distinguish matter from form
The author calls subject and predicate the 'matter' (madda) and the relation the 'form' (sura). Why does he call the relation a form? Because it determines the quality (affirmation/negation) and structure of the proposition.
3
Check the distinctness of the relation
The author argues that the relation is distinct from both subject and predicate. If it were not, you wouldn't be able to conceive the terms independently. Test this: take 'Zayd is standing' – can you conceive Zayd without the standing relation? Yes. Can you conceive standing without Zayd? Yes. So the relation is separate.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the proposition 'The world is temporally originated' (al-'alimu hadithun). Identify the subject (world), predicate (temporally originated), and the relation of affirmation between them.

فصل: في تعريف حال القول الجازم الذي هو أول والذي ليس بأول أما القول الجازم حمليّاً كان أو شرطيا فإنه يفتقر في اللغة اليونانية إلى استعمال الكلمات الوجودية، وهي التي تدل على نسبة وزمان من غير أن يتحصل فيها المعنى المنسوب إلى الموضوع الغير المعين إلا إذا كان الموضوع نفسه كلمة. وإنه لا يكون من اللوازم في اللغة العربية إلا في الشرطيات: إما في المتصلات فإنك تقول إذا كان وكلما كان ومتى كان، وإما في المنفصلات فتقول إما أن يكون كذا وإما أن يكون كذا. فأما في الحمليـات فلا يجب أن يكون كذلك بل الذي يجب فيها هو الذي يكون من الأركان؛ وذلك هو والصورة. أما المادة فهي إما الموضوع أو المحمول، وأما الصورة فهي النسبة بينهما وإنها مغايرة لكل واحد منهما وإلا لا يمكن تعقل كل واحد منهما مع دخول عنها؛ فهذه الثلاثة التي يتركب منها ما يكون من الحمليات أية قضية كانت. ولا يستراب في أن اجتماع هذه المعاني في الذهن فردًا فردًا لا يكون موضوعًا ومحمولًا ونسبة يتم بها القضية في الذهن بل يفتقر أن يعتقد الذهن تعلق البعض منها بالبعض إيجابية كانت النسبة أو سلبية؛ إذ الشيء من حيث هو الشيء غير الشيء من حيث كونه موضوعًا للشيء أو محمولاً على الشيء لما أن الشيء قد تعلم وإن لم تعلم ذلك. ثم اللفظ إنما كان ليدل على ما في الضمير فيحاكي به ما في الضمير وإلا لا يدل على ما في الضمير كما هو هو في الضمير فيتضمن ثلاث دلالات دلالة على الموضوع ودلالة على المحمول ودلالة على العلاقة والارتباط بينهما. والذي يدل على هذه العلاقة لا يكون من اللوازم بل يترك اعتمادًا على ما يدل عليه لفظيًا كان ذلك كما في قولنا الإنسان كاتب، أو معنويا كما
Chapter: On defining the state of the assertive statement (qawl jazim) which is primary and which is not primary. As for the assertive statement, whether categorical or conditional, it requires in the Greek language the use of existential words (kalimat wujudiyya), which indicate a relation and tense without incorporating the meaning attributed to the subject unless the subject itself is a word. That [i.e., the existential word] is not among necessary [elements] in the Arabic language except in conditionals: in conjunctive conditionals you say 'if it is' and 'whenever it is' and 'when it is', and in disjunctive conditionals you say 'either it is thus or it is thus'. As for categoricals, it is not necessary to have that; rather what is necessary in them is what is from the pillars (arkan); that is [the matter] and the form. As for matter, it is either the subject or the predicate; and as for form, it is the relation (nisba) between them, and it is distinct from each of them, otherwise it would not be possible to conceive each of them with the entry of the other?; these are the three from which any categorical proposition is composed. There is no doubt that the mere collection of these meanings in the mind individually does not constitute a subject, predicate, and relation by which the proposition is completed in the mind; rather, the mind must believe in the attachment of some to others, whether the relation is affirmative or negative. For a thing in itself is different from a thing as being a subject for something or a predicate of something, because a thing may be known even if you do not know that. Then, speech is only used to indicate what is in the mind, so it imitates what is in the mind; otherwise it does not indicate what is in the mind as it is in the mind. So it includes three significations: signification of the subject, signification of the predicate, and signification of the connection and relation between them. What indicates this relation is not among necessary [elements]; rather it is left depending on what indicates it, whether that is literal (lafzi) as in our saying 'al-insanu katibun' (man is a writer), or conceptual (ma'nawi) as...
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of the assertive statement (qawl jazim), specifically focusing on categorical propositions (qadaya hamliyya). The author begins by distinguishing between the Greek language and Arabic regarding the need for a copula (existential word) expressing relation and tense. In Greek, such a word is required; in Arabic, it is only required in conditional propositions (both conjunctive and disjunctive), not in categoricals. For categoricals, the essential components (arkan) are matter and form. Matter is the subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul); form is the relation (nisba) between them. The relation is distinct from both subject and predicate, otherwise the proposition would not be intelligible. Three elements - subject, predicate, and relation - compose any categorical proposition. However, merely having these meanings in the mind individually does not constitute a proposition; the mind must actively judge that the predicate is attached to the subject (affirmatively or negatively). The author emphasizes that a thing in itself differs from it being considered as a subject or predicate; one can know a thing without knowing its role as subject or predicate. Finally, speech (lafz) must imitate the mental content and thus conveys three significations: subject, predicate, and the relation/connection. The indication of the relation is not necessarily a separate word; it can be literal (lafzi) like the copula in 'al-insanu katibun' (man is a writer) where the relation is understood from the construct, or conceptual (ma'nawi) without a specific word. This discussion clarifies the necessary components of a categorical proposition and the linguistic means of expressing them, particularly in the Arabic logical tradition.

Key Terms
مادة
صورة
نسبة
موضوع
محمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what are the three components of any categorical proposition?
A) Subject, predicate, and quantity
B) Subject, predicate, and relation
C) Subject, verb, and object
D) Matter, form, and copula

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition is composed of matter (subject and predicate) and form (the relation between them).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Wine is forbidden' (al-khamru haramun) from fiqh. Identify its subject, predicate, and the type of relation (affirmative or negative).
?
Construct a new categorical proposition on a fiqh topic and label its matter and form.

The Necessity of Mental Judgment in Propositions
Author defines the matter and form of categorical propositions and the language used to express them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a mere collection of terms in the mind does not constitute a proposition, and why the mind must judge the attachment between subject and predicate.

Core Idea
Without mental judgment, words are just a list, not a proposition.

The author makes a subtle but crucial point: simply having the concepts of subject, predicate, and relation present in the mind is not enough to form a proposition. The mind must actively believe (i'tiqad) that the predicate is attached to the subject, either affirmatively or negatively. This act of judgment is what makes the proposition a truth-bearing statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about mental collection
Find the sentence: 'There is no doubt that the mere collection of these meanings in the mind individually does not constitute a subject, predicate, and relation by which the proposition is completed in the mind.' Notice the word 'individually'.
2
Understand the need for judgment (i'tiqad)
The author says the mind must 'believe in the attachment of some to others'. This is the act of judgment – affirming or denying the predicate of the subject.
3
Test with an example
Consider the terms 'Zayd' and 'standing' and the relation of affirmation. Even if you have all three in mind, you don't have a proposition until you judge that Zayd is actually standing. If you merely think of them separately, it's not an assertion.

Linguistic Expression of Propositional Components: The Role of Existential Words
Author defines the matter and form of categorical propositions and the language used to express them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the requirement for existential words (copula) in Greek versus Arabic for categorical propositions, and identify how Arabic expresses the relation between subject and predicate.

Core Idea
Arabic does not need a copula for categoricals; the relation is understood from the structure.

The author draws an interesting contrast between Greek and Arabic. In Greek, a categorical proposition requires an existential word (kalima wujudiyya) that indicates both the relation and tense. In Arabic, such a word is only necessary in conditional propositions. For Arabic categoricals, the relation is signified either literally (lafzi) through word order or conceptually (ma'nawi) without a separate word. This highlights the flexibility of Arabic in expressing logical relations.

Guided Path
1
Find the contrast between Greek and Arabic
The author says Greek requires existential words; Arabic does not except in conditionals. Identify the sentence that makes this distinction.
2
Note where Arabic does require existential words
In conditional propositions: 'if it is' (in kana), 'whenever it is' (kullama kana), 'either it is' (imma an yakuna). These are the copula-like words in conditionals.
3
Understand how Arabic categoricals express the relation without a word
The author gives an example: 'al-insanu katibun' (man is a writer). There is no explicit 'is' word; the relation is understood from the juxtaposition of subject and predicate in the nominal sentence.
4
Distinguish between lafzi and ma'nawi indication
The relation can be indicated literally (like in 'al-insanu katibun' where the case endings or word order show relation) or conceptually (without any explicit marker, purely from context).

The Copula and the Unity of the Categorical Proposition
Author defines the copula and explains how it makes a categorical proposition one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the copula in a categorical proposition and determine whether the proposition is truly one using the criteria from the source.

Core Idea
The copula is the tool that turns two concepts into a single judgment.

In logic, a categorical proposition is more than just a subject and a predicate placed together. There must be a relation that unites them into a single assertion. This relation is called the nisba, and the word that indicates it is the copula (rabitah). In this lesson, we will examine what the copula is, how it can be explicit or implicit, and what conditions make a proposition truly one—a crucial skill for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the proposition's core parts
Identify the subject and predicate in any categorical proposition. For example, in ‘al-insanu hayawan’ (Man is an animal), the subject is ‘man’ and the predicate is ‘animal’. The copula may be absent in Arabic.
2
Determine if the copula is present or implied
Check if there is an explicit word like ‘huwa’ or ‘kana’ indicating the relation. In ‘Zayd huwa hayy’ (Zayd is alive), ‘huwa’ is the copula. In ‘Zayd hayy’, it is implied. Understand that the Arabic language often omits it, relying on mental awareness.
3
Understand the copula's function as a tool
Recognize that the copula is not part of the subject or predicate; it is a tool word (adawat) indicating the relation. It can appear as a noun (e.g., ‘huwa’) or a verb (e.g., ‘kana’).
4
Test the unity of the proposition
Check if the subject and predicate are each one in meaning. For a proposition to be one, the subject must be one subject and the predicate one predicate. For example, ‘al-‘aqlu jawharun’ (Reason is a substance) is one. But ‘al-‘aynu jismun’ (The ‘ayn is a body) meaning multiple things (sun’s eye, sight’s eye, water source) is not one proposition; it is multiple. Understand the author’s reasoning: the predicate ‘body’ is applied to each distinct meaning separately, so it is not a single connection.
5
Apply the rule to Quranic examples
Note that Arabic uses temporal copulas like ‘kana’ for timeless relations, as in ‘Wa kana Allahu ghafuran raheema’ (And Allah is Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful). This shows the copula’s flexibility in indicating a relation not bounded by time.

في قولنا الإنسان حيوان واللفظ قد يكون مفردًا مخصوصا بها كهو في قولنا زيد هو حيوان، وقد لا يكون. فإن من التركيب ما يكون هيئة دالة على ارتباط البعض بالبعض ارتباط حمل ووضع كما في الحمليات، أو ارتباط تقييد بعض ببعض كما في الحدود نفس تلو البعض منها للبعض. وإن لم يكن دالا على حال أحدهما عند الآخر فقد وجد عند الاجتماع ما يدل على ذلك. وإن قلت إن ذلك معنوي لا لفظي لما أن اللفظ لا ينفرد بذلك المعنى؛ فلا نزاع فيه؛ إذ الغرض أن يكون هنا معنى غير معنى الموضوع والمحمول من حقه أن يدل عليه وهو النسبة. فاللفظ الدالة علي النسبة تسمى رابطة؛ وحكمها حكم الأدوات كما في الوجوديات من الألفاظ وإنها قد تحذف والحذف هو الغالب في لغة العرب / اعتمادًا على شعور الذهن بمعناها، وقد يذكر. وذلك إما في قالب الاسم كما في قولك زيد هو حي فإن لفظ هو لم تُذكر لتدل بنفسها بل لتدل على أن زيدًا هو شيء لم يذكر؛ ولهذا كانت في حكم الأدوات، وإما في قالب الكلمة كما في قولك زيد كان عالما : وهذا هو الغالب في لغة العرب كذلك حتى إنهم استعملوا الألفاظ الدالة على نسبة وزمــان في الدلالة على نسبة غير زمانية مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَكَانَ اللهُ غَفُورًا رَحِيمًا ﴾ [النساء : ٩٦] في الدلالة على نسبة غير مختصة بزمان مخصوص، بل ذائع في أي زمان كان مثل قولهم: كل ثلاثة فإنها تكون فردًا». ولما كانت الرابطة مصرحًا بها أو مضمرًا هي التي تحدث من الكثرة وحدة كانت القضية وهي القول الجازم واحدة أما في الحملية فإنها تدل على ربط واحد وذلك أن تقول إن الموضوع هو المحمول وإنه إنما يكون واحدًا إذا كان الموضوع واحدًا من حيث هو الموضوع. والمحمول كذلك سواء كان كل واحد منهما واحدًا لفظا ومعنى كقولك العقل جوهر، أو لا يكون كل واحد منهما واحدا بل الموضوع كما في قولك الجوهر شيء قابل للأضداد أو المحمول كما في قولك الشيء القابل للأضداد جوهر. ولا يظن أن قول القائل العين جسم مريدا به عين الشمس وعين البصر وعين الماء قضية واحدة وإن صح قوله كما هو مذهب البعض بل كثيرة؛ إذ الجسم يحمل على هذا بعينه وعلى ذلك؛ وذلك كذلك. والحق فيه أنه لا يصح لأن ما أريد به فلا يكون مسماه ولا من لوازم مسماه أيضًا. اللهم إلا أن يقال كل واحد منها مسماه وقد أريد به ذلك، فيقال إنه في حيز المنع؛ إذ المسمى كل واحد منها مفترقا لا مجتمعًا.
In our saying 'Man is an animal', the word may be a specific single word like 'he' in our saying 'Zayd is an animal', or it may not be. For among compounds, there is what is a form indicating the connection of some parts to others with a connection of predication and positing, as in categorical propositions, or a connection of qualifying some parts by others, as in definitions where the parts are subsequent to each other. And if it does not indicate the state of one of them in relation to the other, then upon combination there is found what indicates that. If you say that that is conceptual (ma'nawi) not verbal, because the word alone does not have that meaning, then there is no dispute; for the purpose is that there be here a meaning other than the meaning of subject and predicate which it is proper to indicate, and that is the relation (nisba). So the word indicating the relation is called a copula (rabitah); and its ruling is the ruling of tools (adawat), as in existential words, and it may be omitted, and omission is predominant in the Arabic language, relying on the mind's awareness of its meaning, and it may be mentioned. That is either in the form of a noun, as in your saying 'Zayd he is alive', for the word 'he' is not mentioned to indicate by itself but to indicate that Zayd is something not mentioned; hence it is in the ruling of tools; or in the form of a verb, as in your saying 'Zayd was knowledgeable'. And this is predominant in the Arabic language, to the extent that they used words indicating relation and time to indicate a non-temporal relation, such as His saying, the Exalted: 'And Allah is Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful' [Quran 4:96] to indicate a relation not specific to a particular time, but rather general in any time, like their saying: 'Every three is odd.' And since the copula, whether explicit or implicit, is what creates unity from multiplicity, the proposition (qadiyya) — which is the assertive statement — is one. As for the categorical proposition, it indicates a single connection, and that is that you say that the subject is the predicate, and it is only one when the subject is one in terms of being the subject, and the predicate similarly, whether each of them is one in word and meaning, as in your saying 'Reason is a substance', or each of them is not one, but the subject as in your saying 'Substance is something receptive of opposites', or the predicate as in your saying 'The thing receptive of opposites is a substance'. And let it not be thought that a person's saying 'The 'ayn is a body' meaning by it the eye of the sun, the eye of sight, and the water source, is a single proposition, even if his statement is correct, as is the opinion of some; rather it is multiple. For 'body' is predicated of this exact one and of that one, and so on. The truth in it is that it is not valid, because what is meant by it is neither its name nor a necessary concomitant of its name. Unless it is said that each one of them is its name and he intended that, then it is said that it is in the realm of denial, for the named each one of them is separate, not combined.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on the nature of the copula (rabitah) and the unity of the categorical proposition (hamliyya). The author begins by analyzing the structure of a compound expression like 'Man is an animal'. He notes that the word indicating the relation between subject and predicate may be a specific word like 'huwa' (he) or may be absent. He distinguishes between two types of compound: one that indicates a predication relation (as in categoricals) and one that indicates a qualification relation (as in definitions). The key point is that there is a meaning beyond the subject and predicate themselves, namely the relation (nisba), and the word that indicates this relation is called the rabitah (copula). The rabitah is considered a tool word (adawat) and is often omitted in Arabic, relying on the mind's understanding. The author gives examples: the pronoun 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa hayy' (Zayd is alive) and the past tense verb 'kana' in 'Zayd kana 'aliman' (Zayd was knowledgeable). He even notes that Arabic uses temporal words like 'kana' to indicate a non-temporal relation, citing the Quranic verse 'And Allah is Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful' to show that the relation is timeless. Then the author explains that the copula, whether explicit or implicit, creates unity from multiplicity, making the proposition one. For the categorical proposition, it indicates a single connection. The proposition is one only when subject and predicate are each one, whether in word and meaning (e.g., 'Reason is a substance') or when one of them is multiple in word but one in meaning (e.g., 'Substance is something receptive of opposites' where the predicate is a description, or 'The thing receptive of opposites is a substance' where the subject is a description). The author warns against a mistaken view: if someone says 'The 'ayn is a body' meaning by 'ayn' three different things (sun's eye, sight's eye, water source), it is not one proposition but many, because 'body' is predicated of each individually. The truth is that such a statement is not valid because what is intended is neither the name nor a necessary concomitant of the name; unless one claims that each is the name and he intended that, but then the names are separate, not combined. This section provides a rigorous foundation for understanding propositional unity, a crucial concept in logic.

Key Terms
رابطة (rabitah) – the copula, a tool word indicating the relation between subject and predicate
نسبة (nisbah) – the relation that unites subject and predicate into a judgment
حملیة (hamliyya) – a categorical proposition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the function of the copula (rabitah) in a categorical proposition?
A) It is part of the predicate.
B) It indicates the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate.
C) It makes the predicate definite.
D) It is always omitted in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
The copula is a tool that indicates the relation between subject and predicate, and a proposition is truly one only when its subject and predicate are each one in meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1) Find a categorical proposition in an Islamic theological text (e.g., 'The Quran is the word of Allah'). Identify the copula if explicit, and explain whether it is explicit or implicit. 2) Consider the proposition 'Al-salat is a pillar of Islam'. If someone uses 'al-salat' to mean both the ritual prayer and the act of blessing, is this a single proposition? Why or why not? Use the author's criteria.

The Unity of Conditional Propositions
Author explains how conditional propositions become one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a conditional proposition requires both parts to be complete and distinguish it from a categorical proposition.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition becomes a single proposition only when both parts are fully stated.

Conditional propositions like 'If the sun is rising' are incomplete until the consequent is mentioned. This lesson explores how conditionals form a single truth-evaluable proposition and why they differ from simple categoricals.

Guided Path
1
Identify an incomplete conditional
Consider the example 'If the sun is rising' – this cannot be judged true or false because the conditional is incomplete.
2
Add the completing part
When you add the consequent, e.g., 'then it is daytime', the conditional becomes a single proposition that admits truth and falsehood.
The author calls this 'what is concomitant or incompatible'.
3
Distinguish from categorical
Unlike a categorical proposition that directly predicates one thing of another, a conditional is a compound proposition that yields a single judgment only when fully stated.
The categorical is the primary type for simple assertion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الشرطية فكذلك لأنها وإن كانت متركبة من قضايا مختلفة يجري فيها الصدق والكذب؛ فالألفاظ المقترنة بالبعض منها تخرجها عن أن تكون قضية يجري فيها الصدق والكذب كما مر . فلو قلت إن كانت الشمس طالعة وسكت فلا يقال إنه صادق أو كاذب. وكذلك إذا قلت إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة. وأما إذا أردت عليه ما يتممه بأن تذكر ما يلازمه وما ينافيه؛ فحينئذ يحذف منهما قضية واحدة يجري فيها الصدق والكذب لكنها لا تكون من الأقوال الجازمة قولاً جازماً بسيطًا على الإطلاق بل ذلك هو الحملي من حيث إن يحمل الواحد فيه على الواحد كما مرّ من قبل فيدل على أن شيئًا موجود لشيء وهو الإيجاب، أو ليس بموجود له وهو السلب. وما قيل إن السلب حكم نفي شيء عن شيء فكأنه قيل إن السلب حكم بسلب شيء عن شيء لما أن النفي والسلب واحد. ولو قلت إن النفي أعم من السلب فإن قولنا لا إنسان هو نفي الإنسانية من غير نسبة إلى منفي عنه؛ ففيه نظر؛ إذ لا يعرف هذا إلا بالنقل أنه وضع بإزاء ما هو الأعم من ذلك وضعا أولا أو ثانيا بل الحق
And as for the conditional proposition, it is similar, because even though it is composed of different propositions that admit truth and falsehood, the words conjoined with some of them remove them from being a proposition that admits truth and falsehood, as has passed. So if you say 'If the sun is rising' and then stop, it is not said that it is true or false. Similarly, if you say 'Either the sun is rising'. But when you intend upon it what completes it, by mentioning what is concomitant and what is incompatible, then from them a single proposition emerges that admits truth and falsehood, but it is not from the assertive statements a simple assertive absolutely; rather that is the categorical, insofar as it predicates one thing of one thing, as has passed, and it indicates that something exists for something — that is affirmation (ijab), or does not exist for it — that is negation (salb). And what is said that negation (salb) is a judgment negating something from something, it is as if it were said that negation is a judgment of negating something from something, because nafy and salb are one. And if you say that nafy is more general than salb, for our saying 'No man' is a negation of humanity without attribution to what is negated from; then there is consideration; for this is not known except by transmission that it was placed as corresponding to what is more general than that, either primarily or secondarily. But the truth...
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion of conditional propositions (shartiyya) and the distinction between negation (nafy) and privation (salb). The author begins by stating that the conditional proposition is similar to the categorical in that it requires completion to be a proposition that can be true or false. He gives examples: saying 'If the sun is rising' and stopping does not yield a truth-evaluable statement; similarly 'Either the sun is rising' is incomplete. Only when the consequent (what is concomitant or incompatible) is added does a single proposition emerge that admits truth and falsehood. However, this proposition is not a simple assertive statement like the categorical; it is a compound that predicates one thing of one thing only in a derived sense. The categorical is the primary type that indicates affirmation (ijab) or negation (salb) directly. The author then addresses a common confusion: some equate salb (negation in the sense of a negative proposition) with nafy (negation in general). He reports a view that nafy is broader because it can negate without specifying what is negated from whom, as in 'No man' (la insan), which merely negates humanity without attributing it to anything. The author notes that this distinction is not known except through linguistic convention (naql) and that it is debated whether it is primary or secondary. The passage ends abruptly, indicating the argument is cut off. This section is important for understanding the nature of conditional propositions and the precise definitions of logical terms like salb and nafy.

Key Terms
شرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'If the sun is rising' not a complete proposition?
A) It lacks a subject.
B) It lacks the consequent that completes the condition.
C) It is always true.
D) It is a categorical proposition.

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition requires both antecedent and consequent to be a single truth-evaluable proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the incomplete statement 'If you are in doubt about the prayer hours...' Complete it so that it becomes a valid conditional proposition in a fiqh context.

The Distinction Between Salb and Nafy
Author distinguishes negation (nafy) from privation (salb).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between nafy and salb and evaluate the argument that nafy is more general.

Core Idea
Nafy is more general than salb because it can negate without attributing.

The terms salb and nafy both relate to negation, but some logicians argue they differ. This lesson examines the claim that nafy is broader, using the example 'la insan' (no man), and considers the author's doubt about this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of salb
Salb is defined as a judgment that negates something from something (e.g., 'Zayd is not writing'). It requires a subject and predicate.
The author mentions salb as another term for the negative proposition.
2
Examine the example of nafy
Consider 'la insan' (no man) – this negates humanity without attributing it to any subject. It is a pure negation.
The author reports that some say nafy is more general because of such examples.
3
Compare the scope
Nafy includes both salb (negation of a predicate from a subject) and absolute negation without a subject. Thus nafy is broader.
4
Assess the author's objection
The author questions whether this distinction is based on linguistic convention (naql) rather than logical necessity, implying it may not be a real logical difference.
The passage ends abruptly, suggesting the argument is incomplete.

Ambiguous Terms and the Unicity of Propositions
Explains that ambiguous terms yield multiple propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a proposition is actually multiple propositions due to ambiguous terms and explain why.

Core Idea
An ambiguous term does not yield a single truth-apt proposition.

In Islamic logic, a proposition must have a clear subject and predicate. When a word like 'ayn (eye/sun/water) is used ambiguously, a statement like 'al-ayn jism' is not one proposition but many. This lesson explores why this matters and how to spot it using a classical example.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 'ayn example
The word 'ayn can mean the eye of the sun, the organ of vision, or a spring of water. When someone says 'al-ayn jism' (the eye is a body), the predicate 'body' is attached to each different subject separately. Therefore, it is not a single proposition but multiple propositions, each with its own subject.
The author refutes the idea that such a statement is one proposition, even if the speaker intends all meanings simultaneously.
2
Analyze the refutation
The author argues that what is intended by the ambiguous term is neither its actual referent nor a necessary concomitant. If one claims each meaning is intended, then each referent is separate, not combined, so the statement cannot be a single proposition.
This shows the importance of univocity in logical discourse.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
In Islamic law, the word 'zakat' can mean both obligatory charity and voluntary charity. If someone says 'Zakat is obligatory', the proposition is actually multiple: one about obligatory zakat (true) and one about voluntary zakat (false).
This demonstrates the practical relevance of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد منهما واحدا بل الموضوع كما في قولك الجوهر شيء قابل للأضداد أو المحمول كما في قولك الشيء القابل للأضداد جوهر. ولا يظن أن قول القائل العين جسم مريدا به عين الشمس وعين البصر وعين الماء قضية واحدة وإن صح قوله كما هو مذهب البعض بل كثيرة؛ إذ الجسم يحمل على هذا بعينه وعلى ذلك؛ وذلك كذلك. والحق فيه أنه لا يصح لأن ما أريد به فلا يكون مسماه ولا من لوازم مسماه أيضًا. اللهم إلا أن يقال كل واحد منها مسماه وقد أريد به ذلك، فيقال إنه في حيز المنع؛ إذ المسمى كل واحد منها مفترقا لا مجتمعًا. وأما في الشرطية فكذلك لأنها وإن كانت متركبة من قضايا مختلفة يجري فيها الصدق والكذب؛ فالألفاظ المقترنة بالبعض منها تخرجها عن أن تكون قضية يجري فيها الصدق والكذب كما مر . فلو قلت إن كانت الشمس طالعة وسكت فلا يقال إنه صادق أو كاذب. وكذلك إذا قلت إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة. وأما إذا أردت عليه ما يتممه بأن تذكر ما يلازمه وما ينافيه؛ فحينئذ يحذف منهما قضية واحدة يجري فيها الصدق والكذب لكنها لا تكون من الأقوال الجازمة قولاً جازماً بسيطًا على الإطلاق بل ذلك هو الحملي من حيث إن يحمل الواحد فيه على الواحد كما مرّ من قبل فيدل على أن شيئًا موجود لشيء وهو الإيجاب، أو ليس بموجود له وهو السلب. وما قيل إن السلب حكم نفي شيء عن شيء فكأنه قيل إن السلب حكم بسلب شيء عن شيء لما أن النفي والسلب واحد. ولو قلت إن النفي أعم من السلب فإن قولنا لا إنسان هو نفي الإنسانية من غير نسبة إلى منفي عنه؛ ففيه نظر؛ إذ لا يعرف هذا إلا بالنقل أنه وضع بإزاء ما هو الأعم من ذلك وضعا أولا أو ثانيا بل الحق فيه أن يقال هو الحكم بلا وجود شيء لشيء وما يوجبه موجب أي شيء كان فلا يتعذر أن يسلبه سالب. وبالعكس زمانياً كان أو غير زماني فيكون لكل إيجاب سلب يقابله، ولكل سلب إيجاب أيضًا؛ وهو التناقض أي التقابل بين الإيجاب والسلب بالحقيقة. وهذا من جملة ما يعرف من بعد على التحقيق إن شاء الله تعالى.
Neither of them is one, but the subject as in your saying 'substance is something receptive of opposites' or the predicate as in your saying 'the thing receptive of opposites is a substance'. And do not think that the statement of one who says 'the eye is a body' meaning by it the eye of the sun, the eye of vision, and the eye of water is a single proposition, even if his statement is valid according to some – rather it is many; for 'body' is predicated of this particular one and of that, and so on. The truth about it is that it is not valid, because what is intended by it is neither its actual referent nor a necessary concomitant of its referent. Unless it is said that each one of them is its referent and it is intended by that; then it is said that this is in the realm of impossibility, because the referent of each one of them is separate, not combined. As for the conditional, it is similar, because even though it is composed of different propositions in which truth and falsity occur, the particles conjoined with some of them remove them from being a proposition in which truth and falsity occur, as has passed. So if you say 'If the sun is rising' and stop, it is not said that it is true or false. Likewise if you say 'Either the sun is rising'. But if you intend to complete it by mentioning what necessitates it and what contradicts it, then a single proposition is abstracted from them in which truth and falsity occur – but it is not from among the categorical statements a simple categorical statement absolutely; rather that is the categorical in terms of predicating one thing of one thing, as has passed before, indicating that a thing exists for a thing – that is affirmation – or does not exist for it – that is negation. And what is said that negation is a judgment of denying a thing from a thing is as if it is said that negation is a judgment of negating a thing from a thing, because denial and negation are one. And if you say that denial is more general than negation, for our saying 'no man' is a denial of humanity without attribution to that which is denied from it – this requires consideration; for this is not known except through transmission that it is placed for what is more general than that, either primarily or secondarily. Rather the truth in it is to say that it is the judgment of non-existence of a thing for a thing, and whatever affirms an affirmer, any thing it may be, it is not impossible that a negator negate it. And vice versa, whether temporal or non-temporal. So for every affirmation there is a negation opposed to it, and for every negation there is also an affirmation; and that is contradiction, i.e., the opposition between affirmation and negation in reality. And this is among the things that will be known later upon verification, God willing.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage addresses two critical issues in propositional logic. First, it examines the unicity of propositions when terms are ambiguous. The author uses the example of the word 'ayn (عين), which can mean 'sun', 'eye', or 'spring of water'. When someone says 'al-ayn jism' (the eye is a body) intending all these meanings simultaneously, it is not a single proposition but multiple propositions, because the predicate 'body' is attached to each different subject separately. The author refutes the idea that such a statement is one proposition, emphasizing that the referent (musamma) of each meaning is distinct. Second, the discussion turns to conditional propositions. The author states that incomplete conditionals, like 'If the sun is rising' without the consequent, are not truth-apt. Only when the conditional is completed by mentioning the consequent (what follows or contradicts) does it become a single truth-apt proposition, though it remains different from a simple categorical (hamli) proposition. This leads to a definition of affirmation (ijab) as judgment that something exists for something, and negation (salab) as judgment that something does not exist for something. The author distinguishes between 'nafy' (denial) and 'salab' (negation), clarifying that negation is a judgment of non-existence with respect to a subject, while denial is simply saying 'no man' without attribution. The core conclusion is the law of contradiction: every affirmation has a corresponding negation, and vice versa, which is the real opposition between them.

Key Terms
قضية
عين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Islamic law, consider the word 'zakat' which can mean both the obligatory charity and the voluntary charity. If someone says 'Zakat is obligatory', is this a single proposition?
A) Yes, because the word 'zakat' has one meaning in context.
B) No, because the term is ambiguous between two different things, so it is multiple propositions.
C) Yes, because the predicate 'obligatory' applies to both meanings equally.
D) No, because the proposition is conditional.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous terms in propositions lead to multiple distinct propositions, each with its own truth value; a true proposition requires univocal terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the word 'riba' which can mean both pre-Islamic interest and certain modern financial transactions. Determine whether the statement 'Riba is haram' is one proposition or multiple, and explain your reasoning based on this lesson.

Propositions
beginner
