Conditional Propositions and the Need for Completion
Explains that incomplete conditionals are not truth-apt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a complete conditional proposition from an incomplete one and explain why an incomplete conditional is not truth-apt.

Core Idea
An incomplete conditional does not produce a truth-apt proposition.

The author discusses conditionals like 'If the sun is rising' without the consequent. Such a phrase is neither true nor false. Only when completed with the consequent does it become a single proposition. This lesson clarifies the structure of conditional propositions and their requirements.

Guided Path
1
Examine incomplete conditional
Consider 'If the sun is rising' without any consequent. This is an incomplete conditional. The particles like 'in' (if) require a completion to form a proposition. Without it, the expression has no truth value.
The same applies to disjunctive conditionals like 'Either the sun is rising'.
2
Identify the completion requirement
The conditional is completed by mentioning what follows from the antecedent (the consequent) or what contradicts it. Only then does a single proposition emerge that can be true or false.
This proposition is not a simple categorical (hamli) but a conditional one.
3
Apply to Islamic legal conditionals
In the Qur'an, conditional statements like 'If they come to you, judge between them' (Q 5:42) require the consequent to be a complete proposition. Without the consequent, no ruling can be derived.
This shows the practical importance of conditional completeness.

Key Takeaway

Defining Affirmation, Negation, and Contradiction
Defines affirmation, negation, and contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define affirmation (ijab), negation (salab), and contradiction (tanaqud) as used in Islamic logic and apply the law of contradiction.

Core Idea
Every affirmation has a corresponding negation, and this opposition is the basis of contradiction.

The passage concludes with the definition of affirmation as a judgment that a thing exists for a thing, negation as a judgment that a thing does not exist for a thing, and the law of contradiction: for every affirmation there is a negation and vice versa. These definitions are foundational for logical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Define affirmation (ijab)
Affirmation is the judgment that a thing exists for a thing, e.g., 'The world is created' asserts existence of creation for the world.
This is the basis of positive propositions.
2
Define negation (salab)
Negation is the judgment that a thing does not exist for a thing, e.g., 'The world is not eternal' denies eternity of the world.
The author distinguishes between nafy (denial without attribution) and salab (negation with attribution).
3
Understand the law of contradiction
For every affirmation, there is a corresponding negation that opposes it, and vice versa. This is termed 'tanaqud' (contradiction). It holds whether the proposition is temporal or non-temporal.
This principle is fundamental for establishing truth and falsity in arguments.
4
Apply to an Islamic theological proposition
Take the affirmation 'Allah is one' (tawhid). Its contradictory negation is 'Allah is not one'. These cannot both be true, and they cannot both be false simultaneously.
This shows the practical use of contradiction in kalam.

Source Text

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the contradictory negation of the proposition 'Salah is obligatory'?
A) 'Salah is not recommended'
B) 'Salah is not obligatory'
C) 'Salah is forbidden'
D) 'Salah is optional'

Key Takeaway
Affirmation and negation are judgments of existence and non-existence, respectively; they form a contradiction when directly opposed, a principle that holds for all propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write an affirmation about a ruling in fiqh, e.g., 'Salah is obligatory for every Muslim'. Then write its corresponding negation. Verify that these two are contradictories: they cannot both be true and cannot both be false.

Classification of Categorical Propositions by Quantity
Author classifies categorical propositions by quantity into three types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given categorical proposition is particular (makhsusa), unrestricted (muhmala), or restricted (mahsuura) by examining its subject and quantifier.

Core Idea
The quantity of a proposition depends on the subject type and presence of a quantifier.

In this passage, the author classifies categorical propositions based on the subject and the presence of a quantifier. Understanding these three types — makhsusa, muhmala, and mahsuura — is essential for correctly evaluating the truth conditions of propositions and for constructing valid syllogisms. Let's walk through the classification step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject type
Determine if the subject is a particular (e.g., Zayd) or a universal (e.g., man). This is the first key distinction.
Particulars refer to a specific individual; universals refer to a class.
2
If particular, label as makhsusa
If the subject is an individual, the proposition is makhsusa (also called shakhsiyya). It is true or false only for that individual.
Example: 'Zayd is a writer' is makhsusa.
3
If universal, check for quantifier
If the subject is universal, see whether a quantifier word (sor) like 'all', 'some', or 'none' is present. The quantifier determines the quantity.
No quantifier means the proposition is muhmala (unrestricted).
4
Classify accordingly
If no quantifier: it is muhmala and equivalent to a particular proposition. If a quantifier is present: it is mahsuura (restricted/quantified), which is either universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya).
Example: 'Every man is an animal' is universal mahsuura; 'Some man is a scholar' is particular mahsuura.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في تعريف أصناف القضايا المحصورة والمهملة والمخصوصة ولما كان الموضوع في القضية لا يخلو من أن يكون كليا أو جزئياً فالحكم كذلك لا يخلو من أن يكون على كلي أو على جزئي. فإن كان على جزئي كما في قولنا زيد كاتب، زيد ليس بكاتب والقضية تسمى مخصوصة حينئذ موجبة أو سالبة لما فيها من الحكم المخصوص بشخص واحد، وقد تسمى شخصية أيضًا؛ إذ الشخصي والجزئي في هذا الموضع بمعنى واحد. وإن كان على الكلي فذلك الكلي لا يخلو من أن يكون مجردًا عن السور والقضية تسمى مهملة حينئذ لما فيها من الإهمال موجبة كانت أو سالبة كما في قولنا الإنسان كاتب، الإنسان ليس بكاتب». وإنها في قوة الجزئية ليتوقف صدقها على صدق الجزئية لا غير. أو لا يكون مجرَّدًا والقضية تسمى محصورة لما فيها من الحصر في الحقيقة، وقد تسمّى مسورة أيضًا لاقترانها بكلمة السور موجبة كانت أو سالبة. والمسورة قد تكون كلية كقولنا «كل إنسان حيوان، لا واحد من الإنسان بفرس»، وقد تكون جزئية كقولنا بعض الإنسان عالم بعض الإنسان ليس بعالم. وأما لفظة السور فإنها في اللغة عبارة عما يكون شاملا للشيء، قال الشاعر: لَمَّا أَتَى خَبَرُ الزُّبَيْرِ تَوَاضَعَتْ سُوَرُ الْمَدِينَةِ وَالْجِبَالُ الْخُشَعُ
Chapter: On defining the types of restricted, unrestricted, and particular propositions. Since the subject in the proposition is either universal or particular, likewise the judgment is either about a universal or about a particular. If it is about a particular, as in our saying 'Zayd is a writer', 'Zayd is not a writer', then the proposition is called particular (makhsusah), whether affirmative or negative, because it contains a judgment specific to one individual. It is also called singular (shakhsiyya), for 'singular' and 'particular' in this context are synonymous. If it is about the universal, then that universal is either stripped of the quantifier – and the proposition is called unrestricted (muhmalah) because of the lack of restriction, whether affirmative or negative, as in our saying 'man is a writer', 'man is not a writer' – and it is equivalent to a particular proposition, in that its truth depends only on the truth of the particular proposition. Or it is not stripped, and the proposition is called restricted (mahsuurah) because of the actual restriction, and it is also called quantified (musawwarah) because it is accompanied by the word of quantification, whether affirmative or negative. The quantified may be universal, as in our saying 'every man is an animal', 'no man is a horse', and may be particular, as in our saying 'some man is a scholar', 'some man is not a scholar'. As for the word 'sor' (quantifier), in language it refers to that which encompasses something. The poet said: 'When the news of al-Zubayr came, the walls of the city and the humble mountains humbled themselves.'
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a classificatory definition that divides categorical propositions based on the quantity of the subject. The author first notes that the subject is either universal (kulli) or particular (juz'i). If the judgment is about a particular (individual), as in 'Zayd is a writer', the proposition is called 'makhsusah' (particular/singular). This type is also called 'shakhsiyya' (personal). If the judgment is about a universal subject, we have two subcases: either the universal is without a quantifier (sor) – then the proposition is 'muhmalah' (unrestricted), e.g., 'Man is a writer'. This is equivalent to a particular proposition (juz'iyya), meaning it is true if some man is a writer, not necessarily all. The other subcase is when the universal has a quantifier – then the proposition is 'mahsuurah' (restricted/quantified). The quantified may be universal (e.g., 'Every man is an animal') or particular (e.g., 'Some man is a scholar'). Finally, the author explains the term 'sor' (quantifier) linguistically as something that encompasses or surrounds, and cites a poetic verse about the walls (suwar) of the city to illustrate the meaning of encompassing. This classification is foundational for understanding the square of opposition and immediate inferences.

Checkpoint
In the proposition 'Al-'alim mutaqi' (The scholar is pious) without a quantifier, which type is it?
A) Makhsusa (particular/individual)
B) Muhmala (unrestricted)
C) Kulliyya (universal)
D) Juz'iyya (particular quantified)

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions are classified by quantity into particular (makhsusa), unrestricted (muhmala), and restricted (mahsuura); unrestricted propositions are treated as particular in terms of truth conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Given the proposition 'Zayd is a scholar', identify its type (makhsusa, muhmala, or mahsuura) and explain why, using the criteria from this lesson.
?
Task 2: Given the proposition 'The believer is truthful' (al-mu'min sadiq) without a quantifier, classify it and state its truth condition (i.e., under what circumstances it is true).

Classification of Propositions: Particular, Indefinite, and Quantified
Author defines three types of propositions based on quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between particular, indefinite, and quantified propositions in an Arabic logical text, and explain the logical force of each.

Core Idea
An indefinite proposition is logically equivalent to a particular, not a universal.

In this passage, the author classifies propositions based on whether the subject is particular or universal, and whether a quantifier is present. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly interpreting statements in Islamic scholarly texts, where quantifiers are not always explicit. We will learn to identify each type and understand their logical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject type
Notice that every proposition has a subject that is either particular (a specific individual) or universal (a general term). The author uses 'Zayd' as an example of a particular subject and 'al-insān' (man) as a universal subject.
A particular subject refers to one specific entity, while a universal subject refers to a class.
2
Classify by presence of quantifier
If the subject is universal and the proposition has no quantifier, it is indefinite (muhmala). If it has a quantifier (like 'kull' or 'ba‘ḍ'), it is quantified (maḥṣūra). If the subject is particular, it is called particular (makhṣūṣa) or singular.
Examples: 'Zayd is a writer' = particular; 'Man is a writer' (no quantifier) = indefinite; 'Every man is an animal' = quantified.
3
Understand the logical force of indefinite
The author emphasizes that an indefinite proposition is equivalent in force to a particular proposition: its truth depends only on the truth of a particular instance. Even if the universal is also true, the indefinite does not assert universality.
This is crucial: 'Man is a writer' does not mean 'All men are writers'; it only requires at least one man to be a writer.
4
Recognize the quantifier words
The passage lists the standard quantifier words: 'kull' for universal affirmative, 'lā shay'/ 'lā wāḥid' for universal negative, 'al-ba‘ḍ' for particular affirmative, and 'laysa kull'/ 'laysa ba‘ḍ'/ 'ba‘ḍ laysa' for particular negative.
Memorize these; they are the building blocks for categorical syllogisms in later lessons.

زيد كاتب، زيد ليس بكاتب والقضية تسمى مخصوصة حينئذ موجبة أو سالبة لما فيها من الحكم المخصوص بشخص واحد، وقد تسمى شخصية أيضًا؛ إذ الشخصي والجزئي في هذا الموضع بمعنى واحد. وإن كان على الكلي فذلك الكلي لا الْمَدِينَةِ وَالْجِبَالُ الْخُشَعُ. و في اصطلاح أهل المنطق عبارة عما يبين كمية للحكم، وذلك في الموجبة الكلية «كل»، وفي السالبة الكلية (لا شيء» و «لا واحد»، وفي الموجبة الجزئية «البعض»، وفي السالبة والكلي فكذلك على كل واحد كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان فإن فيه الحكم بالحيوانية على كل واحد من الإنسان وذلك من حيث إن هذا إنسان؛ وذلك كذلك فالمحكوم عليه وإن كان متحققا في كل واحد فهو واحد بالحقيقة وهو الإنسان. ثم الألف واللام في مثل قولنا الإنسان إنه وإن كان من جملة ما يقتضي العموم في لغة العرب فلا يلزم أن يكون كذلك وإلا فلا مهمل في لغة العرب؛ وقد كان فيها من المهملات لما أن المهمل من الأقوال ما Chapter: On defining the types of quantified, indefinite, and particular propositions. Since the subject in a proposition cannot but be either universal or particular, the judgment likewise cannot but be either upon a universal or a particular. If it is upon a particular, as in our saying 'Zayd is a writer', 'Zayd is not a writer', then the proposition is called particular (makhsūṣa), whether affirmative or negative, because of the judgment being particular to one individual, and it is also called singular; for the singular and the particular in this context are synonymous. If it is upon the universal, then that universal cannot but be either stripped of the quantifier, and the proposition is then called indefinite (muhmala) because of the omission (ihmāl), whether affirmative or negative, as in our saying 'Man is a writer', 'Man is not a writer'. And it is equivalent in force to the particular, for its truth depends on the truth of the particular alone. Or it is not stripped [of the quantifier], and the proposition is called quantified (maḥṣūra) because of the restriction (ḥaṣr) in reality, and it is also called quantified (musawwara) due to its being accompanied by a quantifier word, whether affirmative or negative. The quantified may be universal, as in our saying 'Every human is an animal', 'No human is a horse', and may be particular, as in our saying 'Some human is knowledgeable', 'Some human is not knowledgeable'. As for the word 'sūr' (quantifier), in language it denotes what encompasses something; the poet said: 'When the news of al-Zubayr came, the walls (suwar) of Medina and the humble mountains humbled themselves.' And in the terminology of logicians, it denotes what clarifies the quantity of the judgment. That is: for the universal affirmative, 'every'; for the universal negative, 'nothing' and 'no one'; for the particular affirmative, 'some'; for the particular negative, 'not every' or 'not some' or 'some not'. The affirmative is by affirming the judgment, not by affirming the subject, such that if you judge the existence of something for something, whether each of them is existent or non-existent, the proposition is affirmative. And just as the judgment can be upon the particular and the universal, so too it can be upon each individual, as in our saying 'Every human is an animal', for in it the judgment of animality is upon every individual human, inasmuch as this is a human; and that is so; thus the subject, even if it is realized in each individual, is one in reality, namely 'human'. Then the definite article (alif and lām) in expressions like our saying 'al-insān' (the human), even though it is among what implies generality in the Arabic language, it is not necessary that it be so; otherwise there would be no indefinite in Arabic. But there are indefinites in it, because an indefinite statement is one in which judgment is made about the nature that can be taken as universal or can be taken as particular. Hence it is necessary that the particular be true in the indefinite, with the possibility that the universal be true; so the universality of the subject, even if it does not entail the universality of the judgment, is not incompatible with it, so it is possible. And the particular is true on the two assumptions of its being universal and its not being universal. And do not think that the definite article in the noun is for definition of the genus or for definition of covenant; for just as it is for these, so it may be for other than them. That is in your saying 'al-insān' meaning 'human insofar as it is human', not 'human insofar as it is universal or particular or what is not universal or not particular'. For the definite article in such a form is for defining the essence itself. And the essence insofar as it is essence is something beyond universality, particularity, and other accidents. So 'al-insān' in our saying 'human insofar as it is human' is not...
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a definitional chapter on the classification of categorical propositions by quantity. The author begins by stating that the subject of any proposition is either universal or particular, and the judgment consequently applies either to a universal or a particular. If the judgment is about a particular individual, the proposition is called makhsūṣa (particular) or shakhṣiyya (singular). An example is 'Zayd is a writer'. If the judgment is about a universal, then either the proposition has no quantifier, in which case it is called muhmala (indefinite), e.g., 'Man is a writer', which in logical force is equivalent to a particular proposition, or it has a quantifier (sūr), in which case it is called maḥṣūra (quantified) or musawwara. Quantified propositions are either universal, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' (affirmative) and 'No human is a horse' (negative), or particular, e.g., 'Some human is knowledgeable' (affirmative) and 'Some human is not knowledgeable' (negative). The author then explains the term 'sūr' linguistically as something encompassing, citing a verse where it means the walls of a city, and terminologically as indicating the quantity of the judgment. The four quantifier words are listed: 'kull' for universal affirmative, 'lā shay' and 'lā wāḥid' for universal negative, 'al-ba‘ḍ' for particular affirmative, and 'laysa kull' or 'laysa ba‘ḍ' or 'ba‘ḍ laysa' for particular negative. The passage then clarifies that an affirmative proposition is defined by the affirmation of the judgment, not the existence of the subject; e.g., 'Zayd is not writing' can still be affirmative if it affirms the judgment of non-existence. Next, the author discusses the definite article in terms of generality: although 'alif-lām' suggests generality in Arabic, it does not always imply universality; otherwise there would be no indefinite propositions in Arabic. The indefinite proposition (muhmala) judges the nature that can be taken universally or particularly, so the particular is always true in the indefinite, while the universal may also be true. The definite article can be for defining the genus, for covenant, or for defining the essence (mahiyya) as in 'al-insān' meaning 'human qua human', which is beyond universality and particularity. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the discussion continues.

According to this passage, what is the logical status of an indefinite proposition (muhmala) such as 'Man is a writer'?
A) It is equivalent to the universal, because it lacks a quantifier.
B) It is equivalent to the particular, because its truth depends on the truth of a particular.
C) It is equivalent to the singular, because it refers to a specific individual.

Key Takeaway
The logical force of an indefinite proposition is particular, not universal; it requires only at least one instance to be true.

Propositions

5 min
Identifying Proposition Quantifiers and Understanding Affirmation
Author defines proposition and lists quantifiers with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier and affirmative/negative quality of any Islamic legal or theological proposition.

Core Idea
The quantifier tells you how many; the quality (affirmation/negation) is about the judgment, not existence.

This lesson introduces the logical definition of a proposition (qadiyya) and its four quantifiers. In Islamic sciences, propositions like 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' or 'Some believers are not sinful' are the building blocks of arguments. Understanding quantifiers and the nature of affirmation helps you parse rulings and theological claims precisely.

Guided Path
1
Find the logical definition
The author defines a proposition as 'an expression that clarifies the quantity of the judgment'. Identify the two key parts: 'expression' (the linguistic form) and 'clarifies the quantity of the judgment' (its logical function).
This definition focuses on quantity, not truth value.
2
List the four quantifiers
From the text, extract: universal affirmative 'كل', universal negative 'لا شيء' or 'لا واحد', particular affirmative 'البعض', particular negative 'ليس كل' or 'ليس بعض' or 'بعض ليس'. Memorize these as the standard quantifier words.
These quantifiers are used in all categorical propositions.
3
Understand the nuance of affirmation
The passage stresses that an affirmative proposition is defined by affirming the judgment, not by requiring the subject to exist. For example, 'Every phoenix is a bird' is affirmative even if no phoenix exists, because the judgment is affirmed.
This prevents confusion when dealing with non-existent subjects in theological discussions (e.g., 'Every partner of Allah is false').
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the proposition: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Identify: quantifier = 'every' (universal affirmative). It affirms the judgment of forbiddance for every intoxicant. The subject 'intoxicant' exists in reality, but the affirmation does not depend on that existence.
This example is from usul al-fiqh on the prohibition of khamr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و في اصطلاح أهل المنطق عبارة عما يبين كمية للحكم، وذلك في الموجبة الكلية «كل»، وفي السالبة الكلية «لا شيء» و «لا واحد»، وفي الموجبة الجزئية «البعض»، وفي السالبة الجزئية «ليس كل» أو «ليس بعض» أو «بعض ليس». والموجبة هي بإثبات الحكم لا بثبوت المحكوم عليه وبه حتى إذا حكمت بثبوت شيء لشيء وجوديا كان كل واحد منهما أو عدميا فالقضية موجبة. والحكم كما يكون على الجزئي والكلي فكذلك على كل واحد كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان فإن فيه الحكم بالحيوانية على كل واحد من الإنسان وذلك من حيث إن هذا إنسان؛ وذلك كذلك فالمحكوم عليه وإن كان متحققا في كل واحد فهو واحد بالحقيقة وهو الإنسان.
And in the terminology of the people of logic, it (the proposition) is an expression that clarifies the quantity of the judgment. That is: for the universal affirmative, 'every'; for the universal negative, 'nothing' and 'no one'; for the particular affirmative, 'some'; for the particular negative, 'not every' or 'not some' or 'some not'. The affirmative is by affirming the judgment, not by the existence of the subject of judgment; thus, if you judge the existence of something for something existentially, whether each of them is existential or non-existential, the proposition is affirmative. The judgment, just as it applies to the particular and the universal, also applies to each individual, as in our saying 'Every human is an animal', for in it there is a judgment of animality on every individual human, in that this is a human; and so likewise. Thus the subject of judgment, even if it is realized in each individual, is one in reality, and it is human.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) in logical terms and enumerates its quantifiers. It begins by stating that a proposition, in the terminology of logicians, is an expression that clarifies the quantity of a judgment—i.e., whether the judgment applies to all, some, or none of the subject. The quantifiers are then listed: 'every' for universal affirmative, 'nothing' and 'no one' for universal negative, 'some' for particular affirmative, and 'not every', 'not some', or 'some not' for particular negative. Next, the author clarifies that an affirmative proposition is defined by the affirmation of the judgment, not by the existential status of the subject. For example, if you affirm that something has a property, whether the subject or predicate is existential or non-existential, the proposition remains affirmative. The judgment applies to each individual of the subject, as in 'Every human is an animal', where the judgment of animality is made on every individual human. This shows that although the subject is realized in many individuals, it is one in reality (the universal human nature). In the Islamic sciences, similar examples abound: e.g., 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram)' is a universal affirmative proposition with the quantifier 'every'.

قضية
موجبة

Three Logical Uses of the Definite Article in Arabic
Author distinguishes three logical uses of the definite article.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three logical functions of the definite article (alif-lam) in Arabic propositions: definition of essence, genus, and covenant, and determine the proposition's quantity accordingly.

Core Idea
The definite article can indicate the essence, the whole genus, or a specific individual — each changes the proposition's logical quantity.

In Arabic logic, the definite article 'al-' is more versatile than it appears. Many students assume it always signals a universal judgment, but the great logicians showed that it can define the essence itself, the genus, or a previously known individual. This lesson teaches you to parse these three uses by examining the intended meaning of the proposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definite article
Find the definite article (al-) attached to the subject noun. This is the starting point.
2
Check the intended meaning
Ask: Is the judgment about the nature stripped of universality/particularity (e.g., 'human' as such)? If yes, the article defines the essence; the proposition is indefinite (muhmala).
Example: al-insan in 'al-insan min haythu huwa al-insan'
3
Test for universality
If the judgment applies to every individual of the kind, the article defines the genus; the proposition is universal. Example: 'al-insan natiq' meaning 'every human is rational'.
4
Test for specificity
If the judgment refers to a particular individual known from context, the article defines the covenant; the proposition is singular. Example: 'al-rajul salih' meaning 'the man (Zayd) is righteous'.
5
Summarize the quantity
The same phrase can have three different quantified propositions depending on which use is intended. Always determine the author's intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الألف واللام في مثل قولنا الإنسان إنه وإن كان من جملة ما يقتضي العموم في لغة العرب فلا يلزم أن يكون كذلك وإلا فلا مهمل في لغة العرب؛ وقد كان فيها من المهملات لما أن المهمل من الأقوال ما يحكم فيه على الطبيعة التي يصلح أن تؤخذ كلية ويصلح أن تؤخذ جزئية. ولهذا يلزم أن تكون الجزئية صادقة في المهملة مع احتمال أن تكون الكلية صادقة فكلية الموضوع وإن لم تكن مستلزمة لكلية الحكم فلا تكون منافية لها فتكون محتملة ،إذن، والجزئية صادقة على تقديري كونها كلية وعدم كونها كلية. ولا يظن أن الألف واللام في الاسم لتعريف الجنس أو لتعريف العهد فإنه كما يكون لهذا وذلك فقد يكون لغيرهما كذلك. وذلك في قولك الإنسان وتعني به الإنسان من حيث هو الإنسان لا من حيث هو الإنسان العام أو الخاص أو الذي ليس بعام أو ليس بخاص. فإن الألف واللام في مثل هذه الصورة لتعريف نفس الماهية. والماهية من حيث هي الماهية أمر وراء العمومية والخصوصية وغيرهما من اللواحق. فالإنسان في قولنا الإنسان من حيث هو الإنسان لا يكون متصفا بصفة ما البتة غير الإنسان في قولنا الإنسان متصف بصفة النوعية مثلا. فإنك إذا قلت الإنسان نوع فقد حكمت على الإنسان من حيث إنه كلي والكلي لا يكون بمعنى المجرد عن كونه كليًّا ولا بمعنى الكل أيضًا. ولا يصح أن يقال كل إنسان نوع؛ وذلك لا يصح أصلاً : فاستعمال الألف واللام إذا كان لتعريف الماهية فلا يدل بهما على العموم بل لا يمكن وإلا لكان معناه أن كل إنسان عام وذلك ممتنع . فيدل بهما إذن على تعيين الطبيعة فلا يكون موقعهما موقع كل . فلو قلت الإنسان هو الضحاك مثلا فقد حكمت على الماهية من حيث هي الماهية فيدل بهما على تعيين الطبيعة والقضية مهملة فكان معناه أن لا ضاحك إلا الإنسان ولا يصح أن يقال معناه أن لا ضاحك إلا كل واحد من الإنسان؛ فإن ذلك يفيد اقتصار الضاحكية على كل واحد من الناس واقتصار الحكم على الشيء مما يمنعه على الغير. وأما الحكم عليها لا من حيث هي هي بل من حيث إنه يعرض لها ما يفيد العمومية مثلاً كما إذا قلت الإنسان ناطق مثلا وتعنى به أن كل إنسان ناطق. واستعمال الألف واللام في مثل هذا التعريف الجنس فيدل بهما على العموم والقضية محصورة كلية، أو على الخصوص كما إذا قلت الرجل صالح وتعني به واحدًا معينًا كزيد مثلا . واستعمالهما لتعريف العهد حينئذ أي لتعريف المعهود فيدل بهما على شخص معين؛ والقضية شخصية. وبالجملة فإن نفس الماهية يصلح أن توجد طبيعة مجردة عما يعرض لها من الجزئية والكلية وغيرهما، ويصلح أن توجد كلية وجزئية كذلك كما مر . وقد يقال ما يكون دالا على نفس الماهية بذلك لا يكون دالا على العمومية والخصوصية والكلية والجزئية؛ إذ لو كان دالا على العمومية مثلا فإما أن يكون دالا بطريق المطابقة وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون مشتركًا فإنه يدل على الغير وهو نفس الماهية
Then, the definite article (alif-lam) in expressions like 'al-insan' (the human) – although it is among what necessitates generality in the Arabic language, it is not necessary that it be so; otherwise, there would be no indefinite (muhmala) in Arabic. But there were among them indefinites, because the indefinite among expressions is that which judges about a nature that can be taken universally or particularly. Hence it is necessary that the particular be true in the indefinite with the possibility that the universal be true. Thus the universality of the subject, even if it does not entail the universality of the judgment, does not contradict it; so it is possible. The particular is true on both suppositions: that it is universal and that it is not universal. And do not think that the definite article in the noun is for definition of the genus or definition of the covenant; indeed, just as it is for these two, it may be for others as well. That is in your saying 'al-insan' meaning the human insofar as he is human, not insofar as he is the universal human or the particular human or that which is not universal or not particular. For the definite article in such a form is for defining the essence itself. The essence, insofar as it is essence, is something beyond generality, particularity, and other accidents. So 'al-insan' in our statement 'al-insan min haythu huwa al-insan' (the human insofar as he is human) is not qualified by any attribute at all, unlike 'al-insan' in our statement 'al-insan muttasif bi sifat al-naw'iyya' (the human is characterized by the specific attribute). For if you say 'al-insan naw' (the human is a species), you have judged the human insofar as it is a universal, and the universal is not in the sense of stripped of universality nor in the sense of the whole. Nor is it correct to say 'kull insan naw' (every human is a species); that is not valid at all. So if the definite article is used for definition of the essence, it does not indicate generality; rather it is impossible, otherwise its meaning would be 'every human is general', which is impossible. Thus it indicates the designation of the nature, so its position is not the position of 'kull'. So if you say 'al-insan huwa al-dahhak' (the human is the laughing one), you have judged the essence insofar as it is essence; thus it indicates the designation of the nature and the proposition is indefinite, so its meaning is 'no laugher except the human'. It is not valid to say its meaning is 'no laugher except every individual human', for that would confine laughter to every individual person, and confining a judgment to a thing prevents it from others. As for judging it not insofar as it is itself but insofar as it has an accident that gives generality, for example, if you say 'al-insan natiq' (the human is rational) meaning thereby that every human is rational – the use of the definite article in such a case is for definition of the genus, so they indicate generality and the proposition is restricted as universal. Or to indicate particularity, as if you say 'al-rajul salih' (the man is righteous) meaning thereby a specific individual like Zayd; then their use is for definition of the covenant, i.e., definition of the previously known, so they indicate a specific person and the proposition is singular. In summary, the essence itself can exist as a nature stripped of what occurs to it of particularity, universality, etc., and it can exist as universal and particular as has preceded. And it may be said that what indicates the essence itself by that does not indicate generality, particularity, universality, or particularity; for if it indicated generality, for example, then either it indicates by way of conformity, in which case it would be ambiguous since it indicates something else, namely the essence itself.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage analyzes the logical functions of the definite article (alif-lam) in Arabic, distinguishing three uses: definition of the essence (ta'rif al-mahiyya), definition of the genus (ta'rif al-jins), and definition of the covenant (ta'rif al-'ahd). The author begins by noting that although the definite article often indicates generality, it does not always do so; otherwise there would be no indefinite propositions (muhmala) in Arabic. Indefinite propositions judge the nature itself, which can be taken universally or particularly. The author warns against assuming the definite article always defines the genus or a specific individual; it can define the essence, which is beyond universality and particularity. For example, 'al-insan' in 'al-insan min haythu huwa al-insan' (human insofar as human) refers to the essence, not to the universal or particular. When the definite article defines the essence, it does not indicate universality—'every human is general' would be impossible. Instead, it designates the nature, as in 'al-insan huwa al-dahhak' (the human is the laughing one), meaning no laugher except human. In contrast, when the article defines the genus, it implies universality, as in 'al-insan natiq' meaning 'every human is rational'. When it defines a specific individual (definite by previous knowledge), the proposition is singular, as in 'al-rajul salih' meaning 'the man (Zayd) is righteous'. The passage concludes that the essence itself can be considered stripped of accidents, or as universal or particular. The key insight is that the definite article has logical functions beyond simple generality, and one must carefully parse its role in determining the quantity of the proposition. In Islamic jurisprudence, similar distinctions apply: e.g., 'al-khamr haram' could mean 'wine (the essence) is forbidden' (an indefinite judgment) or 'every wine is forbidden' (universal) depending on context.

The Definite Article and Essence Indefinite Propositions
Author explains how 'al-' defines the essence and yields indefinite propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when 'al-' indicates the essence (mahiyya) and classify the proposition as indefinite (muhmala).

Core Idea
When 'al-' defines the essence, the proposition is about the nature itself, not its instances.

In this lesson, we unpack the first use of the definite article 'al-' in Arabic logic: defining the essence (mahiyya). This use results in an indefinite proposition (qadiyya muhmala), where the predicate is affirmed of the nature itself without quantification. Understanding this function is crucial for correctly interpreting many foundational propositions in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject with 'al-'
Find the subject term in the proposition that begins with 'al-'. For example, 'al-insaanu' in 'al-insaanu naw'un'.
2
Examine the predicate
Check if the predicate is a description that applies to the nature itself (e.g., 'species') rather than to individuals. If it applies to the nature, 'al-' likely indicates the essence.
If the predicate is a property like 'rational' that applies to each individual, 'al-' may indicate universality.
3
Test with 'kull' replacement
Attempt to replace 'al-' with 'kullu' (every). If the meaning becomes absurd or incorrect, as in 'kullu insaanin naw'un', then 'al-' is for the essence.
The author explicitly states that 'kullu insaanin naw'un' is incorrect.
4
Classify the proposition
If 'al-' indicates the essence, the proposition is indefinite (muhmala). The predicate is true of the nature, but the proposition does not imply anything about the extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون متصفا بصفة ما البتة غير الإنسان في قولنا الإنسان متصف بصفة النوعية مثلا. فإنك إذا قلت الإنسان نوع فقد حكمت على الإنسان من حيث إنه كلي والكلي لا يكون بمعنى المجرد عن كونه كليًّا ولا بمعنى الكل أيضًا. ولا يصح أن يقال كل إنسان نوع؛ وذلك لا يصح أصلاً : فاستعمال الألف واللام إذا كان لتعريف الماهية فلا يدل بهما على العموم بل لا يمكن وإلا لكان معناه أن كل إنسان عام وذلك ممتنع . فيدل بهما إذن على تعيين الطبيعة فلا يكون موقعهما موقع كل . فلو قلت الإنسان هو الضحاك مثلا فقد حكمت على الماهية من حيث هي الماهية فيدل بهما على تعيين الطبيعة والقضية مهملة فكان معناه أن لا ضاحك إلا الإنسان ولا يصح أن يقال معناه أن لا ضاحك إلا كل واحد من الإنسان؛ فإن ذلك يفيد اقتصار الضاحكية على كل واحد من الناس واقتصار الحكم على الشيء مما يمنعه على الغير. وأما الحكم عليها لا من حيث هي هي بل من حيث إنه يعرض لها ما يفيد العمومية مثلاً كما إذا قلت الإنسان ناطق مثلا وتعنى به أن كل إنسان ناطق. واستعمال الألف واللام في مثل هذا التعريف الجنس فيدل بهما على العموم والقضية محصورة كلية، أو على الخصوص كما إذا قلت الرجل صالح وتعني به واحدًا معينًا كزيد مثلا . واستعمالهما لتعريف العهد حينئذ أي لتعريف المعهود فيدل بهما على شخص معين؛ والقضية شخصية. وبالجملة فإن نفس الماهية يصلح أن توجد طبيعة مجردة عما يعرض لها من الجزئية والكلية وغيرهما، ويصلح أن توجد كلية وجزئية كذلك كما مر . وقد يقال ما يكون دالا على نفس الماهية بذلك لا يكون دالا على العمومية والخصوصية والكلية والجزئية؛ إذ لو كان دالا على العمومية مثلا فإما أن يكون دالا بطريق المطابقة وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون مشتركًا فإنه يدل على الغير وهو نفس الماهية بطريق المطابقة؛ ولا كلام في المشترك. وإما أن يكون دالا بطريق التضمن: وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون العمومية داخلة في نفس الماهية؛ وذلك لا يمكن فإن من الإنسان ما لا يكون عاماً كهذا وذلك. وإما أن يكون بطريق الالتزام وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون العمومية لازمة ينتقل الذهن من نفس الماهية إليها؛ وذلك محال فإن من الماهية ما لا ينتقل الذهن من نفس الماهية إليها كما هي في هذا وذلك. غير أنه يمكن أن يدل على البعض من تلك الأمور بطريق الالتزام وإن لم يدل على الكل؛ وذلك فيما يكون بينا ينتقل الذهن من نفس الماهية إليه. وأما في السبب فالكلي منه بالحقيقة هو أن يقال «لا واحد من الناس بحجر لما أن النفي فيه نفي عام على خلاف ما يقال بحسب لغة العرب «ليس أحد من الناس بحجر»؛ إذ النفي فيه نفي خاص، والمنفي عنه وإن كان عاما يعم الكل على سبيل البدل فإنه خاص في الحقيقة: وهو إما زيد وإما عمر و هلم جرا، والعمومية بهذا الطريق مما ينا في العمومية الحقيقية. وقول من قال إنه من جملة ما يقتضي العمومية بحسب اللفظ؛ وذلك في حيز المنع لما أنه لا يقتضي العمومية بحسب اللفظ أصلا وإن كان موهما عند البعض أنه مما يقتضي العمومية بحسب اللفظ اللهم إلا أن يقال إنه من جملة ما يقتضي العمومية بحسب الاستعمال؛ وذلك في حيز المنع أيضًا؛ إذ هو غير معلوم. ثم من الزوائد ما يزيد في المعنى ويفيد حكمًا خاصًا فيما يلحقها من القضايا مثل كلمة «إنما»، فإنها يقتضي اختصاص المحمول بالموضوع، فيقال إنما الإنسان ناطق، ويدل بها على اختصاص الناطقية بالإنسان. وهذه الدلالة بحسب الوضع لا محالة. وقد قيل في بيان أنها للاختصاص كلمة «إن» للإثبات وكلمة «ما» للنفي، فيحمل على النفي والإثبات إذن ولا يمكن حملها على نفي المذكور وإثبات ما عداه فيحمل على إثبات المذكور ونفي ما عداه؛
It is not at all qualified with a description other than human in our saying 'human is qualified with the description of species-ness' for example. For if you say 'human is a species', you have judged upon human insofar as it is a universal, and the universal is not in the sense of being abstracted from its being universal, nor in the sense of the whole either. And it is not correct to say 'every human is a species'; that is not correct at all. So the use of the definite article (alif and lam) when it is for defining the essence (mahiya) does not indicate universality; rather it is impossible, otherwise its meaning would be that every human is general, and that is absurd. Thus they indicate the determination of the nature, so their position is not the position of 'every'. So if you say 'human is the laughing one' for example, you have judged upon the essence insofar as it is the essence, so they indicate the determination of the nature, and the proposition is indefinite (muhmala), so its meaning is that no laugher exists except human. And it is not correct to say its meaning is that no laugher exists except every individual human; for that would imply restricting laugher-ness to every individual person, and restricting the judgment to a thing prevents it from others. As for judging it not insofar as it is itself but insofar as there occurs to it what benefits universality, for example as when you say 'human is rational' and mean by it that every human is rational. The use of the definite article in such a case is for defining the genus (jins), so they indicate universality, and the proposition is a universal quantified proposition (mahsuura kulliya). Or they indicate specificity, as when you say 'the man is righteous' and mean by it a specific individual like Zayd. And their use then is for defining the covenant (al-ahd), i.e., defining the covenanted, so they indicate a specific person; and the proposition is singular (shakhsiyya). In sum, the essence itself is suitable to exist as a nature abstracted from what occurs to it of particularity, universality, and others, and it is suitable to exist as universal and particular as well, as has passed. And it may be said that what indicates the essence itself by that does not indicate universality, particularity, universality, and particularity; for if it indicated universality, for example, then either it indicates by way of correspondence (mutabaqa), and then it would necessarily be ambiguous (mushtarak) because it indicates another thing, namely the essence itself, by correspondence; and there is no dispute about ambiguity. Or it indicates by way of inclusion (tadammun), and then universality would necessarily be included in the essence itself; and that is impossible, for among humans there is what is not universal, like this and that. Or it indicates by way of implication (iltizam), and then universality would necessarily be a necessary concomitant such that the mind transfers from the essence itself to it; and that is impossible, for among essences there is what the mind does not transfer from the essence itself to it, as it is in this and that. However, it is possible that it indicates some of those matters by way of implication even if it does not indicate all; that is in what is evident such that the mind transfers from the essence itself to it. As for the case of the cause, the universal in it truly is to say 'no one of people is a stone' because the negation in it is a universal negation, contrary to what is said according to the Arabic language 'not one of people is a stone'; for the negation in it is a particular negation, and the negand even if it is general encompassing all in the manner of substitution (badal), it is particular in reality: it is either Zayd or Amr etc., and universality in this way is opposed to true universality. And the statement of one who says that it is among what necessitates universality verbally; that is in the realm of denial because it does not necessitate universality verbally at all, even if it is suggestive to some that it is among what necessitates universality verbally, unless it is said that it is among what necessitates universality by usage; that is also in the realm of denial, for it is not known. Then among the additions (zawa'id) there are those that increase in meaning and benefit a specific judgment in the propositions that accompany them, such as the word 'innama'. For it necessitates the restriction of the predicate to the subject, so one says 'innama al-insanu natiqun' (only man is rational), and it indicates the restriction of rationality to man. And this indication is by convention necessarily. And it has been said in explanation that it is for restriction: the word 'inna' is for affirmation and the word 'ma' is for negation, so it combines negation and affirmation, and it cannot be taken as negating the mentioned and affirming what is other than it, so it is taken as affirming the mentioned and negating what is other than it.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of how the definite article 'al-' functions logically in Arabic propositions. The author begins by distinguishing two primary uses of 'al-' when attached to a term like 'insaan': one where it defines the essence (maahiya) and one where it implies universality. For example, in 'al-insaanu naw'un' (human is a species), the subject is judged as a universal, but the phrase does not mean 'every human is a species'—that would be incorrect. Here 'al-' indicates the nature itself, not the universal quantifier. So the proposition is indefinite (muhmala), meaning the predicate applies to the nature without extension. In contrast, in 'al-insaanu naatiqun' (human is rational) with the intention of 'every human is rational', 'al-' functions as a definition of the genus (jins) and yields a universal proposition. This shows that 'al-' can also indicate particularity, as in 'al-rajulu saalihun' meaning 'that particular man', making the proposition singular. The author then clarifies that the essence itself (maahiya) is neutral; it can be considered abstracted from universality/particularity, or as universal/particular depending on how it is qualified. Next, the author argues that a term indicating the essence cannot simultaneously indicate universality by way of correspondence (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), or implication (iltizam). If by correspondence, it would be ambiguous; if by inclusion, universality would be part of the essence, which is false (e.g., this human is not universal); if by implication, the mind would always transfer from essence to universality, which is not always the case. However, implication can occur accidentally. The discussion then turns to the expression 'laysa ahadun' (not one), as in 'laysa ahadun min an-naasi bi-hajar'. The author notes that in 'لا واحد من الناس بحجر' the negation is universal (no individual person is a stone), whereas in the Arabic phrase 'ليس أحد من الناس بحجر' the negation is particular because 'أحد' implies substitution (badal) — the negation applies to any individual but not to the entire set collectively. This particularity is opposed to true universality. The claim that such expressions verbally indicate universality is denied. Finally, the passage examines the word 'innama' (only). 'Innama' restricts the predicate to the subject; for instance, 'innama al-insaanu naatiqun' means 'only man is rational'. This restriction is composed of 'inna' for affirmation and 'ma' for negation, so it affirms the predicate for the subject and negates it for everything else. The explanation that it negates the mentioned and affirms what is other is impossible; instead, it affirms the mentioned and negates what is other.

Key Terms
الألف واللام
الماهية
قضية مهملة
تعيين الطبيعة

Checkpoint
In 'al-insaanu naw'un' (human is a species), what type of proposition does the author identify?
A) Universal proposition (kulliyya)
B) Indefinite proposition (muhmala)
C) Singular proposition (shakhsiyya)
D) Particular proposition (juz'iyya)

Key Takeaway
When 'al-' defines the essence, the proposition is about the nature, not its instances, and remains indefinite.

Propositions

The Definite Article for Universality or Particularity
Author explains how 'al-' indicates universality or particularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether 'al-' in a proposition indicates universality (genus definition) or particularity (a specific individual).

Core Idea
The same definite article 'al-' can make a proposition universal or singular, depending on the speaker's intention.

Not all definite articles are equal. In this lesson, we explore two other functions of 'al-': when it introduces a universal proposition (by defining the genus) and when it introduces a singular proposition (by indicating a specific known individual). The context and intention decide which function is at play.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Read the proposition and consider the intended meaning. If the predicate is a property that holds for every individual of that kind (e.g., 'human is rational'), 'al-' likely indicates universality.
2
Check for genus definition
If 'al-' defines the genus (jins), the proposition is universal and quantified. The author gives the example 'al-insaanu naatiqun' meaning 'every human is rational'.
This is different from the essence use because here the predicate applies to each individual.
3
Check for particular reference (al-ahd)
If the proposition refers to a specific individual known to the speaker/listener, then 'al-' indicates the covenanted (al-ahd), and the proposition is singular. Example: 'al-rajulu saalihun' meaning 'that particular man is righteous'.
4
Distinguish from essence use
A key distinction: if the predicate is a genus or property that applies to the nature (e.g., 'species'), it is essence; if it applies to individuals (e.g., 'rational'), it is universal; if it points to a specific person, it is particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون متصفا بصفة ما البتة غير الإنسان في قولنا الإنسان متصف بصفة النوعية مثلا. فإنك إذا قلت الإنسان نوع فقد حكمت على الإنسان من حيث إنه كلي والكلي لا يكون بمعنى المجرد عن كونه كليًّا ولا بمعنى الكل أيضًا. ولا يصح أن يقال كل إنسان نوع؛ وذلك لا يصح أصلاً : فاستعمال الألف واللام إذا كان لتعريف الماهية فلا يدل بهما على العموم بل لا يمكن وإلا لكان معناه أن كل إنسان عام وذلك ممتنع . فيدل بهما إذن على تعيين الطبيعة فلا يكون موقعهما موقع كل . فلو قلت الإنسان هو الضحاك مثلا فقد حكمت على الماهية من حيث هي الماهية فيدل بهما على تعيين الطبيعة والقضية مهملة فكان معناه أن لا ضاحك إلا الإنسان ولا يصح أن يقال معناه أن لا ضاحك إلا كل واحد من الإنسان؛ فإن ذلك يفيد اقتصار الضاحكية على كل واحد من الناس واقتصار الحكم على الشيء مما يمنعه على الغير. وأما الحكم عليها لا من حيث هي هي بل من حيث إنه يعرض لها ما يفيد العمومية مثلاً كما إذا قلت الإنسان ناطق مثلا وتعنى به أن كل إنسان ناطق. واستعمال الألف واللام في مثل هذا التعريف الجنس فيدل بهما على العموم والقضية محصورة كلية، أو على الخصوص كما إذا قلت الرجل صالح وتعني به واحدًا معينًا كزيد مثلا . واستعمالهما لتعريف العهد حينئذ أي لتعريف المعهود فيدل بهما على شخص معين؛ والقضية شخصية. وبالجملة فإن نفس الماهية يصلح أن توجد طبيعة مجردة عما يعرض لها من الجزئية والكلية وغيرهما، ويصلح أن توجد كلية وجزئية كذلك كما مر . وقد يقال ما يكون دالا على نفس الماهية بذلك لا يكون دالا على العمومية والخصوصية والكلية والجزئية؛ إذ لو كان دالا على العمومية مثلا فإما أن يكون دالا بطريق المطابقة وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون مشتركًا فإنه يدل على الغير وهو نفس الماهية بطريق المطابقة؛ ولا كلام في المشترك. وإما أن يكون دالا بطريق التضمن: وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون العمومية داخلة في نفس الماهية؛ وذلك لا يمكن فإن من الإنسان ما لا يكون عاماً كهذا وذلك. وإما أن يكون بطريق الالتزام وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون العمومية لازمة ينتقل الذهن من نفس الماهية إليها؛ وذلك محال فإن من الماهية ما لا ينتقل الذهن من نفس الماهية إليها كما هي في هذا وذلك. غير أنه يمكن أن يدل على البعض من تلك الأمور بطريق الالتزام وإن لم يدل على الكل؛ وذلك فيما يكون بينا ينتقل الذهن من نفس الماهية إليه. وأما في السبب فالكلي منه بالحقيقة هو أن يقال «لا واحد من الناس بحجر لما أن النفي فيه نفي عام على خلاف ما يقال بحسب لغة العرب «ليس أحد من الناس بحجر»؛ إذ النفي فيه نفي خاص، والمنفي عنه وإن كان عاما يعم الكل على سبيل البدل فإنه خاص في الحقيقة: وهو إما زيد وإما عمر و هلم جرا، والعمومية بهذا الطريق مما ينا في العمومية الحقيقية. وقول من قال إنه من جملة ما يقتضي العمومية بحسب اللفظ؛ وذلك في حيز المنع لما أنه لا يقتضي العمومية بحسب اللفظ أصلا وإن كان موهما عند البعض أنه مما يقتضي العمومية بحسب اللفظ اللهم إلا أن يقال إنه من جملة ما يقتضي العمومية بحسب الاستعمال؛ وذلك في حيز المنع أيضًا؛ إذ هو غير معلوم. ثم من الزوائد ما يزيد في المعنى ويفيد حكمًا خاصًا فيما يلحقها من القضايا مثل كلمة «إنما»، فإنها يقتضي اختصاص المحمول بالموضوع، فيقال إنما الإنسان ناطق، ويدل بها على اختصاص الناطقية بالإنسان. وهذه الدلالة بحسب الوضع لا محالة. وقد قيل في بيان أنها للاختصاص كلمة «إن» للإثبات وكلمة «ما» للنفي، فيحمل على النفي والإثبات إذن ولا يمكن حملها على نفي المذكور وإثبات ما عداه فيحمل على إثبات المذكور ونفي ما عداه؛
It is not at all qualified with a description other than human in our saying 'human is qualified with the description of species-ness' for example. For if you say 'human is a species', you have judged upon human insofar as it is a universal, and the universal is not in the sense of being abstracted from its being universal, nor in the sense of the whole either. And it is not correct to say 'every human is a species'; that is not correct at all. So the use of the definite article (alif and lam) when it is for defining the essence (mahiya) does not indicate universality; rather it is impossible, otherwise its meaning would be that every human is general, and that is absurd. Thus they indicate the determination of the nature, so their position is not the position of 'every'. So if you say 'human is the laughing one' for example, you have judged upon the essence insofar as it is the essence, so they indicate the determination of the nature, and the proposition is indefinite (muhmala), so its meaning is that no laugher exists except human. And it is not correct to say its meaning is that no laugher exists except every individual human; for that would imply restricting laugher-ness to every individual person, and restricting the judgment to a thing prevents it from others. As for judging it not insofar as it is itself but insofar as there occurs to it what benefits universality, for example as when you say 'human is rational' and mean by it that every human is rational. The use of the definite article in such a case is for defining the genus (jins), so they indicate universality, and the proposition is a universal quantified proposition (mahsuura kulliya). Or they indicate specificity, as when you say 'the man is righteous' and mean by it a specific individual like Zayd. And their use then is for defining the covenant (al-ahd), i.e., defining the covenanted, so they indicate a specific person; and the proposition is singular (shakhsiyya). In sum, the essence itself is suitable to exist as a nature abstracted from what occurs to it of particularity, universality, and others, and it is suitable to exist as universal and particular as well, as has passed. And it may be said that what indicates the essence itself by that does not indicate universality, particularity, universality, and particularity; for if it indicated universality, for example, then either it indicates by way of correspondence (mutabaqa), and then it would necessarily be ambiguous (mushtarak) because it indicates another thing, namely the essence itself, by correspondence; and there is no dispute about ambiguity. Or it indicates by way of inclusion (tadammun), and then universality would necessarily be included in the essence itself; and that is impossible, for among humans there is what is not universal, like this and that. Or it indicates by way of implication (iltizam), and then universality would necessarily be a necessary concomitant such that the mind transfers from the essence itself to it; and that is impossible, for among essences there is what the mind does not transfer from the essence itself to it, as it is in this and that. However, it is possible that it indicates some of those matters by way of implication even if it does not indicate all; that is in what is evident such that the mind transfers from the essence itself to it. As for the case of the cause, the universal in it truly is to say 'no one of people is a stone' because the negation in it is a universal negation, contrary to what is said according to the Arabic language 'not one of people is a stone'; for the negation in it is a particular negation, and the negand even if it is general encompassing all in the manner of substitution (badal), it is particular in reality: it is either Zayd or Amr etc., and universality in this way is opposed to true universality. And the statement of one who says that it is among what necessitates universality verbally; that is in the realm of denial because it does not necessitate universality verbally at all, even if it is suggestive to some that it is among what necessitates universality verbally, unless it is said that it is among what necessitates universality by usage; that is also in the realm of denial, for it is not known. Then among the additions (zawa'id) there are those that increase in meaning and benefit a specific judgment in the propositions that accompany them, such as the word 'innama'. For it necessitates the restriction of the predicate to the subject, so one says 'innama al-insanu natiqun' (only man is rational), and it indicates the restriction of rationality to man. And this indication is by convention necessarily. And it has been said in explanation that it is for restriction: the word 'inna' is for affirmation and the word 'ma' is for negation, so it combines negation and affirmation, and it cannot be taken as negating the mentioned and affirming what is other than it, so it is taken as affirming the mentioned and negating what is other than it.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of how the definite article 'al-' functions logically in Arabic propositions. The author begins by distinguishing two primary uses of 'al-' when attached to a term like 'insaan': one where it defines the essence (maahiya) and one where it implies universality. For example, in 'al-insaanu naw'un' (human is a species), the subject is judged as a universal, but the phrase does not mean 'every human is a species'—that would be incorrect. Here 'al-' indicates the nature itself, not the universal quantifier. So the proposition is indefinite (muhmala), meaning the predicate applies to the nature without extension. In contrast, in 'al-insaanu naatiqun' (human is rational) with the intention of 'every human is rational', 'al-' functions as a definition of the genus (jins) and yields a universal proposition. This shows that 'al-' can also indicate particularity, as in 'al-rajulu saalihun' meaning 'that particular man', making the proposition singular. The author then clarifies that the essence itself (maahiya) is neutral; it can be considered abstracted from universality/particularity, or as universal/particular depending on how it is qualified. Next, the author argues that a term indicating the essence cannot simultaneously indicate universality by way of correspondence (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), or implication (iltizam). If by correspondence, it would be ambiguous; if by inclusion, universality would be part of the essence, which is false (e.g., this human is not universal); if by implication, the mind would always transfer from essence to universality, which is not always the case. However, implication can occur accidentally. The discussion then turns to the expression 'laysa ahadun' (not one), as in 'laysa ahadun min an-naasi bi-hajar'. The author notes that in 'لا واحد من الناس بحجر' the negation is universal (no individual person is a stone), whereas in the Arabic phrase 'ليس أحد من الناس بحجر' the negation is particular because 'أحد' implies substitution (badal) — the negation applies to any individual but not to the entire set collectively. This particularity is opposed to true universality. The claim that such expressions verbally indicate universality is denied. Finally, the passage examines the word 'innama' (only). 'Innama' restricts the predicate to the subject; for instance, 'innama al-insaanu naatiqun' means 'only man is rational'. This restriction is composed of 'inna' for affirmation and 'ma' for negation, so it affirms the predicate for the subject and negates it for everything else. The explanation that it negates the mentioned and affirms what is other is impossible; instead, it affirms the mentioned and negates what is other.

العموم
الخصوص
تعريف الجنس
تعريف العهد
قضية محصورة كلية
قضية شخصية

Checkpoint
In the proposition 'al-rajulu saalihun' meaning 'that man is righteous', how does 'al-' function according to the author?
A) Defining the essence (mahiyya)
B) Defining the genus (jins) – universal
C) Defining the covenant (al-ahd) – particular
D) It is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
The definite article can indicate universality when defining the genus, or particularity when referring to a known individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the proposition 'al-khamru haraamun' (wine is forbidden). Does 'al-khamr' indicate the essence, universality, or a particular wine? Explain your reasoning using the three functions of 'al-' from this lesson.

The Restrictive Particle 'Innama'
Author explains how 'innama' restricts the predicate to the subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how 'innama' restricts the predicate to the subject and identify it in propositions.

Core Idea
Innama is a compound of affirmation and negation that restricts the predicate to the subject alone.

In this lesson, we examine the particle 'innama' (only), which is used to restrict a predicate exclusively to a subject. Understanding its logical structure is vital for interpreting many Qur'anic verses and theological statements that emphasize uniqueness or exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify 'innama' in a proposition
Locate the particle 'innama' that begins the proposition. It typically comes at the start, e.g., 'innama al-insaanu naatiqun'.
2
Recognize the restriction meaning
'Innama' indicates that the predicate (e.g., rational) is exclusive to the subject (e.g., human). This is called hasr or restriction.
It means 'only humans are rational'.
3
Analyze its composition
The particle is composed of 'inna' (affirmation) and 'ma' (negation). Thus, it simultaneously affirms the predicate for the subject and negates it for everything else.
The author explains that it cannot mean negating the mentioned and affirming what is other; it must affirm the mentioned and negate what is other.
4
Contrast with other uses of 'al-'
Note that 'innama' restricts, while 'al-' alone can indicate essence, universality, or particularity. 'Innama' adds explicit exclusivity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For if you say 'human is a species', you have judged upon human insofar as it is a universal, and the universal is not in the sense of being abstracted from its being universal, nor in the sense of the whole either. And it is not correct to say 'every human is a species'; that is not correct at all. So the use of the definite article (alif and lam) when it is for defining the essence (mahiya) does not indicate universality; rather it is impossible, otherwise its meaning would be that every human is general, and that is absurd. Thus they indicate the determination of the nature, so their position is not the position of 'every'. So if you say 'human is the laughing one' for example, you have judged upon the essence insofar as it is the essence, so they indicate the determination of the nature, and the proposition is indefinite (muhmala), so its meaning is that no laugher exists except human. And it is not correct to say its meaning is that no laugher exists except every individual human; for that would imply restricting laugher-ness to every individual person, and restricting the judgment to a thing prevents it from others. As for judging it not insofar as it is itself but insofar as there occurs to it what benefits universality, for example as when you say 'human is rational' and mean by it that every human is rational. The use of the definite article in such a case is for defining the genus (jins), so they indicate universality, and the proposition is a universal quantified proposition (mahsuura kulliya). Or they indicate specificity, as when you say 'the man is righteous' and mean by it a specific individual like Zayd. And their use then is for defining the covenant (al-ahd), i.e., defining the covenanted, so they indicate a specific person; and the proposition is singular (shakhsiyya). In sum, the essence itself is suitable to exist as a nature abstracted from what occurs to it of particularity, universality, and others, and it is suitable to exist as universal and particular as well, as has passed. And it may be said that what indicates the essence itself by that does not indicate universality, particularity, universality, and particularity; for if it indicated universality, for example, then either it indicates by way of correspondence (mutabaqa), and then it would necessarily be ambiguous (mushtarak) because it indicates another thing, namely the essence itself, by correspondence; and there is no dispute about ambiguity. Or it indicates by way of inclusion (tadammun), and then universality would necessarily be included in the essence itself; and that is impossible, for among humans there is what is not universal, like this and that. Or it indicates by way of implication (iltizam), and then universality would necessarily be a necessary concomitant such that the mind transfers from the essence itself to it; and that is impossible, for among essences there is what the mind does not transfer from the essence itself to it, as it is in this and that. However, it is possible that it indicates some of those matters by way of implication even if it does not indicate all; that is in what is evident such that the mind transfers from the essence itself to it. As for the case of the cause, the universal in it truly is to say 'no one of people is a stone' because the negation in it is a universal negation, contrary to what is said according to the Arabic language 'not one of people is a stone'; for the negation in it is a particular negation, and the negand even if it is general encompassing all in the manner of substitution (badal), it is particular in reality: it is either Zayd or Amr etc., and universality in this way is opposed to true universality. And the statement of one who says that it is among what necessitates universality verbally; that is in the realm of denial because it does not necessitate universality verbally at all, even if it is suggestive to some that it is among what necessitates universality verbally, unless it is said that it is among what necessitates universality by usage; that is also in the realm of denial, for it is not known. Then among the additions (zawa'id) there are those that increase in meaning and benefit a specific judgment in the propositions that accompany them, such as the word 'innama'. For it necessitates the restriction of the predicate to the subject, so one says 'innama al-insanu natiqun' (only man is rational), and it indicates the restriction of rationality to man. And this indication is by convention necessarily. And it has been said in explanation that it is for restriction: the word 'inna' is for affirmation and the word 'ma' is for negation, so it combines negation and affirmation, and it cannot be taken as negating the mentioned and affirming what is other than it, so it is taken as affirming the mentioned and negating what is other than it.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of how the definite article 'al-' functions logically in Arabic propositions. The author begins by distinguishing two primary uses of 'al-' when attached to a term like 'insaan': one where it defines the essence (maahiya) and one where it implies universality. For example, in 'al-insaanu naw'un' (human is a species), the subject is judged as a universal, but the phrase does not mean 'every human is a species'—that would be incorrect. Here 'al-' indicates the nature itself, not the universal quantifier. So the proposition is indefinite (muhmala), meaning the predicate applies to the nature without extension. In contrast, in 'al-insaanu naatiqun' (human is rational) with the intention of 'every human is rational', 'al-' functions as a definition of the genus (jins) and yields a universal proposition. This shows that 'al-' can also indicate particularity, as in 'al-rajulu saalihun' meaning 'that particular man', making the proposition singular. The author then clarifies that the essence itself (maahiya) is neutral; it can be considered abstracted from universality/particularity, or as universal/particular depending on how it is qualified. Next, the author argues that a term indicating the essence cannot simultaneously indicate universality by way of correspondence (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), or implication (iltizam). If by correspondence, it would be ambiguous; if by inclusion, universality would be part of the essence, which is false (e.g., this human is not universal); if by implication, the mind would always transfer from essence to universality, which is not always the case. However, implication can occur accidentally. The discussion then turns to the expression 'laysa ahadun' (not one), as in 'laysa ahadun min an-naasi bi-hajar'. The author notes that in 'لا واحد من الناس بحجر' the negation is universal (no individual person is a stone), whereas in the Arabic phrase 'ليس أحد من الناس بحجر' the negation is particular because 'أحد' implies substitution (badal) — the negation applies to any individual but not to the entire set collectively. This particularity is opposed to true universality. The claim that such expressions verbally indicate universality is denied. Finally, the passage examines the word 'innama' (only). 'Innama' restricts the predicate to the subject; for instance, 'innama al-insaanu naatiqun' means 'only man is rational'. This restriction is composed of 'inna' for affirmation and 'ma' for negation, so it affirms the predicate for the subject and negates it for everything else. The explanation that it negates the mentioned and affirms what is other is impossible; instead, it affirms the mentioned and negates what is other.

Key Terms
إنما
اختصاص
حصر
إثبات
نفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
'Innama' is a logical tool for restriction, affirming a property for one thing and denying it for all others.

The Exclusive Particle 'إنما' in Logical Propositions
Author explains how 'إنما' indicates exclusivity and the debate over which term is restricted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the logical function of the Arabic particle 'إنما' and identify whether it restricts the subject to the predicate or the predicate to the subject in a given proposition.

Core Idea
'إنما' creates exclusivity by affirming the predicate and negating everything else.

In Arabic logic, the particle 'إنما' (innama) plays a crucial role in forming exclusive propositions. It combines affirmation and negation to indicate restriction. However, there is a debate: does it restrict the subject to the predicate, or the predicate to the subject? This lesson will dissect the meaning of 'إنما' using logical analysis and a Quranic example.

Guided Path
1
Decompose the particle
Recognize that 'إنما' is composed of 'إن' (affirmation) and 'ما' (negation). The combination yields affirmation of the mentioned and negation of everything else.
This compositional analysis is key to understanding its exclusive force.
2
Identify the two possible restrictions
Determine whether 'إنما' restricts the subject to the predicate (i.e., the subject is limited to that predicate) or restricts the predicate to the subject (i.e., that predicate applies only to that subject).
The author notes that both views exist.
3
Apply the Quranic test
Use the verse {قل إنما أنا بشر مثلكم} to see which restriction is indicated. Here, the Prophet restricts himself to humanity, not humanity to him, supporting the first view.
This is evidence for restricting subject to predicate.
4
Note the common view and final clarification
The more common position is that 'إنما' restricts the predicate to the subject, but predication itself does not imply restriction; the particle adds it.
Understand that predication alone only asserts existence of the predicate for the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الزوائد ما يزيد في المعنى ويفيد حكما خاصا فيما يلحقها من القضايا مثل كلمة «إنما»، فإنها يقتضي اختصاص المحمول بالموضوع، فيقال إنما الإنسان ناطق، ويدل بها على اختصاص الناطقية بالإنسان. وهذه الدلالة بحسب الوضع لا محالة. وقد قيل في بيان أنها للاختصاص كلمة «إن» للإثبات وكلمة «ما» للنفي، فيحمل على النفي والإثبات إذن ولا يمكن حملها على نفي المذكور وإثبات ما عداه فيحمل على إثبات المذكور ونفي ما عداه؛ وحينئذ يلزم الاختصاص بالضرورة. ثم القائلون بأنها للاختصاص يختلفون في أنها لاختصاص الموضوع بالمحمول أو لاختصاص المحمول بالموضوع كما أشار إليه الشيخ في الإشارات وغيرها. ومن جملة ما يتحقق به الأول دون الثاني قوله تعالى: ﴿ قُلْ إِنَّمَا أَنَا بَشَرٌ مِثْلُكُمْ ﴾؛ فإنه يدل على اختصاص النبي عليه السلام بالبشرية لا على اختصاص البشرية به، غير أن المشهور منهما هو الثاني وهو مسطور في الأكثر من الكتب، فحمل الشيء على الشيء من حيث هو حمل الشيء على الشيء لا يقتضي الاختصاص وغير الاختصاص بل يقتضي وجود الشيء للشيء فقط. فالاختصاص وغيره ليستفاد من الغير، وذلك ما أشار إليه الشيخ في قوله: «إن هذه الزيادة تجعل الحمل أي المحمول مساويًا أو خاصا بالموضوع»، والمحمول قد يكون أعم كما في قولنا الإنسان حيوان، وقد يكون أخص كما في قولنا الإنسان كاتب، وقد يكون مساويا كما في قولنا الإنسان ناطق.
Then among the additions are those that increase in meaning and convey a specific judgment in the propositions that accompany them, such as the word 'إنما'. It necessitates the restriction of the predicate to the subject, so it is said 'إنما الإنسان ناطق' (Only man is rational), and by it is indicated the restriction of rationality to man. This indication is according to convention necessarily. And it has been said in explanation that it is for restriction: the word 'إن' is for affirmation and the word 'ما' is for negation, so it is taken as negation and affirmation then, and it cannot be taken as negating the mentioned and affirming its opposite, so it is taken as affirming the mentioned and negating its opposite; then restriction necessarily follows. Then those who say that it is for restriction differ: is it for the restriction of the subject to the predicate or for the restriction of the predicate to the subject as the Shaykh indicated in al-Isharat and others. And among what verifies the first without the second is the saying of the Exalted: {Say: I am only a man like you}; for it indicates the restriction of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to humanity, not the restriction of humanity to him. However, the well-known of the two is the second, and it is recorded in most books. So the predication of a thing of a thing, in terms of being predication of a thing of a thing, does not necessitate restriction or non-restriction, but rather necessitates the existence of the thing for the thing only. So restriction and its opposite are understood from something else. And that is what the Shaykh indicated in his saying: 'This addition makes the predication – i.e., the predicate – equal to or specific to the subject.' And the predicate may be more general as in our saying 'Man is an animal', and may be more specific as in our saying 'Man is a writer', and may be equal as in our saying 'Man is rational.'
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage defines the particle 'إنما' and its logical function of indicating restriction or exclusivity. The author explains that 'إنما' is composed of 'إن' (affirmation) and 'ما' (negation), and it conveys affirmation of the mentioned predicate and negation of everything else, thus restriction. There is a disagreement: does it restrict the subject to the predicate (e.g., 'Only man is rational' – i.e., no one else is rational) or restrict the predicate to the subject (e.g., 'Man is only rational' – i.e., man has no other attribute)? The author cites the Quranic verse {Say: I am only a man like you} to support the first view, because the Prophet is restricting himself to humanity, not humanity to him. However, the more common view is the second, that it restricts the predicate to the subject. The author then clarifies that predication itself does not imply exclusivity; exclusivity is added by the particle. He quotes Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) that 'إنما' makes the predicate equal to or specific to the subject. The passage then lists three possible relations of predicate to subject: more general (أعم), more specific (أخص), and equal (مساو). Examples from logic are given: 'Man is an animal' (general), 'Man is a writer' (specific), 'Man is rational' (equal). This shows that predication alone can have various logical relations, and 'إنما' narrows the range.

Key Terms
إنما (innama): A composite particle indicating restriction: it affirms the predicate for the subject and negates it from everything else, but debated whether it restricts subject to predicate or vice versa. [Note: Stable in logic; debated in usul al-fiqh regarding extent of exclusivity.]
الاختصاص (al-ikhtisas): Restriction or exclusivity: the predicate applies only to the subject, or the subject only has that predicate. [Note: Stable term across disciplines.]

Key Takeaway
The particle 'إنما' creates exclusivity, but its precise direction—whether it restricts the subject to the predicate or vice versa—is debated and must be determined by context.
