Definite Article and 'ليس إلا' as Equality Indicators
Author discusses equality indicators in propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and extensional equality indicated by the definite article in the predicate and the phrase 'ليس إلا'.

Core Idea
The definite article in the predicate and 'ليس إلا' both signal coextensiveness, but 'ليس إلا' can indicate definitional or extensional equality.

In this passage, the author explains two linguistic devices that indicate the subject and predicate are coextensive: the definite article on the predicate and the restrictive phrase 'ليس إلا'. Understanding these is crucial for interpreting definitions and universal statements in Islamic logic, as they affect how we understand terms in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two expressions
Locate the definite article (alif-lam) on the predicate in phrases like 'الإنسان هو الضحاك', and the phrase 'ليس إلا' in negative constructions.
The definite article on the predicate is not the same as the generic definite article.
2
Understand the function of alif-lam on the predicate
According to the author, when the predicate has the definite article, it indicates equality in actuality (coextensiveness) between subject and predicate in Arabic.
For example, 'الإنسان هو الضحاك' (Man is the laugher) means every man is a laugher and every laugher is a man.
3
Examine the negative constructions
The author mentions 'ليس إنما هو كذا' and 'ليس إلا' as negations that also indicate equality, but the focus is on 'ليس إلا'.
The negation 'ليس إنما هو كذا' judges the negation of what is actualized in affirmation.
4
Distinguish essential vs extensional equality for 'ليس إلا'
The phrase 'ليس إلا' can indicate equality in essence (the meaning of the two terms is identical) or equality in extension (they always co-occur, but meanings may differ).
For example, 'ليس الإنسان إلا الناطق' can be read as a definition (man = rational animal) or as a universal statement (all men are rational and vice versa).
5
Verify with the example
The author concludes that 'كل إنسان ناطق وبالعكس', showing bidirectional universal. Check if the context suggests which type of equality is intended.
This distinction affects how we interpret definitions and universal claims in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جملة تلك الزوائد دخول الألف واللام في المحمول كما يقال الإنسان هو الضحاك مثلا، فإنه يدل على التساوي بينهما في التحقق وذلك بحسب لغة العرب. وعلى هذا في السلب، فإنك إذا قلت ليس إنما هو كذا فقد حكمت على سلب ما يتحقق في الإيجاب، وكذلك إذا قلت ليس الإنسان إلا الناطق فإنه مما يقتضي المساواة لكنه تارة يقتضي المساواة في الماهية حتى يفهم منه أن معنى الإنسان ليس إلا معنى الناطق، وتارة يقتضي المساواة في العموم حتى يفهم أن الإنسان لا يوجد إلا وأن يوجد الناطق، وأن الناطق لا يوجد إلا وأن يوجد الإنسان؛ فيكون كل إنسان ناطقا وبالعكس.
And among those additions is the entry of the definite article (alif and lam) into the predicate, as is said 'الإنسان هو الضحاك' (Man is the laugher) for example, for it indicates equality between them in actuality, and that is according to the language of the Arabs. And likewise in negation, if you say 'ليس إنما هو كذا' (He is not only such), you have judged the negation of what is actualized in affirmation. And similarly if you say 'ليس الإنسان إلا الناطق' (Man is nothing but rational), it necessitates equality, but sometimes it necessitates equality in essence such that it is understood that the meaning of man is nothing but the meaning of rational, and sometimes it necessitates equality in extension such that it is understood that man does not exist except that rational exists, and that rational does not exist except that man exists; thus every man is rational and vice versa.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage discusses two types of expressions that indicate equality between subject and predicate. First, when the predicate has the definite article (e.g., 'Man is the laugher'), in Arabic this implies coextensiveness: every man is a laugher and every laugher is a man. This is a specific function of the definite article in predicate position. Second, the passage covers two negative constructions: 'ليس إنما هو كذا' (He is not only such) and 'ليس إلا' (nothing but). The author focuses on 'ليس إلا' and explains that it can indicate two kinds of equality: equality in essence (the meaning of man and rational are identical) or equality in extension (they always coexist). For example, 'Man is nothing but rational' could be taken as a definition (man = rational animal) or as a universal statement (all men are rational and all rational are men). This distinction is important for understanding how logicians handle terms and their definitions. The passage concludes that the result is a bidirectional universal: 'كل إنسان ناطق وبالعكس' (every man is rational and vice versa), which is a mutual inclusion.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'ليس إلا' is ambiguous between essential and extensional equality, so context determines whether it is a definition or a universal statement.

Dissecting the Universal Quantifier in 'Every J is B'
Author clarifies the precise meaning of the universal quantifier in categorical affirmative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct meaning of the universal quantifier in a categorical affirmative proposition and distinguish it from common misinterpretations such as the universal nature, the collection, the essence, the described, temporally conditioned, or potential existence.

Core Idea
The universal quantifier 'كل ج' means each individual J in actuality, not the universal concept, the collection, or J under any restriction.

In the foundational science of logic, the precise meaning of quantifiers is critical for syllogistic validity. This passage from the classical text systematically clarifies what is meant by 'كل ج' in a universal affirmative proposition, eliminating several plausible but incorrect interpretations. By following the author's reasoning, you will learn to avoid subtle errors that would render syllogisms unproductive.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the proposition form
The proposition 'كل ج ب' is a universal affirmative categorical. The subject is 'كل ج'. The author is defining exactly what 'كل ج' signifies.
This is the starting point for the entire discussion.
2
List the rejected interpretations
The author rejects: (1) the universal J (الجيم الكلي), (2) the collection of all Js, (3) the thing whose essence is J, (4) the thing described as J, (5) J that is always or at a time or under condition, (6) J in external existence only, (7) J in potentiality.
These are found in the Arabic text.
3
Understand the logical reasons for rejection
Each rejection prevents a specific logical flaw: (1)-(2) confuse distributive and collective; (3) would prevent syllogistic productivity; (4) leads to infinite regress; (5)-(7) are either too restrictive or not actual.
4
Identify the correct meaning
The correct meaning is the most general: whatever can be truly described as J in reality, actually (not potentially), whether in the mind or externally, and where the description holds at the time of judgment. The author distinguishes between 'at any time' and 'while being J', favoring the latter.
5
Apply the distinction to the example
Consider 'كل نائم مستيقظ' (every sleeper is awake). If 'كل نائم' means anyone who is a sleeper at some time, it is true; but if it means anyone who is sleeping at the moment, it is false. The correct logical interpretation is the latter.
This example illustrates why temporal precision matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا قلنا كل «ج» في قولنا كل ج) ب، فلا نعني به الجيم الكلي ولا كل الجيمات من حيث إنه كل بل كل واحد واحد والفرق بين هذه الثلاثة من جملة ما قد مرّ ذكره ولا نعني به الشيء الذي حقيقته «ج» أيضًا، وإلا لا ينعقد قياس منتج أصلا، ولا الشيء الذي يكون موصوفا بأنه «ج» وإلا يلزم التسلسل. فإن المعنى بذلك الموصوف حينئذ هو الموصوف بأنه موصوف بأنه «ج»، وكذلك في ذلك إلى غير النهاية. ولا الشيء الذي يكون «ج» دائما أو وقتاً ما أو بشرط ما بل نعني به ما هو الأعم منها وهو الذي يصدق عليه أنه ج ب الحقيقة ولا نعني به أيضًا ما يكون «ج» في الخارج بل الذي لا يوجد في الخارج إلا وهو «ج»، ولا الذي يكون (ج) ب) القوة كذلك، بل الذي يكون ج ب» الفعل ولو في وقت ما سواء كان بالفعل في الأعيان أو في الأذهان. فلو قلنا كل «ج» لكان من المحتمل أن يكون المراد كل ما يصدق عليه أنه ج سواء كان حال كونه «ج» أو قبله أو بعده، أو كل ما يصدق عليه أنه «ج» حال كونه «ج»، ولا خفاء في الفرق بينهما فإن في الأول يصح أن يقال كل نائم مستيقظ وفي الثاني لا يصح.
When we say "every J" in our statement "every J is B", we do not mean by it the universal J, nor every J-ness inasmuch as it is a universal, but rather each single one; and the difference between these three is part of what has been mentioned. And we do not mean by it the thing whose reality is "J" either, otherwise no productive syllogism would be formed at all. Nor the thing that is described as being "J", otherwise an infinite regress would follow; for then the meaning of that described thing would be that which is described as being described as "J", and so on to infinity. Nor the thing that is "J" always, or at some time, or under a condition. Rather we mean by it what is more general than these, namely that of which it is true that it is J in reality. And we do not mean by it what is "J" in external existence, but rather that which does not exist in external existence except as being "J". Nor that which is "J" in potentiality in that way, but rather that which is "J" in actuality, even if only at some time, whether it is actual in external entities or in minds. So if we said "every J", it would be possible that what is intended is everything of which it is true that it is J, whether while being J or before or after, or everything of which it is true that it is J while being J; and the difference between them is not hidden, for in the first it is correct to say "every sleeper is awake", while in the second it is not correct.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage defines the precise meaning of the subject term in a universal affirmative categorical proposition of the form "كل ج ب" (every J is B). The author systematically eliminates several incorrect interpretations. First, the subject is not the universal J itself (the nature qua universal), nor is it the collection of all Js considered as a whole. Rather it is each individual J taken one by one. Second, it is not the thing whose essence is J-ness, because then a syllogism like "Every J is B, every B is C" would be unproductive if J is a real essence. Third, it is not the thing described as J, because that leads to an infinite regress of descriptions. Fourth, it is not J that exists always, at a specific time, or under a condition—interpretations that are too restrictive. The correct meaning is the most general: whatever is truly J, whether in actual external existence or in the mind, and whether actually J at some time or not, as long as the description holds. The author clarifies by distinguishing two possible readings: (1) everything of which J is true at any time (past, present, future) and (2) everything of which J is true at the moment of judgment. The difference is illustrated with the example "every sleeper is awake": under the first reading it could be true (a person who sleeps at night is awake by day), but under the second it is false (a sleeper is not awake while sleeping). Thus the correct interpretation of the quantifier is crucial for logical validity.

Key Terms
ج (jīm): A placeholder variable for the subject term in a categorical proposition, representing an arbitrary universal term. [Note: Stable as a logical variable; common in Arabic logic texts.]
كلي (kullī): The universal nature considered as a concept, as opposed to the individuals or the whole collection.
كل واحد واحد (kull wāḥid wāḥid): Each individual singly, distributed distributively, not collectively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject interpreting 'كل ج' as 'the thing whose reality is J' in 'كل ج ب'?
A) Because it leads to an infinite regress.
B) Because no productive syllogism would be formed.
C) Because it is too restrictive and excludes potential cases.
D) Because it confuses the universal with the individual.

Key Takeaway
The universal quantifier in categorical propositions distributes over each individual instance that satisfies the predicate in actuality, not over the universal concept, the collection, or any conditioned or potential existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'كل متكلم محدث' (every speaker is temporally originated), explain what 'كل متكلم' does NOT mean according to the author's rejections. Provide one example of an incorrect interpretation that would lead to a logical flaw.

The Meaning of Negative Universal Propositions
Author defines the correct meaning of negative universal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the logical meaning of a negative universal proposition (لا شيء من ج ب) and distinguish it from a false claim about essences.

Core Idea
A negative proposition denies co-existence of descriptions, not identity of essences.

In this passage, the author clarifies a common misunderstanding about negative universal propositions. When we say 'no J is B', we are not claiming that the essence of J is different from the essence of B, but rather that the two descriptions (J-ness and B-ness) never coexist in any single entity. This distinction is crucial for correct logical analysis and for applying propositions in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative proposition form
The author discusses the proposition 'لا شيء من ج ب' (no J is B). Locate this form in the Arabic and note that it is a universal negative.
2
Extract the two possible interpretations
The passage presents two interpretations: (1) the essence of J is not the essence of B, and (2) the two descriptions do not coexist in one essence. Which one does the author accept?
The author rejects the first and accepts the second.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Suppose we say 'لا شيء من الخمر حلال' (no wine is permissible). According to the correct interpretation, this means that the descriptions 'wine' and 'permissible' never coexist in any individual, not that the essence of wine is not the essence of permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الشرائط المعتبرة في الموجبة هي بعينها معتبرة في السالبة، وفي السالبة بحث آخر، وهو أنا إذا قلنا لا شيء من ج ب فلا نعني به أن حقيقة الجيم من حيث هو جيم ليست حقيقة الباء من حيث هي «ب»، وإن كان هذا هو وإنه مما ينافي الصدق في الموجبة أيضًا بل نعني به أن هذين الوصفين مما لا يجتمعان في ذات واحدة. ثم الجيم يسمى عنوان الموضوع، وما يصدق عليه ج يسمى ذات الموضوع والذات مع العنوان قد يتحدان كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان، وقد لا يتحدان كما في قولنا كل ضاحك حيوان. وبالجملة فإنه يظهر من هذه الجملة أن الحكم على الكلي غير الحكم على الكل، والحكم على الكل غير الحكم على كل واحد، فإنه يصح على البعض منها ما لا يصح على الآخر، وبالعكس. وعلى هذا في الحكم على الكلي والجزئي وعلى ما يكون دائما وعلى ما لا يكون دائما بل / في وقت ما، وعلى ما يكون بالفعل [٨٧و] وعلى ما يكون بالقوة، وعلى ما يكون في الذهن وعلى ما يكون في الخارج؛ فإنه يصح على أحدهما في هذه الصورة ما لا يصح على الآخر، وبالعكس. وكيف لا وقد كان هذا من حيث هو هو غير ذلك؛ وذلك أيضًا من حيث هو هو غير هذا. والحكم على الشيء غير الحكم على غير ذلك الشيء بالضرورة فالحكم على كل واحد من الإنسان غير الحكم على الإنسان نفسه كما مر . فلو قلت كل إنسان حيوان مثلا كان معناه أن كل واحدٍ واحدٌ من الإنسان حتى لا يشذ شيء من تلك الآحاد؛ وتلك الآحاد هي الجزئيات في الحقيقة. والجزئيات قد تكون من الجزئيات الشخصية كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان، وقد تكون من الجزئيات النوعية والشحصية معا كما في قولنا كل حيوان حساس
ثم من اللوازم أن تعلم أنه ليس قولنا أن كل واحد واحد مما هو كذا معناه من حيث هو كذا كقولنا كل أبيض مثلا ليس معناه كل ما هو أبيض من حيث هو أبيض فقط بل كل ما يطلق عليه لفظ أبيض كان ذلك الشيء أبيض بأنه نفس الأبيض، أو كان متصفًا بأنه أبيض شجرًا كان ذلك الشيء أو حجرًا أو غير ذلك؛ فكذلك من اللوازم أن تعلم أن قولنا كل أبيض ليس معناه كل ما هو موصوف بأنه أبيض دائمًا أو غير دائم فإن قولنا كل أبيض أعم من قولنا كل أبيض دائمًا أو لا دائما على ما عرف بل معناه أن كل واحد مما يوصف بأنه أبيض كان موضوعا للأبيض وموصوفًا به وإنه أعم بالنسبة إلى الدائم وغير
Then the conditions considered in the affirmative are the same as those considered in the negative, and in the negative there is another investigation: that when we say 'nothing of J is B', we do not mean that the reality of J inasmuch as it is J is not the reality of B inasmuch as it is B, although this is the case and it is inconsistent with truth in the affirmative as well; rather we mean that these two descriptions do not coexist in one essence. Then the J is called the 'title of the subject', and what J is true of is called the 'essence of the subject', and the essence and the title may coincide, as in our saying 'every man is an animal', and may not coincide, as in our saying 'every laughing thing is an animal'. In sum, it appears from this statement that the judgment on the universal is not the judgment on the whole, and the judgment on the whole is not the judgment on each single one, for what is true of some of them is not true of the other, and vice versa. And likewise in the judgment on the universal and the particular, on what is permanent and what is not permanent but rather at some time, on what is actual and what is potential, on what is in the mind and what is in external existence; for what is true of one of them in this form is not true of the other, and vice versa. And how could it not be, since this inasmuch as it is itself is not that, and that inasmuch as it is itself is not this. And the judgment on a thing is necessarily not the judgment on something else. So the judgment on each single man is not the judgment on man himself, as has passed. So if you said 'every man is an animal', for example, its meaning is that each single man, such that no one of those individuals is left out; and those individuals are in reality the particulars. And the particulars may be personal particulars, as in our saying 'every man is an animal', and may be specific and personal particulars together, as in our saying 'every animal is sentient'. Then it is among the necessary consequences that you know that our saying 'each single one of what is such-and-such' does not mean 'inasmuch as it is such-and-such', like our saying 'every white thing' for example—its meaning is not 'everything that is white inasmuch as it is white only', but rather 'everything that the word 'white' is applied to, whether that thing is white in that it is whiteness itself, or is described as being white, whether that thing is a tree or a stone or otherwise'. Likewise, it is among the necessary consequences that you know that our saying 'every white thing' does not mean 'everything that is described as white permanently or non-permanently'; for our saying 'every white thing' is more general than our saying 'every white thing permanently or non-permanently', as has been known, but rather its meaning is that every single thing that is described as white, being a subject for whiteness and described by it, and it is more general with respect to the permanent and non-permanent.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage expands on the conditions for negative universal propositions and clarifies the distinction between judgments on the universal, the whole, and each individual. The author first states that the same conditions that apply to affirmative propositions apply to negatives, but adds an important point: when we say 'لا شيء من ج ب' (no J is B), we do not mean that the essence of J is not the essence of B (which would also be false in affirmatives), but rather that the two descriptions never coexist in the same entity. Next, the author introduces the distinction between the 'title of the subject' (عنوان الموضوع) and the 'essence of the subject' (ذات الموضوع). The title is the term used (e.g., 'laughing'), while the essence is what the term applies to (e.g., man). They may coincide, as in 'every man is an animal' where 'man' is both title and essence, or they may not, as in 'every laughing thing is an animal' where 'laughing' is a property not identical to the essence. Then the author stresses a key logical principle: the judgment on the universal (concept) is different from the judgment on the whole set, and the judgment on the whole set is different from the judgment on each individual. This is illustrated by noting that what holds for one may not hold for the other. This principle applies across various distinctions: universal vs. particular, permanent vs. temporal, actual vs. potential, mental vs. external. The author gives the example of 'every man is an animal': it means each individual man, not the universal man. The particulars are either personal (individual men) or specific and personal together (like 'every animal is sentient' covers both species and individuals). Finally, the author clarifies the meaning of 'كل أبيض' (every white thing): it does not mean 'everything that is white inasmuch as it is white' (which would exclude non-essential whites like a white tree), but rather everything that the word 'white' applies to, whether the thing is white essentially (e.g., snow) or accidentally (e.g., a whitewashed wall). Moreover, 'كل أبيض' is more general than 'كل أبيض دائمًا' (every permanently white thing) or 'كل أبيض غير دائم' (every non-permanently white thing); it simply means every single thing that is described as white at the moment of judgment, regardless of duration.

Title and Essence of the Subject
Author distinguishes between the linguistic title and the real essence of the subject term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the title (عنوان) and the essence (ذات) of the subject in any categorical proposition and determine whether they coincide.

Core Idea
The subject's linguistic title is not always identical to its real essence.

The author introduces two key terms: 'عنوان الموضوع' (title of the subject) and 'ذات الموضوع' (essence of the subject). The title is the term used in the proposition, while the essence is what that term truly refers to. For example, in 'every laughing thing is an animal', the title is 'laughing thing' but the essence is 'man' (since only man laughs). Understanding this distinction is vital for correct logical analysis and for avoiding fallacies in Islamic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two terms in the passage
Find where the author defines 'عنوان الموضوع' and 'ذات الموضوع'. Note that 'J' in the proposition is the title, and 'what J is true of' is the essence.
2
Examine the examples of coincidence and divergence
In 'كل إنسان حيوان' (every man is an animal), the title 'man' and essence 'man' coincide. In 'كل ضاحك حيوان' (every laughing thing is an animal), the title 'laughing thing' does not coincide with essence 'man' (since laughter is an accident).
3
Apply to an Islamic proposition
Take 'كل مصلي متطهر' (every praying person is purified). The title is 'praying person', the essence is 'the person who prays' which is the same as the person, so title and essence coincide. But in 'كل مسافر يقصر الصلاة' (every traveler shortens the prayer), the title 'traveler' and essence 'the person traveling' coincide, but the attribute is accidental.

Propositions

Judgment on Universal vs. Particular vs. Each Individual
Author distinguishes judgment on universal, whole set, and each individual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between judgment on the universal concept, the whole set, and each individual, and apply this distinction to analyze Islamic legal maxims.

Core Idea
Judging the universal is not the same as judging each particular; the universal is one thing, the particulars are many.

The author emphasizes a critical logical distinction: the judgment we make about a universal concept (like 'man') is different from the judgment about the whole set of all men, and different again from the judgment about each individual man. For example, 'man' as a universal is a concept, while 'each man' refers to individual persons. This distinction has profound implications in Islamic legal theory, where rulings apply to universals but must be actualized on individuals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of judgment
The author says: 'الحكم على الكلي غير الحكم على الكل، والحكم على الكل غير الحكم على كل واحد'. Locate this sentence and note the three terms: كلي (universal), كل (whole set), كل واحد (each individual).
2
Understand the example with 'man'
The author gives the example: 'الحكم على كل واحد من الإنسان غير الحكم على الإنسان نفسه'. The universal 'man' is a concept, while 'each man' is an individual. Judging that 'man is an animal' (universal) is different from judging that 'each man is an animal' (distributive).
In logic, 'كل إنسان حيوان' means every individual man is an animal, not the universal.
3
Apply to an Islamic ruling
Consider the ruling 'الصلاة واجبة على كل مسلم' (prayer is obligatory on every Muslim). This is a judgment on each individual Muslim, not on the universal 'Muslim' as a concept, and not on the whole set of Muslims collectively (which would imply the set as a whole must pray, but some individuals could be exempt).

ثم من اللوازم أن تعلم أنه ليس قولنا أن كل واحد واحد مما هو كذا معناه من حيث هو كذا كقولنا كل أبيض مثلا ليس معناه كل ما هو أبيض من حيث هو أبيض فقط بل كل ما يطلق عليه لفظ أبيض كان ذلك الشيء أبيض بأنه نفس الأبيض، أو كان متصفًا بأنه أبيض شجرًا كان ذلك الشيء أو حجرًا أو غير ذلك؛ فكذلك من اللوازم أن تعلم أن قولنا كل أبيض ليس معناه كل ما هو موصوف بأنه أبيض دائمًا أو غير دائم فإن قولنا كل أبيض أعم من قولنا كل أبيض دائمًا أو لا دائما على ما عرف بل معناه أن كل واحد مما يوصف بأنه أبيض كان موضوعا للأبيض وموصوفًا به وإنه أعم بالنسبة إلى الدائم وغير
Then the conditions considered in the affirmative are the same as those considered in the negative, and in the negative there is another investigation: that when we say 'nothing of J is B', we do not mean that the reality of J inasmuch as it is J is not the reality of B inasmuch as it is B, although this is the case and it is inconsistent with truth in the affirmative as well; rather we mean that these two descriptions do not coexist in one essence. Then the J is called the 'title of the subject', and what J is true of is called the 'essence of the subject', and the essence and the title may coincide, as in our saying 'every man is an animal', and may not coincide, as in our saying 'every laughing thing is an animal'. In sum, it appears from this statement that the judgment on the universal is not the judgment on the whole, and the judgment on the whole is not the judgment on each single one, for what is true of some of them is not true of the other, and vice versa. And likewise in the judgment on the universal and the particular, on what is permanent and what is not permanent but rather at some time, on what is actual and what is potential, on what is in the mind and what is in external existence; for what is true of one of them in this form is not true of the other, and vice versa. And how could it not be, since this inasmuch as it is itself is not that, and that inasmuch as it is itself is not this. And the judgment on a thing is necessarily not the judgment on something else. So the judgment on each single man is not the judgment on man himself, as has passed. So if you said 'every man is an animal', for example, its meaning is that each single man, such that no one of those individuals is left out; and those individuals are in reality the particulars. And the particulars may be personal particulars, as in our saying 'every man is an animal', and may be specific and personal particulars together, as in our saying 'every animal is sentient'. Then it is among the necessary consequences that you know that our saying 'each single one of what is such-and-such' does not mean 'inasmuch as it is such-and-such', like our saying 'every white thing' for example—its meaning is not 'everything that is white inasmuch as it is white only', but rather 'everything that the word 'white' is applied to, whether that thing is white in that it is whiteness itself, or is described as being white, whether that thing is a tree or a stone or otherwise'. Likewise, it is among the necessary consequences that you know that our saying 'every white thing' does not mean 'everything that is described as white permanently or non-permanently'; for our saying 'every white thing' is more general than our saying 'every white thing permanently or non-permanently', as has been known, but rather its meaning is that every single thing that is described as white, being a subject for whiteness and described by it, and it is more general with respect to the permanent and non-permanent.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage expands on the conditions for negative universal propositions and clarifies the distinction between judgments on the universal, the whole, and each individual. The author first states that the same conditions that apply to affirmative propositions apply to negatives, but adds an important point: when we say 'لا شيء من ج ب' (no J is B), we do not mean that the essence of J is not the essence of B (which would also be false in affirmatives), but rather that the two descriptions never coexist in the same entity. Next, the author introduces the distinction between the 'title of the subject' (عنوان الموضوع) and the 'essence of the subject' (ذات الموضوع). The title is the term used (e.g., 'laughing'), while the essence is what the term applies to (e.g., man). They may coincide, as in 'every man is an animal' where 'man' is both title and essence, or they may not, as in 'every laughing thing is an animal' where 'laughing' is a property not identical to the essence. Then the author stresses a key logical principle: the judgment on the universal (concept) is different from the judgment on the whole set, and the judgment on the whole set is different from the judgment on each individual. This is illustrated by noting that what holds for one may not hold for the other. This principle applies across various distinctions: universal vs. particular, permanent vs. temporal, actual vs. potential, mental vs. external. The author gives the example of 'every man is an animal': it means each individual man, not the universal man. The particulars are either personal (individual men) or specific and personal together (like 'every animal is sentient' covers both species and individuals). Finally, the author clarifies the meaning of 'كل أبيض' (every white thing): it does not mean 'everything that is white inasmuch as it is white' (which would exclude non-essential whites like a white tree), but rather everything that the word 'white' applies to, whether the thing is white essentially (e.g., snow) or accidentally (e.g., a whitewashed wall). Moreover, 'كل أبيض' is more general than 'كل أبيض دائمًا' (every permanently white thing) or 'كل أبيض غير دائم' (every non-permanently white thing); it simply means every single thing that is described as white at the moment of judgment, regardless of duration.

A universal quantified proposition distributes the predicate to each individual, not to the universal concept.

Distinguishing the Universal from the Whole
Author distinguishes judgment on universal from judgment on whole, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a judgment on a universal concept (al-kulli) and a judgment on the collection of its instances (al-kull), and interpret 'kull' statements correctly in Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
Judging the universal is not the same as judging its individuals.

In Islamic logic, precise handling of universal statements is essential. This passage clarifies that a judgment about a universal concept (e.g., 'man is an animal') differs from a judgment about each individual falling under it (e.g., 'every man is an animal'). The author explains this distinction using key terms and extends it to various dichotomies. Understanding this difference is crucial for correctly interpreting propositions in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Find the terms al-kulli (universal), al-kull (whole), and kull wāḥid (each one). Note that the author uses 'insān' (man) as an example of a universal.
These are distinct logical categories.
2
Analyze the examples
Compare 'insān hayawān' (man is an animal) and 'kull ḍāḥik hayawān' (every laughing thing is an animal). The first predicates animal of the universal 'man'; the second predicates animal of each individual that laughs. They are not the same.
The second shows subject-predicate non-identity.
3
Extract the central thesis
The passage states: 'الحكم على الكلي غير الحكم على الكل، والحكم على الكل غير الحكم على كل واحد'. This means the judgment on the universal differs from the judgment on the whole collection, and the judgment on the whole collection differs from the judgment on each individual. What holds for the universal may not hold for the collection or its individuals.
This is a core logical principle.
4
Apply to dichotomies
The author lists pairs: perpetual/temporal, actual/potential, mental/external. For each, what applies to one may not apply to the other. This shows judgments are context-dependent.
Reflect on an Islamic example: the concept of ‘ilm (knowledge) is eternal in Allah, but human knowledge is created.
5
Interpret 'kull insān'
The passage clarifies that 'kull insān hayawān' means each and every individual human, not the universal humanity. This is crucial for correct logical inference.
Avoid the common mistake of treating 'kull' as the universal itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنسان حيوان، وقد لا يتحدان كما في قولنا كل ضاحك حيوان. وبالجملة فإنه يظهر من هذه الجملة أن الحكم على الكلي غير الحكم على الكل، والحكم على الكل غير الحكم على كل واحد، فإنه يصح على البعض منها ما لا يصح على الآخر، وبالعكس. وعلى هذا في الحكم على الكلي والجزئي وعلى ما يكون دائما وعلى ما لا يكون دائما بل في وقت ما، وعلى ما يكون بالفعل وعلى ما يكون بالقوة، وعلى ما يكون في الذهن وعلى ما يكون في الخارج؛ فإنه يصح على أحدهما في هذه الصورة ما لا يصح على الآخر، وبالعكس. وكيف لا وقد كان هذا من حيث هو هو غير ذلك؛ وذلك أيضًا من حيث هو هو غير هذا. والحكم على الشيء غير الحكم على غير ذلك الشيء بالضرورة فالحكم على كل واحد من الإنسان غير الحكم على الإنسان نفسه كما مر . فلو قلت كل إنسان حيوان مثلا كان معناه أن كل واحد واحد من الإنسان حتى لا يشذ شيء من تلك الآحاد؛ وتلك الآحاد هي الجزئيات في الحقيقة. والجزئيات قد تكون من الجزئيات الشخصية كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان، وقد تكون من الجزئيات النوعية والشحصية معا كما في قولنا كل حيوان حساس
Man is an animal, and they may not be identical as in our statement 'Every laughing thing is an animal.' In summary, it appears from this statement that the judgment on the universal is different from the judgment on the whole, and the judgment on the whole is different from the judgment on every single one. For it applies to some of them what does not apply to the other, and vice versa. And on this basis, regarding the judgment on the universal and the particular, and on what is perpetual and what is not perpetual but at a time, and on what is actual and what is potential, and on what is in the mind and what is external: it applies to one of them in this form what does not apply to the other, and vice versa. How could it not be, as this thing from the perspective of its essence is not that, and that also from the perspective of its essence is not this. And the judgment on a thing is necessarily different from the judgment on another thing. So the judgment on every single human is different from the judgment on humanity itself, as has passed. So if you say 'Every human is an animal' for example, its meaning is that each and every human, so that none of those individuals escapes; and those individuals are the particulars in reality. And the particulars may be from personal particulars as in our statement 'Every human is an animal,' and they may be from specific and personal particulars together as in our statement 'Every animal is sentient.'
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the distinction between judging a universal concept and judging its instances. The author begins by noting that while 'man is an animal' is true, 'every laughing thing is an animal' is also true but the subject and predicate are not identical (laughing thing is not the same as animal). He then states the central thesis: the judgment on the universal (al-kulli) differs from the judgment on the whole (al-kull), and the judgment on the whole differs from the judgment on each individual (kull wahid). This means that what holds for the universal concept may not hold for the collection of its individuals, and vice versa. The author extends this to various dichotomies: perpetual vs temporal, actual vs potential, mental vs external. The reason is that things differ in their essences, so judgments necessarily differ. He clarifies the meaning of 'every human is an animal': it refers to each and every human individual, not to the universal 'humanity'. These individuals are the real particulars. Finally, he notes that particulars can be of two types: personal particulars (individual humans) or a combination of specific and personal particulars (e.g., 'every animal is sentient' where the subject is the species 'animal' and the predicate applies to each individual animal). The key takeaway is the logical precision: 'kull' in 'kull insān' quantifies over individuals, not over the universal.

Understanding the Subject in Universal Affirmatives
Author explains necessary implications of universal affirmative, focusing on subject's condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the actual reference of the subject term in a universal affirmative proposition and distinguish it from merely potential or essential reference.

Core Idea
The subject in 'every A is B' refers to all things actually described as A, not merely those that could be A.

When a logician says 'every white thing is colored', what exactly does 'every white thing' refer to? Does it include only things that are eternally white? Only things that are white by essence? Or everything that could possibly be white? In this passage, the author unpacks the necessary implications of the subject term in a universal affirmative proposition. Understanding these nuances is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where propositions about attributes and rulings are constantly employed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the necessary implications in the text
The author lists three necessary implications (lawāzim) for understanding 'kull abyad'. Find them: (1) it includes both things that are white by essence and things described as white; (2) it includes both perpetual and non-perpetual whiteness; (3) it refers to actual description, not possible description.
Use the Arabic term 'اللوازم' to find these in the source.
2
Differentiate actual from potential
The passage explicitly states that 'kull abyad' does not mean 'everything that can be white' (ليست صفة الصحة). Explain why: the subject refers to things that are actually described as white, whether in external reality or in the mind.
The term 'الفعل' (actuality) is key here.
3
Understand the role of the intellect
The author clarifies that actuality includes mental existence. For example, geometric statements like 'every sphere encloses a figure with twenty triangular bases' are about intelligible objects, not necessarily existing ones. The intellect describes them as having certain properties.
This expands the scope beyond physical existence.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Take the usuli statement 'kull khamr harām' (every wine is forbidden). According to this passage, does it refer to all actual wines (including those in the mind) or all possible wines? Answer: actual wines, whether existing externally or mentally considered as wine.
This prevents over-extension to hypothetical substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من اللوازم أن تعلم أنه ليس قولنا أن كل واحد واحد مما هو كذا معناه من حيث هو كذا كقولنا كل أبيض مثلا ليس معناه كل ما هو أبيض من حيث هو أبيض فقط بل كل ما يطلق عليه لفظ أبيض كان ذلك الشيء أبيض بأنه نفس الأبيض، أو كان متصفًا بأنه أبيض شجرًا كان ذلك الشيء أو حجرًا أو غير ذلك؛ فكذلك من اللوازم أن تعلم أن قولنا كل أبيض ليس معناه كل ما هو موصوف بأنه أبيض دائمًا أو غير دائم فإن قولنا كل أبيض أعم من قولنا كل أبيض دائمًا أو لا دائما على ما عرف بل معناه أن كل واحد مما يوصف بأنه أبيض كان موضوعا للأبيض وموصوفًا به وإنه أعم بالنسبة إلى الدائم وغير الدائم، أو كان نفس الأبيض. وهذه الصفة ليست صفة الصحة فإن قولنا كل أبيض لا يفهم منه البتة كل ما يصح أن يكون أبيض بل كل ما هو موصوف بالفعل أنه أبيض سواء كان دائمًا أو في وقت ما على التعيين أو لا على التعيين. وهذا الفعل ليس فعل الوجود في الأعيان فقط كما مر من قبل. فربما لم يكن الموضوع ملتفتا إليه من حيث هو موجود في الأعيان، كقولنا كل كرة تحيط بذي عشرين قاعدة مثلثة، ولا الصفة هي على أن يكون الشيء وهو موجود بل من حيث هو معقول بالفعل موصوفًا بالصفة على أن العقل يصفه بأن وجوده بالفعل يكون كذا سواء وجد أو لم يوجد فيكون قولك كل أبيض معناه كل واحد مما يوصف عند العقل بأن يجعل وجوده بالفعل أنه أبيض دائمًا أو في وقت أي وقت كان. وكما عرفته في قولنا كل أبيض فكذلك في الغير فهذا جانب الموضوع وهو «ج» في قولنا كل ج ب
Then from the necessary implications is that you know that our statement 'every single one of what is such' does not mean 'from the perspective of its being such', like our statement 'every white thing' for example does not mean 'everything that is white from the perspective of its being white only', but rather everything to which the word 'white' is applied, whether that thing is white by being the same as whiteness, or is described as white, be it a tree, a stone, or otherwise. Similarly, from the necessary implications is that you know that our statement 'every white thing' does not mean everything that is described as white perpetually or non-perpetually, for our statement 'every white thing' is more general than our statement 'every white thing perpetually' or 'not perpetually' as known, but rather it means that every single thing that is described as white, it is a subject for whiteness and described by it, and it is more general with respect to the perpetual and non-perpetual, or it is the same as whiteness. And this description is not a description of possibility, for our statement 'every white thing' absolutely does not mean everything that can be white, but rather everything that is actually described as white, whether perpetually or at a specific time or not specific. And this actuality is not the actuality of existence in external objects only, as has passed. For perhaps the subject is not considered from the perspective of its existence in external objects, like our statement 'every sphere encloses a figure with twenty triangular bases', and the description is not that the thing is existent, but rather from the perspective of its being actually intelligible, described by the description, such that the intellect describes it as its actual existence being such, whether it exists or not. So your statement 'every white thing' means every single thing that the intellect describes as making its actual existence be white, perpetually or at any time. And as you have understood in our statement 'every white thing', so likewise in others. So this is the side of the subject, which is 'ج' in our statement 'every ج is ب'.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the meaning of the subject term in a universal affirmative proposition like 'every white thing is something'. The author identifies several necessary implications (al-lawāzim) for understanding such statements. First, 'every white thing' does not mean only those things that are white by essence (i.e., whiteness itself); it includes any entity that is described as white, whether it is a tree, a stone, or anything else. Second, the statement is not restricted to perpetual whiteness; it is more general, covering both perpetual and non-perpetual instances. Third, and most importantly, 'every white thing' refers to things that are actually described as white, not merely things that could be white (i.e., possible whites). The actuality here is not necessarily external existence (al-wujūd fī al-a'yān); it can be mental existence. For example, a geometric statement like 'every sphere encloses a figure with twenty triangular bases' is about intelligible objects in the mind, not necessarily existing externally. Thus, the subject of the proposition is considered as it is in the intellect, described by the intellect as having an actual existence (either external or mental). The passage concludes that this analysis applies to all universal affirmative propositions, and this is the side of the subject (jānib al-mawḍū') represented by the variable 'ج' in 'كل ج ب'.

Key Terms
اللوازم
الفعل
العقل

B) Everything that is actually described as white, whether externally or in the intellect

D) Only things that are white perpetually

Key Takeaway
The subject term in a universal affirmative proposition refers to all things actually described by that predicate, whether they exist externally or only in the intellect.

Three Views on the Absolute Proposition
Author defines absolute proposition and describes three views on modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three views on the absolute proposition and identify which view the author endorses.

Core Idea
The absolute proposition is defined differently by three groups: including necessity, excluding it, or restricting to present/past.

In this passage, the author presents three distinct interpretations of what it means for a proposition to be 'absolute' (mutlaq). Understanding these views is crucial because modal distinctions affect how we evaluate arguments, especially in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh. We will walk through each view and see which one the author prefers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups defining 'absolute'
Read the Arabic text carefully and list the three groups: the first group includes both necessary and non-necessary under absolute; the second group excludes necessary; the third group restricts to present or past time.
2
Understand the first view: absolute includes necessity
The first group holds that 'absolute' means no modal restriction at all, so necessary judgments like 'Man is animal' (as long as man exists) are absolute because they lack explicit modal qualifiers.
This is the view the author endorses.
3
Understand the second view: absolute excludes necessity
The second group stipulates that absolute judgments must be non-necessary, i.e., not holding for the entire duration of the subject's existence. They require non-permanence.
The author calls this the 'existential' view.
4
Understand the third view: absolute limited to present/past
The third group adds a temporal condition: the judgment holds only for the present or past moment, not for all times. They distinguish three modalities: absolute (present/past), necessary (all three times), possible (future).
The author calls this the 'temporal' view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية موجهة بجهة ما، والقضية الموجهة كما هو حقها يعرف من بعد. فأما إذا لم تكن موجهة فحكم المحمول ثبوته للموضوع مطلقا من غير قيد أصلا وهذا هو إطلاق القضية عند قوم تندرج تحته الضروري وغير الضروري، وعند قوم آخر يشترط في المطلق أن لا يكون الحكم ضروريا بمعنى ما دام الذات موجودة. فيكون إطلاقه بحسب الضرورة المشروطة مع شرط آخر وهو أن لا يندرج تحته إلا ما لا يكون دائما فإنه إذا اندرج كان من الواجب أن يتناول الدائم وغير الدائم كما في الأول؛ فيكون معنى قولهم إن كل «ج» كيف كان فهو ب لا دائما بل وقت ما ، وقوم آخر وافقوا هؤلاء في شرط اللا دوام، وتخصيص الحكم بوقت ما لكنهم شرطوا بأن ذلك الوقت هو الحاضر أو الماضي فيكون معنى قولهم كل ج) ب كل ما هو «ج» في الحال أو في الماضي «ج» فقد وصف بـ«ب» وقت وجوده». فيكون المطلق عندهم باعتبار ما حصل من الزمان في الوجود، والضروري باعتبار الأزمنة الثلاثة، والممكن باعتبار أي وقت فرض في المستقبل: فعلى هذا الرأي يمكن أن يصدق قول القائل كل حيوان إنسان؛ إذ ربما يعدم جميع الحيوانات في وقت من الأوقات دون الإنسان؛ وقولنا كل إنسان حيوان غير ضروري بحسب هذا الرأي بل مطلقا؛ إذ ربما يعدم نوع الإنسان في وقت من الأوقات فلا يكون موجودًا دائما. وما ليس بموجود دائما فليس بضروري على هذا الرأي. ونحن نخص اسم الإطلاق بالرأي الأول؛ فإنه بالرأي الثاني وجودي وبالرأي الثالث وقتي يعرف من بعد. وأما السالبة الكلية فينبغي أن يكون السلب المطلق يتناول كل واحد واحد مما هو موصوف بـ «ج» كيف ما وصف به تناولا مطلقا، لا يدرى أنه دائم أو غير دائم لكن اللغات التي نعرفها تشعر في السلب المطلق بزيادة معنى على هذا، كما يقال في اللغة العربية لا شيء من ج ب»» وفي الفارسية «هيج ج) (ب» نست»، فإنه يفهم من كل واحد منهما الزيادة وهي إن «ب» مسلوب عن «ج» ما دام موصوفاً بـ «ج»، حتى إذا كان شيء من الأشياء موصوفا بـ [؟] «ج» ولم يكن «ب» مسلوبًا عنه كانت القضية كاذبة. وما يدل على السلب المطلق فذلك مثل ما يقال كل واحد من «ج» يسلب عنه ب من غير بيان وقت وغير وقت: وإذا عرفت بتحقيق الكليتين فقس عليهما الجزئيتين لا في شيء واحد وهو أن دوام السلب والإيجاب في الجزئيات قد يكون من غير ضرورة مثل ما يتفق لبعض الناس أن يكون أبيض البشرة ما دام موجود الذات؛ وليس بضروري.
The proposition is modalized with some mode, and the modalized proposition as is its due is known later. As for when it is not modalized, the judgment of the predicate is its affirmation for the subject absolutely without any restriction at all, and this is the absoluteness of the proposition according to a group, under which the necessary and the non-necessary are included. According to another group, they stipulate in the absolute that the judgment not be necessary in the sense of as long as the essence exists. So its absoluteness is according to the conditional necessity with another condition, which is that only what is not permanent falls under it, because if the permanent falls under it, it would be necessary that it includes both permanent and non-permanent as in the first. So the meaning of their statement 'Every J, however it is, is B, not always but at some time.' And another group agreed with these in the condition of non-permanence and specification of the judgment to some time, but they stipulated that that time is the present or the past. So the meaning of their statement 'Every J is B, every that is J at present or in the past, J, has been described by B at the time of its existence.' So the absolute according to them is in consideration of what has occurred of time in existence, the necessary in consideration of the three times, and the possible in consideration of any time assumed in the future. According to this view, it is possible that the statement of the speaker 'Every animal is human' be true, because maybe all animals cease at some time without humans, and our statement 'Every human is animal' is not necessary according to this view but absolute, because maybe the human species ceases at some time and thus is not always existing. And what is not always existing is not necessary according to this view. And we restrict the name 'absolute' to the first view; for according to the second view it is existential and according to the third view it is temporal, to be known later. As for the universal negative, it is necessary that the absolute negation covers every single one of what is described by J, however it is described, with absolute coverage, not known whether it is permanent or non-permanent, but the languages we know indicate in the absolute negation an increase of meaning beyond this, as is said in Arabic 'No J is B' and in Persian 'Hich J B nist', for from each of them is understood the increase, which is that B is negated from J as long as J is described by J, so that if something among things is described by [؟] J and B is not negated from it, the proposition would be false. And what indicates the absolute negation is like that which is said 'Each one of J is negated from B without specification of time and without time.' And when you understand the two universals in verification, then compare the two particulars to them, with the exception of one thing, which is that the permanence of negation and affirmation in particulars may be without necessity, like what occurs to some people that they are white-skinned as long as the essence exists; and it is not necessary.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage defines different types of propositions based on modality. It first explains that a proposition can be modalized (muwajjaha) with a specific mode, or it can be absolute (mutlaqa). The absolute proposition is one where the predicate is affirmed of the subject without any qualification. However, scholars differ on what 'absolute' means. The first group holds that the absolute includes both necessary and non-necessary propositions. The second group restricts the absolute to non-necessary judgments, meaning the predicate does not hold as long as the subject exists. The third group adds that the absolute applies only to a specific time, either present or past. The author prefers the first view and calls the second view 'existential' and the third 'temporal'. The passage then discusses the universal negative proposition, stating that absolute negation must cover every instance of the subject without specifying permanence or non-permanence. However, languages like Arabic and Persian imply an additional meaning: the negation holds as long as the subject is described by the subject term. Finally, the author notes that particulars differ from universals in that the permanence of negation or affirmation in particulars may be accidental, not necessary, as in the example of a person being white-skinned as long as they exist.

مطلق
ضروري
ممكن

According to the third view on absolute propositions, what condition must hold for the judgment?
A) The judgment must be necessary at all times.

C) The judgment applies to all future times.
D) The judgment is unrestricted.

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'absolute proposition' varies among scholars; the author prefers the inclusive view that covers both necessary and non-necessary judgments.

Universal Negative Proposition: Absolute Negation and Linguistic Nuance
Author describes the universal negative proposition and its linguistic implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the logical requirement of absolute negation and the extra linguistic implication in Arabic and Persian universal negatives.

Core Idea
Universal negative propositions imply that the negation persists as long as the subject exists, adding a temporal nuance beyond simple coverage.

The author now turns to universal negative propositions and emphasizes that absolute negation must cover every instance of the subject. However, he notes that common languages like Arabic and Persian add an extra meaning: the negation holds as long as the subject is described by that term. This nuance is important for interpreting revealed texts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Understand the requirement for absolute negation
The author says absolute negation must cover 'every single one of what is described by J, however it is described, with absolute coverage, not known whether it is permanent or non-permanent.' This means the negation applies to all individuals of the subject without any temporal qualification.
2
Identify the linguistic extra meaning in Arabic and Persian
In Arabic, 'لا شيء من ج ب' and in Persian 'هيج ج ب نست', the speaker understands an additional meaning: B is negated from J as long as J is described by J. This implies a temporal persistence of the negation.
The author uses this to show language can add nuance beyond bare logical form.
3
Apply the distinction to the universal negative form
Contrast the logical form 'Each one of J is negated from B without time specification' with the linguistic form that implies 'as long as J is J.' In practice, the linguistic form is weaker because it depends on the subject's description.
This distinction is vital when analyzing statements in revelation.

Quantifiers in Propositions: Universal and Particular
Author explains quantifiers, their types, and the distinction between quantification of individuals and parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between quantification of individuals and quantification of parts in a proposition, and identify which side(s) of the proposition are quantified.

Core Idea
Quantification can apply to individuals or parts, and to subjects or both subjects and predicates.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by quantity: universal and particular. But the concept of quantification (istiwār) is more nuanced. This passage introduces two distinct uses of quantification: one that counts individual instances under a universal, and one that counts parts of a whole. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting propositions in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of quantification
Locate in the Arabic the terms for quantification in individuals (جزئيات) and quantification in parts (أجزاء). Note the author's explicit mention of both.
The passage states: 'والاستوار قد يذكر لبيان الكمية في الجزئيات ... وقد يذكر لبيان الكمية في الأجزاء'.
2
Determine the scope of each type
From the Arabic, find that the first type (individuals) applies only to the subject, while the second type (parts) can apply to both subject and predicate. The author says: 'الأول لا يكون لا من جانب الموضوع بخلاف الثاني فإنه قد يكون من جانبي الموضوع والمحمول'.
3
Understand the restriction of each type
The first type does not apply to particular (مخصوصات) things, and the second does not apply to counted (محصورات) things. Parse the meaning: quantification of individuals does not apply to singular terms; quantification of parts does not apply to terms that are already counted (e.g., numbers).
The phrase 'لا يتحقق في المخصوصات' indicates that if the subject is a particular individual, you cannot quantify over individuals within it.
4
Resolve the homonymy
The term 'استوار' is used for both types by homonymy (اشتراك معنوي) because both indicate numerical quantity. The author clarifies that the common meaning is quantification of number.
The phrase 'إذ الاستوار لبيان كمية العدد' shows the common ground.
5
Note the controversy on predicate quantification
Some scholars denied quantification in predicates, arguing that universality occurs only when predicated of many. Recognize the argument: a thing as such is not universal; universality is accidental when it is said of many.
The passage ends with a definition: 'والسور لفظة دالة على تقدير' — a quantifier is a word indicating estimation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في القضية فإنها قد تكون مسورة موجبة كانت أو سالبة وقد لا تكون ]

أما القضية فإنها قد تكون مسورة موجبة كانت أو سالبة وقد لا تكون والمسورة قد تكون كلية وقد لا تكون بل تكون جزئية. والاستوار قد يذكر لبيان الكمية في الجزئيات كما مر ذكرها وقد يذكر لبيان الكمية في الأجزاء؛ والفرق بينهما بين فإن الأول منهما لا يتحقق في المخصوصات أصلا والثاني لا يتحقق في المحصورات كذلك. ولأن الأول لا يكون لا من جانب الموضوع بخلاف الثاني منهما فإنه قد يكون من جانبي الموضوع والمحمول، واللفظ يطلق على الأول منهما وعلى الثاني بالاشتراك المعنوي؛ إذ الاستوار لبيان كمية العدد سواء كانت الكمية في الأجزاء أو في الجزئيات.

ومنهم من أنكر الاستوار في المحمولات وذلك لأن الشيء من حيث هو هو لا يكون كليًّا بل الكلية إنما تعرض له عند كونه مقولاً على كثيرين فيما لم يعتبر كثرة في موضوعاته لا تعرض له الكلية والسور لفظة دالة على تقدير
Section: [On the proposition, it may be quantified, whether affirmative or negative, and it may not be.] As for the proposition, it may be quantified, whether affirmative or negative, and it may not be. And the quantified may be universal or may not be but rather particular. And quantification may be mentioned to indicate the quantity in individuals as previously mentioned, and it may be mentioned to indicate the quantity in parts; the difference between them is clear: the first is not realized in particulars at all, and the second is not realized in counted things similarly. And because the first is not from the side of the subject, unlike the second, for it may be from both sides of the subject and predicate. And the term is applied to the first and the second by homonymy; for quantification indicates the quantity of number, whether the quantity is in parts or in individuals. And among them are those who denied quantification in predicates, because a thing as such is not universal; rather, universality occurs to it when it is predicated of many; if multiplicity is not considered in its subjects, universality does not occur to it. And the quantifier is a word indicating estimation.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions based on quantification. It first notes that a proposition may be quantified (musawwara) or unquantified. A quantified proposition can be universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya). The author then discusses two types of quantification: one that indicates quantity in individuals (juz'iyyat) and one that indicates quantity in parts (ajza'). The difference is that quantification of individuals does not apply to particular things (mukhasṣaṣāt), while quantification of parts does not apply to counted things (maḥṣūrāt). Moreover, the first type involves only the subject, while the second can involve both subject and predicate. Both types are called 'quantification' (istiwār) by homonymy, as they both serve to indicate numerical quantity. The passage ends with a note that some scholars denied quantification in predicates, arguing that universality is an accident that occurs to a thing only when it is predicated of many; if multiplicity is not considered in its subjects, universality does not arise. Thus, a quantifier is a word indicating estimation.

Key Terms
سور
استوار
جزئيات
أجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between quantification of individuals and quantification of parts?
A) The first applies to subjects only, while the second applies to both subject and predicate.
B) The first applies to predicates only, while the second applies to subjects only.
C) There is no difference; they are the same.
D) The first is used in negative propositions, the second in affirmative.

Key Takeaway
Quantification in logic can refer to counting individuals or parts, and it can involve the subject alone or both subject and predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example in Islamic jurisprudence where a proposition uses quantification over parts, and explain why it is not quantification over individuals.
?
Examine the Arabic proposition 'كل عالم فان' (Every scholar is mortal). Is the quantification over individuals or parts? Justify your answer using the criteria from this lesson.

Distinguishing Conditional from Absolute Negation
Author distinguishes negative propositions that imply duration from those that are absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a negative proposition expresses conditional or absolute negation based on its wording and temporal implications.

Core Idea
Negative propositions either negate conditionally or absolutely—duration is the key.

In logic, not all negations are created equal. Some negations imply that the predicate is denied only while the subject retains its description, while others deny it completely regardless of time. This passage from our source distinguishes these two types using examples from Arabic and Persian. You will learn to recognize each type and understand the logical implications for quantifiers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key terms
Find the terms 'salb mutlaq' (absolute negation) and 'salb mashrut' (conditional negation) in the passage. Notice that the author first discusses the conditional form using the Arabic example 'لا شيء من ج ب'.
2
Examine the conditional negative example
The expression 'لا شيء من ج ب' is interpreted as meaning B is negated from J as long as J is described as J. This is a conditional negation tied to the subject's existence. If something is J and B is not negated, the proposition is false.
3
Contrast with the absolute negative example
The author contrasts with absolute negation: 'كل واحد من ج يسلب عنه ب من غير بيان وقت' meaning every J is negated from B without any time condition. That is absolute negation—no temporal restriction.
4
Extend to particular propositions
The author warns that in particular negatives, the continuity (dawam) of negation may be accidental, not necessary. For instance, someone being white-skinned as long as their essence exists is not a necessary truth. Understand that absolute negation in particulars may still be conditional in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على هذا، كما يقال في اللغة العربية لا شيء من ج ب»» وفي الفارسية «هيج ج) (ب» نست»، فإنه يفهم من كل واحد منهما الزيادة وهي إن «ب» مسلوب عن «ج» ما دام موصوفاً بـ «ج»، حتى إذا كان شيء من الأشياء موصوفا بـ «ج» ولم يكن «ب» مسلوبًا عنه كانت القضية كاذبة. وما يدل على السلب المطلق فذلك مثل ما يقال كل واحد من «ج» يسلب عنه ب من غير بيان وقت وغير وقت: وإذا عرفت بتحقيق الكليتين فقس عليهما الجزئيتين لا في شيء واحد وهو أن دوام السلب والإيجاب في الجزئيات قد يكون من غير ضرورة مثل ما يتفق لبعض الناس أن يكون أبيض البشرة ما دام موجود الذات؛ وليس بضروري.
According to this, as it is said in Arabic 'Nothing from J is B' and in Persian 'Hich J B nist', it is understood from each of them the increase, namely that 'B' is negated from 'J' as long as it is described by 'J', such that if something is described by 'J' and 'B' is not negated from it, the proposition would be false. And what indicates absolute negation is like what is said 'Every single J is negated from B without specification of time and without time.' And if you understand the two universal propositions with certainty, then analogize to them the two particular propositions in a single thing: that the continuity of negation and affirmation in particulars may be without necessity, like what happens to some people that they are white-skinned as long as the essence exists; and it is not necessary.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage defines two types of negative propositions: conditional (muqayyad) and absolute (mutlaq). The author begins by giving examples in Arabic and Persian: 'Nothing from J is B' is understood to mean that B is negated from J as long as J is described as J; if something is J and B is not negated, the proposition is false. This is a conditional negation tied to the subject's existence. Then he contrasts this with absolute negation, like 'Every single J is B-negated' without any time condition. He then extends this to particular propositions: continuity of negation or affirmation in particulars may be accidental (like someone being white-skinned as long as their essence exists) and not necessary. This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of quantifiers in categorical propositions.

Two Types of Quantification and the Predicate Quantifier Debate
Quantification types and predicate quantifier debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between quantification of individuals and quantification of parts, and evaluate arguments for and against predicate quantification in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Quantification applies to subjects, not predicates – unless you mean parts, not individuals.

This passage from a classical logic textbook tackles a key question: can quantifiers like 'all' and 'some' attach to predicates? The author first defines two senses of quantification—by individuals and by parts—and then presents a debate on whether predicate quantifiers are valid. Mastering this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting quantified propositions in Islamic sciences, especially in usul al-fiqh where rulings like 'every intoxicant is forbidden' require precise quantification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of quantification
The author distinguishes quantification of individuals (جزئيات) – referring to a number of separate subjects – from quantification of parts (أجزاء) – referring to a number of parts within a single subject. Find where he introduces this distinction in the Arabic.
The first type applies only to universal terms, the second only to particular terms.
2
Apply the distinction to subject and predicate
Quantification of individuals can only come from the subject side (e.g., 'كل إنسان'). Quantification of parts can come from both sides (e.g., 'زيد كل هذه الأعضاء' – Zayd is all these members). Notice how the debate hinges on this.
3
Analyze the debate on predicate quantifiers
One group denies predicate quantifiers because universality comes from being predicated of many subjects, so a quantifier only makes sense on the subject. The other group allows it, but the author shows that in examples like 'Zayd is some people', 'some people' is one compound predicate, not a quantifier on 'people'.
Pay attention to the linguistic argument: 'بعض' in 'زيد بعض الناس' is not a quantifier but part of the predicate.
4
Test truth conditions for quantified propositions
The passage gives truth values for statements like 'Zayd is every human' (false always) and 'Zayd is not every such-and-such' (true in all matters). Apply these to different modal materials (affirmation, impossibility, possibility) using the author's categories.
The مادة (matter) refers to whether the predicate is necessarily, impossibly, or possibly true of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في القضية فإنها قد تكون مسورة موجبة كانت أو سالبة وقد لا تكون ]
أما القضية فإنها قد تكون مسورة موجبة كانت أو سالبة وقد لا تكون والمسورة قد تكون كلية وقد لا تكون بل تكون جزئية. والاستوار قد يذكر لبيان الكمية في الجزئيات كما مر ذكرها وقد يذكر لبيان الكمية في الأجزاء؛ والفرق بينهما بين فإن الأول منهما لا يتحقق في المخصوصات أصلا والثاني لا يتحقق في المحصورات كذلك. ولأن الأول لا يكون لا من جانب الموضوع بخلاف الثاني منهما فإنه قد يكون من جانبي الموضوع والمحمول، واللفظ يطلق على الأول منهما وعلى الثاني بالاشتراك المعنوي؛ إذ الاستوار لبيان كمية العدد سواء كانت الكمية في الأجزاء أو في الجزئيات.
ومنهم من أنكر الاستوار في المحمولات وذلك لأن الشيء من حيث هو هو لا يكون كليًّا بل الكلية إنما تعرض له عند كونه مقولاً على كثيرين فيما لم يعتبر كثرة في موضوعاته لا تعرض له الكلية والسور لفظة دالة على تقدير تلك الكثرة فلا تكون داخلة إلا على الموضوع. ومنهم من جوزه: وفيه من الكلام فإنا إذا قلنا زيد بعض الناس مثلا فلا الناس هو المحمول ولا البعض هو السور بل المجموع هو المحمول. ثم المحمول إذا كان شخصيا استحال دخول السور بحسب الجزئيات عليه لكن يمكن إدخال السور بحسب الأجزاء كما يقال زيد هو كل هذه الأعضاء أو بعض هذه الأجسام؛ والكل والبعض في مثل هذا الكلام ليسا من الاستوار البتة. وإلا لكان المعنى بالكل كل واحد لا الكل من حيث هو الكل، وبالبعض بعض ما يوصف بالموضوع لا البعض الذي هو الجزء: وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا زيد كل ذلك الشخص أي كل واحد من ذلك الشخص فهو كاذب فإنه ليس كذلك الشخص محمولا على آحاد كل واحد منها ذلك الشخص : فلا يصح حمله بالإيجاب إذن. فأما في السلب كما يقال زيد ليس كل ذلك الشخص فهو صادق. وقول من قال زيد ليس ولا واحد من هذا الشخص فإنه صادق أيضًا لكن يفهم أنه كاذب لما أنه يفهم أن هذا الشحص أمر خاص وله من الموضوعات وليس هذا واحدا منها؛ غير أن الاتهامات غير ملتفت إليها في القضايا بل الملتفت إليها المفهومات من أنفسها. وعلى هذا إذا قلنا زيد كل إنسان أو كل حيوان أو كل كاتب فإنه كاذب من غير شك. فأما إذا قلنا زيد ليس ولا واحد من كذا فإنه كاذب في مادة الإيجاب؛ صادق في مادة الامتناع، لا صادق ولا كاذب في مادة الإمكان بل يمكن أن يكون صادقًا ويمكن أن لا يكون.
وبالجملة فإن حمل الممكنات على الأشخاص لا يوجب تعيين الصدق والكذب. وأما إذا كان السور جزئيّاً موجبًا فذلك في مادة الإيجاب حق كقولنا زيد هو بعض الناس، وفي مادة الامتناع باطل، وفي مادة الإمكان موقوف. فإن كان السور جزئياً سالبًا كما في قولنا زيد ليس كل كذا فإنه يصدق في كل مادة
Chapter: [On the proposition: it may be quantified, whether affirmative or negative, and it may not be.]
As for the proposition, it may be quantified, whether affirmative or negative, and it may not be. The quantified may be universal or it may not be, rather it may be particular. Quantification may be mentioned to indicate quantity of individuals, as was mentioned previously, and it may be mentioned to indicate quantity of parts; the difference between them is clear: the first does not apply to particulars at all, and the second does not apply to universals likewise. And because the first is not from the side of the subject, unlike the second, which may be from both sides of subject and predicate. The term is applied to both by homonymy; for quantification is to indicate quantity of number, whether the quantity is in parts or in individuals.
And among them are those who denied quantification in predicates, because a thing in itself is not universal; rather universality accrues to it when it is predicated of many. As long as multiplicity is not considered in its subjects, universality does not accrue to it. And the quantifier is a word indicating the estimation of that multiplicity, so it only enters upon the subject. And among them are those who allowed it; and there is discussion in it: for if we say 'Zayd is some people', neither 'people' is the predicate nor 'some' is the quantifier; rather the composite is the predicate. Then if the predicate is particular, it is impossible for a quantifier according to individuals to enter upon it, but it is possible to enter a quantifier according to parts, as it is said 'Zayd is all these members' or 'some of these bodies'. And 'all' and 'some' in such speech are not quantifiers at all. Otherwise the meaning of 'all' would be each one, not 'all' as such, and the meaning of 'some' would be some of what is described by the subject, not 'some' as the part. And it is not like that: for if we say 'Zayd is all that person', meaning each one of that person, it is false, for that person is not predicated of each one of that person; thus its affirmative predication is not valid. As for negation, as in 'Zayd is not all that person', it is true. And the statement of whoever says 'Zayd is not any one of this person' is also true, but it is understood as false because it is understood that this person is a specific thing and has subjects, and this is not one of them; however, accusations are not considered in propositions, rather what is considered are the concepts themselves. And on this, if we say 'Zayd is every human' or 'every animal' or 'every writer', it is false without doubt. As for if we say 'Zayd is not any one of such-and-such', it is false in the matter of affirmation; true in the matter of impossibility; neither true nor false in the matter of possibility, rather it may be true and may not be.
In summary, the predication of possible things to individuals does not necessitate determination of truth and falsehood. And if the quantifier is particular affirmative, then in the matter of affirmation it is true, as in our saying 'Zayd is some people'; in the matter of impossibility it is false; in the matter of possibility it is suspended. And if the quantifier is particular negative, as in our saying 'Zayd is not every such-and-such', it is true in every matter.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage from a chapter on quantified propositions defines and discusses quantification (سور). The author first states that propositions can be quantified (مسورة) or not, and if quantified, they can be universal or particular. He then distinguishes two types of quantification: one indicating quantity of individuals (جزئيات) and one indicating quantity of parts (أجزاء). The first applies only to universals, the second only to particulars. The term 'سور' is used for both by homonymy. Next, the author presents a debate: some deny that quantifiers can attach to predicates, arguing that universality only arises from relation to many subjects, so quantifiers only enter on the subject. Others allow it. The author provides an example: 'Zayd is some people' — here 'some' is not a quantifier on the predicate; rather the composite 'some people' is the predicate. If the predicate is a particular (like a specific person), quantifiers according to individuals are impossible, but quantifiers according to parts can be used, e.g., 'Zayd is all these members'. However, 'all' here does not mean universal quantifier but 'all' as a whole. He then tests truth conditions: 'Zayd is all that person' (meaning each individual) is false; 'Zayd is not all that person' is true. He also discusses statements like 'Zayd is every human' — false universally. The passage ends with a general rule about predication of possibles and the truth conditions for particular affirmative and negative quantifiers in different 'matters' (مادة): affirmative (where predicate is possible for subject), impossible, and possible.

مادة
جزئي

Key Takeaway
Quantification in classical Islamic logic primarily attaches to the subject; predicate quantifiers, when they appear, actually modify the predicate itself and do not function as true quantifiers.
