Quantifiers Enter Only on the Subject
Discusses the rule that quantifiers only enter on the subject, and the case of personal predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a quantifier is correctly applied to the subject or predicate in categorical propositions from Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
Quantifiers only sit on the subject; personal predicate quantifiers are not true quantifiers.

This passage from a classical logic text establishes a key rule: quantifiers (like 'every' or 'some') only apply to the subject of a proposition, not the predicate. The author then examines an apparent exception—when the predicate is a personal term (e.g., 'Zayd')—and shows that phrases like 'every limb' are not genuine quantifiers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main rule
Read the opening: 'That multitude does not enter except upon the subject.' This states the rule that quantifiers only modify the subject.
2
Understand the exception discussed
The author notes some scholars allowed quantifiers on the predicate. Follow the reasoning that rejects this by analyzing 'Zayd is some people'.
The composite 'some people' is the predicate, not 'people' alone.
3
Analyze personal predicate cases
When the predicate is a personal term like 'this person', quantifiers can only enter by parts (e.g., 'every limb'), not by individuals.
The quantifier indicates parts of the personal entity, not distribution over multiple individuals.
4
Distinguish true quantifiers from part-indicators
In 'Zayd is every limb', 'every' does not function as a logical quantifier; it means 'each limb' collectively, not universal quantification.
True quantifiers distribute over individuals of a universal predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلك الكثرة فلا تكون داخلة إلا على الموضوع. ومنهم من جوزه: وفيه من الكلام فإنا إذا قلنا زيد بعض الناس مثلا فلا الناس هو المحمول ولا البعض هو السور بل المجموع هو المحمول. ثم المحمول إذا كان شخصيا استحال دخول السور بحسب الجزئيات عليه لكن يمكن إدخال السور بحسب الأجزاء كما يقال زيد هو كل هذه الأعضاء أو بعض هذه الأجسام؛ والكل والبعض في مثل هذا الكلام ليسا من الاستوار البتة. وإلا لكان المعنى بالكل كل واحد لا الكل من حيث هو الكل، وبالبعض بعض ما يوصف بالموضوع لا البعض الذي هو الجزء: وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا زيد كل ذلك الشخص أي كل واحد من ذلك الشخص فهو كاذب فإنه ليس كذلك الشخص محمولا على آحاد كل واحد منها ذلك الشخص : فلا يصح حمله بالإيجاب إذن. فأما في السلب كما يقال زيد ليس كل ذلك الشخص فهو صادق. وقول من قال زيد ليس ولا واحد من هذا الشخص فإنه صادق أيضًا لكن يفهم أنه كاذب لما أنه يفهم أن هذا الشحص أمر خاص وله من الموضوعات وليس هذا واحدا منها؛ غير أن الاتهامات غير ملتفت إليها في القضايا بل الملتفت إليها المفهومات من أنفسها. وعلى هذا إذا قلنا زيد كل إنسان أو كل حيوان أو كل كاتب فإنه كاذب من غير شك. فأما إذا قلنا زيد ليس ولا واحد من كذا فإنه كاذب في مادة / الإيجاب؛ صادق في مادة الامتناع، لا صادق ولا كاذب في مادة الإمكان بل يمكن أن يكون صادقًا ويمكن أن لا يكون. وبالجملة فإن حمل الممكنات على الأشخاص لا يوجب تعيين الصدق والكذب. وأما إذا كان السور جزئيّاً موجبًا فذلك في مادة الإيجاب حق كقولنا زيد هو بعض الناس، وفي مادة الامتناع باطل، وفي مادة الإمكان موقوف. فإن كان السور جزئياً سالبًا كما في قولنا زيد ليس كل كذا فإنه يصدق في كل مادة
That multitude does not enter except upon the subject. And among them are those who permitted it. And in it there is discussion: for if we say 'Zayd is some people' for example, then 'people' is not the predicate, nor is 'some' the quantifier, but the composite is the predicate. Then if the predicate is personal, it is impossible for the quantifier to enter upon it in terms of particulars, but it is possible to introduce the quantifier in terms of parts, as it is said 'Zayd is every one of these limbs' or 'some of these bodies'; and 'all' and 'some' in such speech are not among the quantifiers at all. Otherwise the meaning would be by 'all' every single one, not 'all' in terms of the whole, and by 'some' a part of what is described by the subject, not the part that is the component. And it is not so: for if we say 'Zayd is every that person', i.e., every individual of that person, it is false, for the person is not a predicate for each individual of that person; so its affirmation is not valid. As for negation, as it is said 'Zayd is not every that person', it is true. And the statement of whoever said 'Zayd is not any single one of this person' is also true, but it is understood as false because it is understood that this person is a particular thing and has subjects, and this is not one of them. However, considerations are not heeded in propositions; rather what is heeded are the concepts themselves. And upon this, if we say 'Zayd is every human' or 'every animal' or 'every writer', it is undoubtedly false. As for if we say 'Zayd is not any single one of such', it is false in matter of affirmation, true in matter of impossibility, neither true nor false in matter of possibility, but may be true or may not be. And in summary, the predication of possibles upon individuals does not necessitate determination of truth and falsehood. And as for if the quantifier is particular affirmative, then that is true in the matter of affirmation, as our saying 'Zayd is some people', false in matter of impossibility, and suspended in matter of possibility. And if the quantifier is particular negative, as in our saying 'Zayd is not every such', then it is true in every matter.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules regarding quantifiers (sawar) in categorical propositions. The author begins by stating a foundational rule: quantifiers only enter upon the subject (mawdu'), not the predicate (mahmul). He notes that some scholars permit quantifiers on the predicate. He then examines the implications. Using the example 'Zayd is some people', he argues that 'people' is not the predicate alone, nor is 'some' the quantifier; rather the composite 'some people' is the predicate. The discussion then focuses on the case where the predicate is personal (shakhsi), i.e., an individual. The author says that when the predicate is a personal term, it is impossible to introduce a quantifier that operates according to particulars (i.e., distributing over individuals of the predicate). However, it is possible to introduce a quantifier according to parts (ajza'), meaning the predicate can be considered as consisting of parts. For example, 'Zayd is every one of these limbs' or 'some of these bodies'. In such expressions, 'every' and 'some' are not genuine quantifiers (sawar) because they do not indicate quantity of individuals but rather parts. The author clarifies confusion: if we say 'Zayd is every that person', meaning each individual of that person, it is false; but 'Zayd is not every that person' is true. Similarly, 'Zayd is not any single one of this person' is true, but understood as false because of a misunderstanding. The author emphasizes that in logic, we consider the actual concepts, not extraneous considerations. He then extends the discussion to universal predicates like 'every human', stating that such predications are false. For negatives with 'not any', truth depends on the material: false in affirmable material, true in impossible, and undetermined in possible. The passage concludes with a summary: predication of possibles (mumkinat) on individuals does not fix truth value. For particular affirmative quantifiers on personal predicates, it is true in affirmable material, false in impossible, and suspended in possible. For particular negative quantifiers, it is true in all materials. All examples are from the Islamic tradition: Zayd as a proper name, humans, animals, writers, etc.

Key Terms

موضوع
محمول
شخصي
مادة

Checkpoint

A) True
B) False
C) Neither true nor false
D) Undetermined

Key Takeaway
Quantifiers only apply to subjects; personal predicates require quantifiers by parts, which are not true logical quantifiers.

?

5 min
Truth Conditions of Personal-Personal Propositions
Author analyzes personal-personal propositions: affirmation false, negation possibly true.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the truth of affirmative and negative propositions where both subject and predicate are personal terms (e.g., Zayd, that person).

Core Idea
In a personal-personal proposition, affirmation is always false, but negation can be true.

In this passage, the author examines propositions where both subject and predicate refer to individuals. He shows that affirming an identity between two distinct individuals is impossible, while denying it can be true. This sets the stage for more complex quantified propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize that the subject and predicate are both personal terms (e.g., 'Zayd' and 'that person').
Personal terms refer to specific individuals.
2
Check the quantifier
Note that when the predicate is personal, the quantifier 'every' (كل) is used metaphorically.
The analysis applies even with 'every' for contrast.
3
Apply the rule for affirmation
According to the author, affirmative personal-personal propositions are always false.
Example: 'Zayd is every that person' is false.
4
Apply the rule for negation
Negative personal-personal propositions can be true, as in 'Zayd is not every that person'.
The truth of negation depends on the actual identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال زيد ليس كل حيوان وليس كل فرس وليس كل كاتب هذا إذا كان المحمول شخصيا. فأما إذا كان كليًّا فذلك من جملة ما لا يستحيل إدخال السور بحسب الجزئيات على المحمول: وحينئذ إما أن يكون الموضوع والمحمول شخصيين أو كليين، أو الموضوع شخصيا والمحمول كليا، أو بالعكس. أما الأول فذلك باطل في الإيجاب فأما في السلب فلا فإنه لم يصح قولنا زيد كل ذلك الشخص فقد صح قولنا زيد ليس كل ذلك الشخص كما مرّ من قبل. وأما الثاني منها فذلك لا يخلو من أن يكون كل واحد من الموضوع والمحمول مهملة أو محصورًا، أو يكون الموضوع محصورا والمحمول مهملا، أو بالعكس. أما الأول فذلك ظاهر لما أن القضية فيه مهملة كقولنا الإنسان حيوان. وأما الثاني فإنه لا يصدق في الإيجاب الكلي أصلا فلا يقال كل واحد من الإنسان كل واحد من الحيوان أو كل واحد من الإنسان كل واحد من الفرس أو كل واحد من الكاتب. فأما في السلب فإنه كاذب في مادة الإيجاب فلا يقال كل إنسان واحد من الحيوان صادق في مادة الامتناع فيقال كل إنسان لا واحد من الفرس غير معلوم الحال في مادة الإمكان. وأما في الإيجاب الجزئي فإنه يصدق في مادة الإيجاب فيقال كل إنسان بعض الحيوان، ولا يصدق في مادة الامتناع والإمكان: فلا يقال كل إنسان بعض الفرس أو بعض الكاتب. وأما في السلب الجزئي فإنه يصدق في المواد بأسرها فيقال كل إنسان لا كل حيوان أو لا كل فرس أو لا كل كاتب.
It is said: Zayd is not every animal, and not every horse, and not every writer – this when the predicate is personal. As for when it is universal, then that is among that which does not preclude introducing the quantifier with respect to the particulars upon the predicate. And then either the subject and predicate are both personal or both universal, or the subject is personal and the predicate universal, or the opposite. As for the first, that is false in affirmation; as for negation, it is not, for it is not correct to say Zayd is every that person, while it is correct to say Zayd is not every that person, as previously mentioned. As for the second, that is not free from either each of the subject and predicate being indefinite or quantified, or the subject is quantified and the predicate indefinite, or opposite. As for the first, that is clear because the proposition therein is indefinite, like our saying 'Man is animal'. As for the second, it is not true in universal affirmation at all, so one does not say: every individual of man is every individual of animal, or every individual of man is every individual of horse, or every individual of man is every individual of writer. As for negation, it is false in the matter of affirmation, so one does not say 'every man is an individual of animal'; true in the matter of impossibility, so one says 'every man is not an individual of horse'; unknown state in the matter of possibility. As for particular affirmation, it is true in the matter of affirmation, so one says 'every man is some animal', and not true in the matter of impossibility and possibility: so one does not say 'every man is some horse' or 'some writer'. As for particular negation, it is true in all materials, so one says 'every man is not every animal' or 'not every horse' or 'not every writer'.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the truth conditions of categorical propositions when quantifiers are applied to both subject and predicate. The author begins by summarizing four main cases from a previous discussion: both terms personal, both universal, subject personal with predicate universal, and the reverse. He then examines the first two cases. For the first case (both personal), affirmation is always false (e.g., 'Zayd is every that person'), while negation can be true (e.g., 'Zayd is not every that person'), as explained earlier. For the second case (both universal), he distinguishes subcases based on whether each term is indefinite (muhmala) or quantified (mahsur). The first subcase (both indefinite) is straightforward: the proposition is indefinite like 'Man is animal'. The second subcase (subject quantified, predicate indefinite) is analyzed in detail: universal affirmation is always false; universal negation is false in the material of affirmation, true in impossibility, indeterminate in possibility; particular affirmation is true only in affirmation; particular negation is true in all materials. This systematic analysis uses the three logical materials (affirmation, impossibility, possibility) to determine truth values.

شخصي (shakhsī): An individual term referring to a specific entity, like 'Zayd'. [Note: Stable term in logic.]

Truth Conditions of Universal-Universal Propositions
Author analyzes universal-universal propositions with detailed subcases and material truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth of universal-universal propositions across subcases (indefinite/quantified) and three logical materials (affirmation, impossibility, possibility).

Core Idea
The truth of a proposition with two universal terms depends on whether they are indefinite or quantified, and on the logical material.

The author now turns to propositions where both subject and predicate are universal terms (e.g., 'man' and 'animal'). He breaks this into subcases: both indefinite, subject quantified and predicate indefinite, and the reverse. Using the three logical materials (affirmation, impossibility, possibility), he systematically evaluates truth values for each combination of quantity and quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subcase
Determine if both terms are indefinite (muhmala) or quantified (mahsur), or mixed. The author considers: both indefinite, subject quantified & predicate indefinite, and the reverse.
The reverse case (subject indefinite, predicate quantified) is noted but not detailed here.
2
Analyze the both-indefinite subcase
When both terms are indefinite, the proposition is itself indefinite, like 'Man is animal'. It can be true.
No quantifier is attached, so it is a simple categorical.
3
Analyze the subject-quantified, predicate-indefinite subcase
Universal affirmative: always false. Universal negative: false in material of affirmation, true in impossibility, unknown in possibility. Particular affirmative: true only in affirmation. Particular negative: true in all materials.
The three materials are: māddat al-ījāb (affirmation), māddat al-imtināʿ (impossibility), māddat al-imkān (possibility).
4
Apply the materials to examples
For 'Every man is an animal' (affirmation material) – universal affirmative is false. 'Every man is not a horse' (impossibility material) – universal negative is true. 'Every man is some writer' (possibility material) – particular affirmative is false.
These examples use 'man', 'animal', 'horse', 'writer' as in the text.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the truth conditions of categorical propositions when quantifiers are applied to both subject and predicate. The author begins by summarizing four main cases from a previous discussion: both terms personal, both universal, subject personal with predicate universal, and the reverse. He then examines the first two cases. For the first case (both personal), affirmation is always false (e.g., 'Zayd is every that person'), while negation can be true (e.g., 'Zayd is not every that person'), as explained earlier. For the second case (both universal), he distinguishes subcases based on whether each term is indefinite (muhmala) or quantified (mahsur). The first subcase (both indefinite) is straightforward: the proposition is indefinite like 'Man is animal'. The second subcase (subject quantified, predicate indefinite) is analyzed in detail: universal affirmation is always false; universal negation is false in the material of affirmation, true in impossibility, indeterminate in possibility; particular affirmation is true only in affirmation; particular negation is true in all materials. This systematic analysis uses the three logical materials (affirmation, impossibility, possibility) to determine truth values.

Key Terms
مهملة (muhmala): An indefinite term without a quantifier; the proposition is indefinite. [Note: Stable; in later logic, muhmala often means 'indefinite proposition'.]
محصور (mahṣūr): A determined term accompanied by a quantifier like 'every' or 'some'. [Note: Stable.]
مادة الإيجاب (māddat al-ījāb): The logical material in which the predicate is actually true of the subject, e.g., 'animal' of 'man'. [Note: Part of the standard 'matters' (mawād) in logic; stable.]
مادة الامتناع (māddat al-imtināʿ): The material in which the predicate is necessarily false of the subject, e.g., 'horse' of 'man'. [Note: Stable.]
مادة الإمكان (māddat al-imkān): The material in which the predicate is neither necessarily true nor false, e.g., 'writer' of 'man'. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Universal-universal propositions have complex truth conditions depending on quantifier distribution and logical material; the author provides a complete truth table for the case where the subject is quantified and the predicate indefinite.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Evaluating Universal-Universal Propositions with Quantified Predicate
Author continues analyzing universal-universal propositions with subject indefinite and predicate quantified, then both quantified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of any universal-universal proposition with a quantified predicate (third subcase) or both terms quantified (fourth subcase) across the three materials of affirmation, impossibility, and possibility.

Core Idea
The truth of quantified propositions hinges on both quantification pattern and material content.

In the previous lesson, we examined universal-universal propositions where both terms are quantified. Now we turn to two further subcases: the third, where the subject is indefinite and the predicate quantified; and the fourth, where both are quantified but the subject is indefinite and the predicate has a universal or particular quantifier. The author systematically evaluates each combination of quality (affirmation/negation) and quantity (universal/particular) across the three materials. Understanding these subcases equips you to handle the full range of categorical propositions in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subcase
Look at the proposition: if the subject is indefinite (not quantified) and the predicate is quantified (with 'every' or 'some'), it is the third subcase. If both terms are quantified but the subject is indefinite and the predicate has a quantifier (every/some), it is the fourth subcase. The key is recognizing the quantifier placement.
The fourth subcase often appears as 'Man is every animal' or 'Man is some animal'.
2
Determine the quality and quantity
For the third subcase, the quantity (universal/particular) is determined by the predicate quantifier. For the fourth, the subject is indefinite but the predicate quantifier indicates universal or particular. Then note whether the proposition is affirmative or negative.
The author handles both universal and particular forms for each quality.
3
Apply the material rules for affirmation
For affirmation: true in the material of affirmation (when the predicate is essentially true of the subject), false in the material of impossibility (when the predicate is essentially false), and true in the material of possibility if the predicate can be potential (e.g., 'writer' as potential).
The author gives examples: 'Every man is the animal' is true in affirmation; 'Every man is the horse' is false in impossibility; 'Every man is the writer' is true in possibility if 'writer' means potential.
4
Apply the material rules for negation
For negation: false in the material of affirmation (since it would deny a necessary truth), true in the material of impossibility (denying an impossible attribute), and unknown in possibility (since it depends on actuality).
Example: 'Every man is not a horse' is true in impossibility; 'Every man is not an animal' is false in affirmation.
5
Handle the fourth subcase carefully
For universal affirmation (e.g., 'Man is every animal'), it is false in all materials because the nature of man is not identical to every individual animal. For particular affirmation (e.g., 'Man is some animal'), it is true only in the material of affirmation. Negation follows parallel rules. The author warns not to confuse the meaning of the universal quantifier here.
The key insight: 'Man' signifies the species nature, not individuals, while 'every writer' picks out each individual writer. They are not equivalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث فظاهر أيضًا لما أن القضية فيه محصورة فيصدق في مادة الإيجاب كما يقال كل إنسان هو الحيوان أي كل واحد من الإنسان كما مر . ولا يصدق في مادة الامتناع فلا يقال كل إنسان هو الفرس. وكذلك في مادة الإمكان كما يقال كل إنسان هو الكاتب إلا وأن يكون المراد بالكاتب الكاتب بالقوة. وعلى هذا في الإيجاب الجزئي فإنه يصدق في مادة الإيجاب فيقال بعض الإنسان هو الحيوان، ولا يصدق في مادة الامتناع فلا يقال بعض الإنسان هو الفرس. وأما في مادة الإمكان فظاهر سواء كان المراد بالكاتب الكاتب بالفعل أو بالقوة: هذا في الإيجاب. وأما في السلب فإنه كاذب في مادة الإيجاب فلا يقال كل إنسان ليس بحيوان صادق في مادة الامتناع فيقال كل إنسان ليس بفرس؛ غير معلوم الحال في مادة الإمكان. وعلى هذا في السلب الجزئي. وأما الرابع فإنه كاذب في المواد بأسرها إذا كان في الإيجاب الكلي فلا يقال الإنسان كل حيوان ولا كل فرس ولا كل كاتب. ولا يظن أن قولنا الإنسان كل كاتب صادق وإن قيل إنه صادق؛ إذ المعنى من الإنسان طبيعة الإنسان، ومن كل كاتب كل واحد مما هو كاتب. فأما في السلب في مادة الإيجاب فلا يقال الإنسان لا شيء من الحيوان صادق في مادة الامتناع فيقال الإنسان لا شيء من الفرس؛ غير معلوم الحال في مادة الإمكان. وأما في الإيجاب الجزئي فإنه صادق في مادة الإيجاب فيقال الإنسان بعض الحيوان كاذب في مادة الامتناع فلا يقال الإنسان بعض الفرس، غير معلوم الحال في مادة الإمكان. وفي السلب الجزئي كاذب في مادة الإيجاب فلا يقال الإنسان ليس بعض الحيوان، صادق في مادة الامتناع فيقال الإنسان ليس بعض الفرس، غير معلوم الحال في مادة الإمكان.
As for the third, it is also clear because the proposition therein is quantified; it is true in the material of affirmation as is said 'Every man is the animal', i.e., every individual of man, as previously. And it is not true in the material of impossibility, so one does not say 'Every man is the horse'. Likewise in the material of possibility, as is said 'Every man is the writer' unless what is meant by 'writer' is the potential writer. And likewise in particular affirmation: it is true in the material of affirmation, so one says 'Some man is the animal'; not true in the material of impossibility, so one does not say 'Some man is the horse'. As for the material of possibility, it is clear whether what is meant by 'writer' is actual or potential writer. This in affirmation. As for negation, it is false in the material of affirmation, so one does not say 'Every man is not an animal'; true in the material of impossibility, so one says 'Every man is not a horse'; unknown state in the material of possibility. And likewise in particular negation. As for the fourth, it is false in all materials if it is universal affirmation, so one does not say 'Man is every animal' nor 'every horse' nor 'every writer'. And one should not think that our saying 'Man is every writer' is true even if it is said to be true; for the meaning of 'man' is the nature of man, and of 'every writer' is every individual that is a writer. As for negation in the material of affirmation, one does not say 'Man is nothing of animal'; true in the material of impossibility, so one says 'Man is nothing of horse'; unknown state in the material of possibility. As for particular affirmation, it is true in the material of affirmation, so one says 'Man is some animal'; false in the material of impossibility, so one does not say 'Man is some horse'; unknown state in the material of possibility. And in particular negation, false in the material of affirmation, so one does not say 'Man is not some animal'; true in the material of impossibility, so one says 'Man is not some horse'; unknown state in the material of possibility.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage continues the analysis of the second main case (both terms universal) by examining the third and fourth subcases. In the third subcase, the subject is indefinite (muhmala) and the predicate is quantified (mahsur). For example, 'Every man is the animal' means every individual man is identical to the species 'animal', which is true in the material of affirmation but false in impossibility. In possibility, it can be true if the predicate is taken potentially, e.g., 'Every man is the writer' where 'writer' may mean potential writer. The author clarifies that this holds whether the predicate is actual or potential. In negation, the pattern is false in affirmation, true in impossibility, indeterminate in possibility. The fourth subcase is when both subject and predicate are quantified, but the subject is indefinite and the predicate is quantified with 'every' or 'some'. For universal affirmation, 'Man is every animal' is false in all materials because the nature of man cannot be every other universal. The author warns against mistakenly thinking 'Man is every writer' is true, explaining that 'man' signifies the nature, and 'every writer' signifies each individual writer, so they are not identical. The negation and particular forms follow the same structure. This systematic breakdown illustrates the precision required in logical analysis.

Key Terms
بالقوة
بالفعل

Key Takeaway
The truth of quantified propositions depends on the material relation between subject and predicate; the fourth subcase with both terms quantified often yields falsehood due to mismatch between nature and individuals.
